Divine Attributes
beginner
5 min
The Five Negative Attributes of Allah
Lists the intrinsic and five negative attributes plus action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five negative attributes and how they describe Allah's perfection by negation.

Core Idea
Allah's negative attributes deny imperfections and affirm His absolute perfection.

This lesson introduces the intrinsic attribute (sifat nafsiyya) and the five negative attributes (sifat salbiyya). These attributes are not mere negations; they affirm Allah's perfection by denying any deficiency. Understanding them is essential for a proper conception of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, identifying the intrinsic attribute and the five negative attributes. Note the attribute of action as well.
Notice the poetic structure.
2
Understand each attribute
For each negative attribute (qidam, baqa, qiyam bi-nafs, mukhalafa, wahdaniyya), reflect on what imperfection it denies and what perfection it affirms.
For example, qidam denies temporal beginning, affirming eternal existence.
3
Connect to action
The attribute of action (fi'l) emphasizes that all effects belong solely to Allah. Consider why this is included with the negative attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَذِي تُسَمَّى صِفَةً نَفْسِيَّةً ثُمَّ تَلِيْهَا خَمْسَةٌ سَلْبِيَّةٌ وَهْيَ القِدَمُ بِالذَّاتِ فَاعْلَمْ وَالبَقَا وَقِيَامُهُ بِنَفْسِهِ نِلْتَ التَّقَى تَخَالُفٌ لِلْغَيْرِ وَحْدَانِيَّةٌ فِي الذَّاتِ أَوْ صِفَاتِهِ العَلِيَّةِ وَالفِعْلِ فَالتَّأْثِيْرُ لَيْسَ إِلَّا لِلْوَاحِدِ القَهَّارِ جَلَّ وَعَلَا وَمَنْ يَقُلْ بِالطَّبْعِ أَوْ بِالعِلَّةِ فَذَاكَ كُفْرٌ عِنْدَ أَهْلِ المِلَّةِ وَمَنْ يَقُلْ بِالقُوَّةِ المُوْدَعَةِ فَذَاكَ بِدْعِيٌّ فَلَا تَلْتَفِتِ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ مُتَّصِفَاً بِهَا لَزِمَ حُدُوثُهُ وَهُوَ مُحَالٌ فَاسْتَقِمْ لِأَنَّهُ يُفْضِي إِلَى التَّسَلْسُلِ وَالدَّوْرِ وَهُوَ المُسْتَحِيْلُ المُنْجَلِي فَهُوَ الجَلِيلُ وَالجَمِيْلُ وَالوَلِيُّ وَالطَّاهِرُ القُدُّوسُ وَالرَّبُّ العَلِيُّ مُنَزَّهٌ عَنِ الحُلُوْلِ وَالجِهَةِ وَالِاتِّصَالِ وَالانْفِصَالِ وَالسَّفَهِ
And this is called the intrinsic attribute. Then five negative attributes follow: pre-eternity of essence, so know, and everlastingness, and self-subsistence — you attain piety — and being different from others, and oneness in essence or in His high attributes, and (the attribute of) action. So effect is only for the One, the Subduer, majestic and high. And whoever says by nature or by cause — that is disbelief according to the people of the religion. And whoever says by innate power — that is an innovator, so pay no attention. If He were not described with these, His temporality would be necessary, and that is impossible, so be upright, because it leads to infinite regress and circularity, and that is the manifest impossibility. For He is the Majestic, the Beautiful, the Patron, the Pure, the Holy, the High Lord, transcendent from indwelling, direction, connection, separation, and foolishness.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a dense discussion of divine attributes. It first mentions the intrinsic attribute (sifat nafsiyya, referring to existence). Then it lists five negative attributes (sifat salbiyya): qidam (pre-eternity), baqa (everlastingness), qiyam bi al-nafs (self-subsistence), mukhalafa lil-hawadith (being different from created things), wahdaniyya (oneness). Then it adds the attribute of action (fi'l), emphasizing that effective causation belongs only to God. It then declares as kufr the belief in natural causation (tab') or secondary causation ('illa), and as innovation (bid'a) the belief in innate power (quwwa muda'a). It provides a proof: if God were not described with these attributes, it would imply His temporality, which is impossible because it would lead to tasalsul (infinite regress) or dawr (circular reasoning). Finally, it describes God as transcendent (munazzah) above hulul (indwelling), direction (jiha), connection (ittisal), separation (infisal), and safah (foolishness).

Key Terms
صِفَةٌ نَفْسِيَّة
صِفَات سَلْبِيَّة
قِدَم
بَقَا
قِيَام بِنَفْسِهِ
مُخَالَفَة لِلْغَيْرِ
وَحْدَانِيَّة
فِعْل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negative attributes are pure negations with no positive content.
Correction: Negative attributes actually affirm perfections by denying their opposites. For example, qidam affirms eternal existence by denying non-existence.

Checkpoint
How many negative attributes are listed in this passage?
A) 3
B) 4
C) 5
D) 6

Key Takeaway
The five negative attributes and the attribute of action establish fundamental truths about Allah's perfection and sole causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah is self-subsisting (qiyam bi-nafsihi), then you depend on Him for every single breath. In what area of your life are you still acting as if you are self-sufficient? What will you do today to consciously rely on Allah in that area?
?
Allah's attribute of pre-eternity (qidam) means He has no beginning, while your existence has a definite start. How does this reality change the way you view your life's purpose? Are you living for temporary gains or for an eternal purpose? Write one concrete change you will make starting this week.
?
The attribute of action (fi'l) teaches that all effect is from Allah alone. Think of a recent success or failure you attributed mostly to your own effort or to someone else. How can you train your heart to see Allah's hand in every outcome? Name one specific step you will take to cultivate this mindfulness.

Refuting False Causality and Affirming Transcendence
Refutes false causality, provides proof, and declares transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why believing in natural or causal powers besides Allah is kufr, and how the impossibility of infinite regress proves Allah's necessity.

Core Idea
Only Allah is the true agent; any other view leads to disbelief or innovation.

This lesson addresses heretical views on causation and presents a rational proof for God's necessity. It also declares Allah's transcendence above physical limitations, emphasizing His absolute uniqueness.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the condemnation of those who attribute causality to nature (tab'), secondary causes ('illa), or inherent power (quwwa muda'a). Note the strong language: kufr and bid'a.
These were common philosophical views at the time.
2
Understand the proof
Analyze the proof: if Allah lacked these attributes, He would be temporal, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul) or circularity (dawr), which are impossible.
This is a foundational argument in kalam.
3
Reflect on transcendence
Consider the list of Allah's names and the negations: hulul (indwelling), jiha (direction), etc. What do these negations tell us about Allah?
Transcendence protects tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَذِي تُسَمَّى صِفَةً نَفْسِيَّةً ثُمَّ تَلِيْهَا خَمْسَةٌ سَلْبِيَّةٌ وَهْيَ القِدَمُ بِالذَّاتِ فَاعْلَمْ وَالبَقَا وَقِيَامُهُ بِنَفْسِهِ نِلْتَ التَّقَى تَخَالُفٌ لِلْغَيْرِ وَحْدَانِيَّةٌ فِي الذَّاتِ أَوْ صِفَاتِهِ العَلِيَّةِ وَالفِعْلِ فَالتَّأْثِيْرُ لَيْسَ إِلَّا لِلْوَاحِدِ القَهَّارِ جَلَّ وَعَلَا وَمَنْ يَقُلْ بِالطَّبْعِ أَوْ بِالعِلَّةِ فَذَاكَ كُفْرٌ عِنْدَ أَهْلِ المِلَّةِ وَمَنْ يَقُلْ بِالقُوَّةِ المُوْدَعَةِ فَذَاكَ بِدْعِيٌّ فَلَا تَلْتَفِتِ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ مُتَّصِفَاً بِهَا لَزِمَ حُدُوثُهُ وَهُوَ مُحَالٌ فَاسْتَقِمْ لِأَنَّهُ يُفْضِي إِلَى التَّسَلْسُلِ وَالدَّوْرِ وَهُوَ المُسْتَحِيْلُ المُنْجَلِي فَهُوَ الجَلِيلُ وَالجَمِيْلُ وَالوَلِيُّ وَالطَّاهِرُ القُدُّوسُ وَالرَّبُّ العَلِيُّ مُنَزَّهٌ عَنِ الحُلُوْلِ وَالجِهَةِ وَالِاتِّصَالِ وَالانْفِصَالِ وَالسَّفَهِ
And this is called the intrinsic attribute. Then five negative attributes follow: pre-eternity of essence, so know, and everlastingness, and self-subsistence — you attain piety — and being different from others, and oneness in essence or in His high attributes, and (the attribute of) action. So effect is only for the One, the Subduer, majestic and high. And whoever says by nature or by cause — that is disbelief according to the people of the religion. And whoever says by innate power — that is an innovator, so pay no attention. If He were not described with these, His temporality would be necessary, and that is impossible, so be upright, because it leads to infinite regress and circularity, and that is the manifest impossibility. For He is the Majestic, the Beautiful, the Patron, the Pure, the Holy, the High Lord, transcendent from indwelling, direction, connection, separation, and foolishness.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a dense discussion of divine attributes. It first mentions the intrinsic attribute (sifat nafsiyya, referring to existence). Then it lists five negative attributes (sifat salbiyya): qidam (pre-eternity), baqa (everlastingness), qiyam bi al-nafs (self-subsistence), mukhalafa lil-hawadith (being different from created things), wahdaniyya (oneness). Then it adds the attribute of action (fi'l), emphasizing that effective causation belongs only to God. It then declares as kufr the belief in natural causation (tab') or secondary causation ('illa), and as innovation (bid'a) the belief in innate power (quwwa muda'a). It provides a proof: if God were not described with these attributes, it would imply His temporality, which is impossible because it would lead to tasalsul (infinite regress) or dawr (circular reasoning). Finally, it describes God as transcendent (munazzah) above hulul (indwelling), direction (jiha), connection (ittisal), separation (infisal), and safah (foolishness).

Key Terms

عِلَّة
قُوَّة مُوْدَعَة
تَسَلْسُل
دَوْر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is acceptable to say that fire burns by its nature.
Correction: The text says whoever says by nature (tab') is a disbeliever; all effect is from Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on someone who claims effects happen by nature (tab')?
A) They are an innovator
B) They are a disbeliever
C) They are mistaken but not kafir
D) They are correct

Key Takeaway
Only Allah truly acts; believing otherwise is kufr, and His transcendence means He is beyond all physical and human imperfections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you succeed in something, do you secretly attribute it to your own skill or effort more than to Allah? What would it take for you to truly internalize that He alone is the agent in every outcome?
?
If you were to live each moment consciously aware that no natural cause or human power has independent effect, how would your fear, hope, and reliance on Allah change? Would you be willing to let go of your false sense of control?
?
The text says Allah is 'transcendent from direction, connection, separation, and foolishness.' Are you comfortable with a God who cannot be located or touched, or does your heart crave a more tangible deity? How does your prayer life reflect your answer?

The Seven Attributes of Meaning and Their Eternal Connection
Enumerates the seven attributes of meaning and states their eternal connection except life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven attributes of meaning (sifāt maʿānī) that are necessary for Allah, the distinction between His will and command, and the principle that these attributes are eternally connected to His essence—noting a textual ambiguity regarding the attribute of life.

Core Idea
Allah's seven essential attributes are eternal, inseparable from Him, and define His relationship to creation.

This passage from al-Sanūsī’s Umm al-Barāhīn lays out the seven attributes of meaning that every Muslim must affirm for Allah. It also clarifies that God’s will is distinct from His command—what He wills may differ from what He commands. Finally, it states that these attributes are necessarily and eternally connected to Him, but includes a puzzling exception for life that requires careful study.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify each attribute
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. As you read, list the seven attributes of meaning mentioned. Note how each is introduced.
The attributes are: knowledge, life, power, will, speech, hearing, sight.
2
Understand the distinction between will and command
The text says that what God wills may be opposite to what He commands. Reflect on why this distinction matters for understanding divine decree and moral obligation.
Will (irāda) is about what God decrees; command (amr) is about what He prescribes.
3
Examine the final statement on eternal connection
The passage concludes that these attributes are necessarily (wājib) and eternally (dawāman) connected to Allah, with the phrase 'except life'. Consider the standard Ashʿarī view that all seven are eternal, and why this exception is problematic.
Most scholars hold that life, like the other six, is eternal. The text may contain a scribal error or reflect a minority interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ المَعَانِي سَبْعَةٌ لِلرَّائي أَيْ عِلْمُهُ المُحِيْطُ بِالأَشْيَاءِ وَحَيَاتُهُ وَقُدْرَةٌ وَإِرَادَةٌ وَكُلُّ شَيْءٍ كَائِنٌ أَرَادَهُ وَإِنْ يَكُنْ بِضِدِّهِ قَدْ أَمَرَا فَالْقَصْدُ غَيْرُ الأَمْرِ فَاطْرَحِ المِرَا فَقَدْ عَلِمْتَ أَرْبَعاً أَقْسَاماً فِي الكَائِنَاتِ فَاحْفَظِ المَقَامَا كَلَامُهُ وَالسَّمْعُ وَالإِبْصَارُ فَهُوَ الإِلَهُ الفَاعِلُ المُخْتَارُ وَوَاجِبٌ تَعْلِيْقُ ذِي الصِّفَاتِ حَتْماً دَوَاماً مَا عَدَا الحَيَاةِ
Then the attributes of meaning are seven in the view (of the sage): meaning His knowledge that encompasses things, and His life, and power, and will, and everything that occurs He wills; and if He commands something with its opposite, then intent is different from command, so throw away dispute. For you have known four divisions concerning created things? so preserve the station. His speech, and hearing, and sight; so He is God, the active, the chooser. And it is necessary that these attributes be connected? necessarily eternally except life.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage enumerates the seven attributes of meaning (sifat ma'ani): knowledge, life, power, will, speech, hearing, and sight. It also draws a distinction between God's will and His command, stating that what He wills may be different from what He commands. It mentions 'four divisions' concerning created things (possibly referring to wajib, mustahil, ja'iz, and something else). Then it concludes that it is necessary that these attributes be permanently connected? except life? The final phrase is ambiguous; in standard Ash'ari doctrine, all seven are eternal and inseparable from God. The commentary should note this potential textual issue or specialized interpretation.

Key Terms

صِفَات مَعَانٍ
حَيَاة
عِلْم
قُدْرَة
إِرَادَة
سَمْع
بَصَر
كَلَام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The attribute of life is not eternal because the text says 'except life'.
Correction: Standard Ashʿarī theology holds that all seven attributes of meaning, including life, are eternal and inseparable from Allah. The phrase 'except life' in this passage is likely a textual error or a very specific minority interpretation; it should not be taken as normative.

Checkpoint
How many attributes of meaning (ṣifāt maʿānī) are listed in this passage?
A) Five
B) Seven
C) Four
D) Nine

Key Takeaway
Allah possesses seven eternal attributes of meaning that are distinct from His essence yet inseparably connected to it, though the textual exception for life warrants careful examination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage distinguishes between what Allah wills and what He commands. Is there a command of Allah that you have been ignoring because it conflicts with your own will? How will you realign your will with His command starting today?
?
You have learned that all seven attributes of meaning are eternally connected to Allah, with a puzzling exception for life. Does this exception shake your certainty in Allah's eternal life? If not, how do you reconcile it with your belief in His perfect and eternal attributes?
?
The author says, 'throw away dispute' regarding the distinction between will and command. Is there a theological dispute in your heart about Allah's attributes that you need to cast aside and submit to the revelation? What step will you take to silence that dispute?

Divine Attributes

The Attribute of Existence: The Essential Attribute
The poem states that existence is a necessary attribute and names it the essential attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that existence (wujud) is a necessary attribute of God and that it is called the essential attribute (sifah nafsiyyah).

Core Idea
Existence is not just a fact; it is a necessary attribute of God.

This lesson introduces the first attribute discussed in the poem: existence. The poet argues that existence is a wajib (necessary) attribute of God, proven by the effect-to-cause argument. He then names it the 'essential attribute' (sifah nafsiyyah) because it is intrinsic to His essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic lines and their translation. Notice how the poet uses the effect-to-cause argument.
2
Understand the proof
The poet says every effect points to an effective cause. God is the necessary cause of all effects, so He must exist.
3
Identify the essential attribute
Existence is called sifah nafsiyyah because it is not a separate addition but inherent to God's essence.
Essential attributes are those that are not negations or actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَاعْلَمْ بِأَنَّ الْوَصْفَ بِالْوُجُوْدِ مِنْ وَاجِبَاتِ الْوَاحِدِ الْمَعْبُودِ إِذْ ظَاهِرٌ بِأَنَّ كُلَّ أَثَرٍ يَهْدِي إِلَى مُؤَثِّرٍ فَاعْتَبِرِ وَذِي تُسَمَّى صِفَةٌ نَفْسِيَّهُ ثُمَّ تَلِيْهَا خَمْسَةٌ سَلْبِيَّهُ وَهْيَ الْقِدَمُ بِالذَّاتِ فَاعْلَمْ وَالْبَقَا وَقِيَامُهُ بِنَفْسِهِ نِلْتَ التَّقَى مُخَالَفَةٌ لِلْغَيْرِ وَحْدَانِيَّةٌ فِي الذَّاتِ أَوْ صِفَاتِهِ الْعَلِيَّةِ وَالْفِعْلِ فَالتَّأْثِيْرُ لَيْسَ إِلَّا لِلْوَاحِدِ الْقَهَّارِ جَلَّ وَعَلَا وَمَنْ يَقُلْ بِالطَّبْعِ أَوْ بِالْعِلَّهْ فَذَاكَ كُفْرٌ عِنْدَ أَهْلِ الْمِلَّهْ وَمَنْ يَقُلْ بِالْقُوَّةِ الْمُوْدَعَةِ فَذَاكَ بِدْعِيٌّ فَلَا تَلْتَفِتِ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ مُتَّصِفَاً بِهَا لَزِمَ حُدُوتُهُ وَهُوَ مُحَالٌ فَاسْتَقِمْ لِأَنَّهُ يُفْضِي إلى التَّسَلْسُلِ وَالدَّوْرِ وَهْوَ الْمُسْتَحِيْلُ الْمُنْجَلِي فَهُوَ الْجَلِيلُ وَالْجَمِيلُ وَالْوَلِي وَالطَّاهِرُ الْقُدُّوسُ وَالرَّبُّ الْعَلِي مُنَزَّهُ عَنِ الْحُلُوْلِ وَالْجِهَةِ وَالْاِتِّصَالِ الْانْفِصَالِ وَالسَّفَهِ
Know that the description of existence (wujud) is among the necessary (wajibat) of the One who is worshipped, since it is apparent that every effect points to an effective cause, so take heed. And this is called the essential attribute (sifah nafsiyyah). Then after it follow five negative attributes (sifah salbiyyah): eternal (qidam) in essence, know that; and everlastingness (baqa); and self-subsistence (qiyam bi nafsih)—you have attained piety; dissimilarity to others (mukhalafah lil-ghayr); and oneness (wahdaniyyah) in essence or in His high attributes and in action. So effective causation is only for the One, the Subduer, exalted and high. And whoever says by nature or by a cause (illa) is a disbeliever according to the people of the faith. And whoever says by an inherent power (quwwah mawda'ah) is an innovator (bid'i), do not pay attention to him. If He were not described with these, His createdness would be necessary, which is impossible, so be steadfast, because it would lead to infinite regress (tasalsul) and circularity (dawr), which is the clear impossibility. So He is the Majestic, the Beautiful, the Patron, the Pure, the Holy, the High Lord, transcendent (munazzah) over indwelling (hulul), direction (jihah), connection (ittisal), disconnection (infisal), and foolishness (safah).
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a classification and a proof. It first states that the attribute of existence is wajib (necessary) for God, using the effect-to-cause argument: every effect indicates an efficient cause. This attribute is called 'sifah nafsiyyah' (essential attribute). Then it lists five 'sifah salbiyyah' (negative attributes): qidam (eternal), baqa (everlasting), qiyam bi nafsihi (self-subsistence), mukhalafah lil-ghayr (dissimilarity to created things), and wahdaniyyah (oneness in essence, attributes, and action). The poem then asserts that only God is the true agent (effect is from Him alone). It refutes naturalism (tab') and necessary causation ('illah) as kufr, and refutes the idea of an inherent power (quwwah mawda'ah) as bid'ah. It gives a proof for the necessity of these attributes: without them, God would be created, which leads to an infinite regress or circularity, both impossible. Finally, it declares God's transcendence over hulul (incarnation), jihah (direction), ittisal (connection), infisal (disconnection), and safah (foolishness).

Key Terms
وُجُوْد
صِفَة نَفْسِيَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the poem, what is the proof that existence is necessary for God?
A) The attributes are mentioned in revelation.
B) Every effect points to an effective cause.
C) God's names require existence.
D) The poet states it without proof.

Key Takeaway
Existence is a necessary attribute of God, proven by the effect-to-cause argument, and is called the essential attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The poet calls us to 'take heed' (fa'tabir). What is one way you have been living as if the universe exists without a necessary cause? How will you change that starting today?
?
Existence is not just a concept but an attribute of God. How does recognizing that God's existence is necessary change the way you pray or rely on Him? What is one area of your life where you act as if God's existence is optional?

The Five Negative Attributes and Their Proof
The poem lists five negative attributes, refutes false doctrines, and proves their necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five negative attributes (sifah salbiyyah) and the proof that without them God would be subject to impossibility.

Core Idea
Negative attributes safeguard God's perfection by negating what is not befitting.

After establishing existence, the poem lists five attributes that are purely negative: qidam (eternal), baqa (everlasting), qiyam bi nafsih (self-subsistence), mukhalafah lil-ghayr (dissimilarity to creation), and wahdaniyyah (oneness). The poet then refutes false causal theories and provides a proof that without these attributes, God would be contingent, leading to infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of five attributes
Read the Arabic lines that enumerate the five negative attributes. Notice each one is a negation of a limitation.
Qidam negates non-existence, baqa negates annihilation, etc.
2
Understand the refutations
The poet rejects the views of nature (tab') and necessary causation ('illah) as disbelief, and inherent power (quwwah mawda'ah) as innovation.
These views undermine God's sole agency.
3
Grasp the proof
The poet argues that if God lacked these attributes, He would be created (hadith), leading to infinite regress or circularity, both impossible.
This proof is called the argument from impossibility of tasalsul and dawr.
4
Reflect on transcendence
The poem ends by declaring God's transcendence over hulul, jihah, etc., reinforcing His uniqueness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَاعْلَمْ بِأَنَّ الْوَصْفَ بِالْوُجُوْدِ مِنْ وَاجِبَاتِ الْوَاحِدِ الْمَعْبُودِ إِذْ ظَاهِرٌ بِأَنَّ كُلَّ أَثَرٍ يَهْدِي إِلَى مُؤَثِّرٍ فَاعْتَبِرِ وَذِي تُسَمَّى صِفَةٌ نَفْسِيَّهُ ثُمَّ تَلِيْهَا خَمْسَةٌ سَلْبِيَّهُ وَهْيَ الْقِدَمُ بِالذَّاتِ فَاعْلَمْ وَالْبَقَا وَقِيَامُهُ بِنَفْسِهِ نِلْتَ التَّقَى مُخَالَفَةٌ لِلْغَيْرِ وَحْدَانِيَّةٌ فِي الذَّاتِ أَوْ صِفَاتِهِ الْعَلِيَّةِ وَالْفِعْلِ فَالتَّأْثِيْرُ لَيْسَ إِلَّا لِلْوَاحِدِ الْقَهَّارِ جَلَّ وَعَلَا وَمَنْ يَقُلْ بِالطَّبْعِ أَوْ بِالْعِلَّهْ فَذَاكَ كُفْرٌ عِنْدَ أَهْلِ الْمِلَّهْ وَمَنْ يَقُلْ بِالْقُوَّةِ الْمُوْدَعَةِ فَذَاكَ بِدْعِيٌّ فَلَا تَلْتَفِتِ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ مُتَّصِفَاً بِهَا لَزِمَ حُدُوتُهُ وَهُوَ مُحَالٌ فَاسْتَقِمْ لِأَنَّهُ يُفْضِي إلى التَّسَلْسُلِ وَالدَّوْرِ وَهْوَ الْمُسْتَحِيْلُ الْمُنْجَلِي فَهُوَ الْجَلِيلُ وَالْجَمِيلُ وَالْوَلِي وَالطَّاهِرُ الْقُدُّوسُ وَالرَّبُّ الْعَلِي مُنَزَّهُ عَنِ الْحُلُوْلِ وَالْجِهَةِ وَالْاِتِّصَالِ الْانْفِصَالِ وَالسَّفَهِ
Know that the description of existence (wujud) is among the necessary (wajibat) of the One who is worshipped, since it is apparent that every effect points to an effective cause, so take heed. And this is called the essential attribute (sifah nafsiyyah). Then after it follow five negative attributes (sifah salbiyyah): eternal (qidam) in essence, know that; and everlastingness (baqa); and self-subsistence (qiyam bi nafsih)—you have attained piety; dissimilarity to others (mukhalafah lil-ghayr); and oneness (wahdaniyyah) in essence or in His high attributes and in action. So effective causation is only for the One, the Subduer, exalted and high. And whoever says by nature or by a cause (illa) is a disbeliever according to the people of the faith. And whoever says by an inherent power (quwwah mawda'ah) is an innovator (bid'i), do not pay attention to him. If He were not described with these, His createdness would be necessary, which is impossible, so be steadfast, because it would lead to infinite regress (tasalsul) and circularity (dawr), which is the clear impossibility. So He is the Majestic, the Beautiful, the Patron, the Pure, the Holy, the High Lord, transcendent (munazzah) over indwelling (hulul), direction (jihah), connection (ittisal), disconnection (infisal), and foolishness (safah).
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a classification and a proof. It first states that the attribute of existence is wajib (necessary) for God, using the effect-to-cause argument: every effect indicates an efficient cause. This attribute is called 'sifah nafsiyyah' (essential attribute). Then it lists five 'sifah salbiyyah' (negative attributes): qidam (eternal), baqa (everlasting), qiyam bi nafsihi (self-subsistence), mukhalafah lil-ghayr (dissimilarity to created things), and wahdaniyyah (oneness in essence, attributes, and action). The poem then asserts that only God is the true agent (effect is from Him alone). It refutes naturalism (tab') and necessary causation ('illah) as kufr, and refutes the idea of an inherent power (quwwah mawda'ah) as bid'ah. It gives a proof for the necessity of these attributes: without them, God would be created, which leads to an infinite regress or circularity, both impossible. Finally, it declares God's transcendence over hulul (incarnation), jihah (direction), ittisal (connection), infisal (disconnection), and safah (foolishness).

مُخَالَفَة لِلْحَوَادِث

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's attributes are separate entities added to His essence.
Correction: The negative attributes are not positive qualities but negations of imperfections; they are not separate.

Checkpoint
According to the poem, what would happen if God were not described with these attributes?
A) He would be limited.
B) He would be created, leading to infinite regress.

D) The attributes would be unnecessary.

Key Takeaway
The five negative attributes are necessary to negate all imperfection from God, and their absence would imply contingency and logical impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you observe events in your life, do you sometimes act as if they are caused by nature, luck, or your own effort independent of God? How does this passage's refutation of naturalism and necessary causation challenge you to see only Allah as the true agent?
?
The proof from infinite regress shows that denying God's attributes leads to logical absurdity. Have you ever doubted God's existence or attributes because of intellectual confusion? Reflect on how this rational argument can fortify your certainty and remove such doubts.

The Seven Attributes of Meaning: Enumeration and Attachments
The poem enumerates the seven attributes of meaning and their attachments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven attributes of meaning (sifaat ma'ani), the distinction between will and command, and the specific attachments of each attribute to categories of existents.

Core Idea
Each of the seven divine attributes attaches to a specific domain of existence.

This lesson introduces the seven attributes of meaning from the poem: knowledge, life, power, will, speech, hearing, and sight. It clarifies that will is distinct from command, and each attribute attaches to particular categories of existents. Understanding these attachments is key to proper theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic poem and its literal translation to get an overview of the content.
Notice the poetic structure and the list of seven attributes.
2
Identify the seven attributes and the will-command distinction
List the seven attributes and note the line clarifying that will (irada) is not the same as command (amr).
This distinction refutes those who conflate the two.
3
Examine the attachments of each attribute
Follow the poem's description: knowledge and speech attach to all divisions; power and will attach only to possible things; hearing and sight attach to every existent.
This shows the scope of each attribute.
4
Understand the eternity of the attributes
The poem concludes that all attributes are eternal in essence because they are not other than the essence.
This affirms that attributes are not separate entities added to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ الْمَعَانِي سَبْعَةٌ لِلرَّائِي أَيْ عِلْمُهُ الْمُحِيْطُ بِالْأَشْيَاءِ يَاتُهُ وَقُدْرَةٌ إِرَادَةُ وَكُلُّ شَيْءٍ كَائِنٍ أَرَادَهُ وَإِنْ يَكُنْ بِضِدِّهِ قَدْ أَمَرَا فَالْقَصْدُ غَيْرُ الْأَمْرِ فَاطْرَحِ الْمِرَا فَقَدْ عَلِمْتَ أَرْبَعاً أَقْسَامًا فِي الْكَائِنَاتِ فَاحْفَظِ الْمَقَامَا كَلَامُهُ وَالسَّمْعُ وَالْإِبْصَارُ فَهُوَ الْإِلَهُ الْفَاعِلُ الْمُخْتَارُ وَوَاجِبٌ تَعْلِيْقُ ذِي الصِّفَاتِ حَتْماً دَوَامًا مَا عَدَا الْحَيَاةِ فَالْعِلْمُ جَزْماً وَالْكَلَامُ السَّامِي تَعَلَّقَ بِسَائِرِ الْأَقْسَامِ وَقُدْرَةٌ إِرَادَةٌ تَعَلَّقَ بِالْمُمْكِنَاتِ كُلِّهَا أَخَا التَّقَى وَاجْزِمْ بِأَنَّ سَمْعَهُ وَالْبَصَرَا تَعَلَّقَا بِكُلِّ مَوْجُودٍ يُرَى وَكُلُّهَا قَدِيْمَةٌ بِالذَّاتِ لِأَنَّهَا لَيْسَتْ بِغَيْرِ الذَّاتِ
Then the attributes of meaning (ma'ani) are seven, for the beholder: His knowledge (ilm) encompassing all things, His life (hayat), power (qudrah), will (iradah)—and everything that exists He willed it—and if He commands its opposite, the intention is other than the command, so abandon dispute. You have known four divisions among existents, so preserve the station. His speech (kalam), hearing (sam'), and sight (basar). So He is God, the agent, the chooser. And necessary is the attachment of these attributes perpetually always, except for life (hayat). For knowledge definitely and the sublime speech attach to all divisions. Power and will attach to all possible (mumkinat) things, O brother of piety. And be certain that His hearing and sight attach to every existent that is seen. And all of them are eternal (qadimah) in essence, because they are not other than the essence.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and classification. It lists the seven 'sifaat ma'ani' (attributes of meaning): 'ilm (knowledge), hayat (life), qudrah (power), iradah (will), kalam (speech), sam' (hearing), and basar (sight). It notes that God's will encompasses all that exists, and if He commands something contrary to what He wills, the intention is distinct from the command—a rebuttal to those who conflate will and command. The poem then mentions four categories of existents (possibly necessary, possible, etc.) but does not detail them here. It explains the attachment of each attribute: knowledge attaches to all things (including necessary and impossible as objects of knowledge), speech attaches to all divisions (perhaps all known things), power and will attach only to possible things (mumkinat), hearing and sight attach to every existent that is perceived (or is subject to hearing/sight). Finally, it asserts that all these attributes are eternal (qadimah) in essence because they are not other than the essence (i.e., they are not accidental additions separate from God).

إِرَادَة
كَلَام

قَدِيمَة بِالذَّات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that power and will attach to all things, including necessary and impossible existents.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that power and will attach only to possible things (mumkinat), not to necessary or impossible ones.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The seven attributes of meaning are eternal and have specific attachments: knowledge and speech to all categories, power and will to possible things, and hearing and sight to every existent—and they are identical with God's essence.

?

Divine Speech and the Impossibility of Opposites
The poem defines divine speech negatively and proves impossibility of opposite attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's speech is an eternal attribute not composed of letters or sounds, and why opposite attributes are impossible for Him.

Core Idea
God's speech is eternal, not made of letters; opposites imply need and imperfection.

This lesson examines two central claims from the poem: first, that divine speech (kalam) is not like human speech composed of letters and arranged in sequence; second, that it is impossible for Allah to be described with opposite attributes. These points establish Allah's transcendence and absolute perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to get the plain meaning.
2
Understand the definition of divine speech
Notice that the poem first negates letters and arrangement for Allah's speech. This means His speech is an eternal attribute, not a created sound or script.
Contrast with human speech which depends on letters and order.
3
Grasp the proof for impossibility of opposites
The poem argues: if Allah lacked perfect attributes, He would have opposite imperfect ones, leading to neediness (faqr) – which is impossible for God.
This shows why Allah must have all perfect attributes and cannot have any defect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ الْكَلَامُ لَيْسَ بِالْحُرُوفِ وَلَيْسَ بِالتَّرْتِيبِ كَالْمَأْلُوْفِ وَيَسْتَحِيْلُ ضِدُّ مَا تَقَدَّمَا مِنَ الصِّفَاتِ الشَّامِخَاتِ فَاعْلَمَا لِأَنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ مَوْصُوفَا بِهَا لَكَانَ بِالسِّوَى مَعْرُوفًا وَكُلُّ مَنْ قَامَ بِهِ سِوَاهَا فَهْوَ الَّذِي فِي الْفَقْرِ قَدْ تَنَاهَى
Then speech (kalam) is not with letters (huruf), nor with arrangement (tartib) like the familiar. And the opposite of what has preceded from the lofty attributes is impossible, so know. Because if He were not described with them, He would be known by other (opposite) attributes. And whoever is characterized by anything other than them, then he is the one who has reached the extreme of poverty (faqr).
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage contains two claims. First, it defines divine speech (kalam) negatively: it is not composed of letters (huruf) nor arranged in sequence (tartib) like human speech. This is to assert that God's speech is an eternal attribute, not a created physical sound. Second, it proves the impossibility of God being described with opposite attributes (e.g., ignorance instead of knowledge, weakness instead of power). The proof: if God were not described with these perfect attributes, He would be described with their opposites, which are imperfections. And whoever is characterized by such imperfections is in extreme need (faqr) and cannot be Divine. Therefore, God must have these positive attributes, and their opposites are impossible for Him.

Key Terms

حُرُوف
تَرْتِيب
ضِد
فَقْر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that God's speech is created or composed of sounds and letters like human speech.
Correction: The poem refutes this by stating clearly that divine speech is not with letters nor arrangement – it is an eternal attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the poem, why is it impossible for God to have opposite attributes (e.g., ignorance)?
A) Because revelation says so.
B) Because then He would be known by opposite attributes, leading to neediness (faqr).
C) Because opposite attributes cannot coexist.
D) Because God's attributes are all positive.

Key Takeaway
The poem affirms that Allah's speech is an eternal, non-physical attribute, and that opposite attributes are impossible because they would imply need.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you truly believe you are hearing the eternal, non-created speech of Allah, or do you reduce it to a mere book? How does that belief change your reverence and attentiveness?
?
Consider any attribute you might subconsciously associate with Allah that implies imperfection, such as anger without wisdom or power without mercy. Why is it impossible for Allah to have any opposite of His perfect attributes? Examine your heart—do you ever doubt His absolute perfection?
?
The poem says that anyone characterized by opposite attributes has reached the extreme of need. Do you live as though you are in need of Allah's perfect attributes to cover your own defects? Or do you pretend to be self-sufficient like the One who cannot have opposites?

Possible Divine Actions and the Breach of Etiquette
Sanusi enumerates possible divine actions and refutes the obligation of al-salah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God is free to do as He wills and is not obligated to create what is most beneficial for His creatures.

Core Idea
God's actions are unrestricted; demanding obligation is a breach of etiquette.

The poem begins by addressing the nature of divine actions. It affirms that God may bring into existence or not, cause happiness or misery, without being bound by any external obligation. The Ash'ari tradition rejects the Mu'tazilite claim that God must do what is best (al-salah).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the first two lines of the poem carefully. Note the list of possible actions and the statement about al-salah.
2
Identify the four possible actions
Find the four verbs: bring into existence, leave, cause misery, cause happiness.
They are all examples of ja'iz.
3
Understand the refutation of al-salah
The poem rejects the view that God must do what is most beneficial. Notice the phrase 'committed a breach of etiquette.'
This is the Ash'ari position against the Mu'tazila.
4
Reflect on the concept of ja'iz
Ja'iz means something is neither necessary nor impossible. Why does this matter for divine actions?
It preserves God's freedom and power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَجَائِزٌ فِي حَقِّهِ الْإِيْجَادُ وَالتَّرْكُ وَالْإِشْقَاءُ وَالْإِسْعَادُ وَمَنْ يَقُلْ فِعْلَ الصَّلَاحِ وَجَبَا عَلَى الْإِلَهِ قَدْ أَسَاءَ الْأَدَبَا وَاجْزِمْ أَخِي بِرُؤْيَةِ الْإِلَهِ فِي جَنَّةِ الْخُلْدِ بِغَيْرِ تَنَاهِي إِذِ الْوُقُوعُ جَائِزٌ بِالْعَقْلِ وَقَدْ أَتَى فِيهِ دَلِيلُ النَّقْلِ وَصِفْ جَمِيعَ الرُّسُلِ بِالْأَمَانَةِ وَالصِّدْقِ وَالتَّبْلِيغِ وَالْفَطَانَةِ وَيَسْتَحِيْلُ ضِدُّهَا عَلَيْهِمُ وَجَائِزٌ كَالْأَكْلِ فِي حَقِّهِمُ إِرْسَالُهُمْ تَفَضُّلٌ وَرَحْمَةٌ لِلْعَالَمِينَ جَلَّ مُوْلِي النِّعْمَةِ وَيَلْزَمُ الْإِيْمَانُ بِالْحِسَابِ وَالْحَشْرِ وَالْعِقَابِ وَالثَّوَابِ وَالنَّشْرِ وَالصِّرَاطِ وَالْمِيزَانِ وَالْحَوْضِ وَالنِّيرَانِ وَالْجِنَانِ وَالْجِنِّ وَالْأَمْلَاكِ ثُمَّ الْأَنْبِيَا وَالْحُورِ وَالْوِلْدَانِ ثُمَّ الْأَوْلِيَا وَبِكُلِّ مَا جَاءَ عَنِ الْبَشِيرِ مِنْ كُلِّ حُكْمِ صَارَ كَالضَّرُورِي وَيَنْطَوِي فِي كِلْمَةِ الْإِسْلَامِ مَا قَدْ مَضَى مِنْ سَائِرِ الْأَحْكَامِ
And it is possible (ja'iz) concerning Him to bring into existence, to leave, to cause misery, and to cause happiness. And whoever says that performing the most beneficial action (fi'l al-salah) is obligatory upon God has committed a breach of etiquette (asa'a al-adab). And be certain, my brother, of seeing Allah (ru'yat Allah) in the Paradise of Eternity without end, since its occurrence is possible (ja'iz) by reason (aql) and indeed the transmitted proof (dalil al-naql) has come concerning it. And describe all the messengers with trustworthiness (amanah), truthfulness (sidq), conveyance (tabligh), and intelligence (fatanah). And their opposites are impossible upon them. And what is possible concerning them, like eating, their sending is a favor (tafaddul) and mercy (rahmah) to the worlds. Exalted is the Master of grace. And necessary is faith in the Reckoning (hisab), the Gathering (hashr), the Punishment (iqab), the Reward (thawab), the Resurrection (nashr), the Path (sirat), the Balance (mizan), the Pool (hawd), the Fire (niran), the Gardens (jinan), the Jinn, the Angels (amlak), then the Prophets (anbiya), the Houris (hur), the Servants (wildan), then the Saints (awliya). And in everything that came from the Bringer of Glad Tidings (al-bashir) of every ruling, it has become like necessary (daruri) knowledge. And in the word of Islam (kalimat al-islam) is encompassed what has passed from the rest of the rulings.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a summary of many doctrinal points. It begins with the possible (ja'iz) actions of God: giving existence, withholding (giving nonexistence), causing misery, and causing happiness. The Ash'ari position is that God is not obligated to do what is most beneficial (al-salah) for creation; the Mu'tazilite view that He is so obligated is declared a breach of etiquette (asa'a al-adab). Next, the poem affirms the beatific vision (ru'yat Allah) in Paradise, which is possible by reason and confirmed by revelation. Then it lists the necessary attributes of prophets: amanah (trustworthiness), sidq (truthfulness), tabligh (conveying the message), and fatanah (intelligence). Their opposites are impossible. Their human actions like eating are possible. Their sending is a favor and mercy from God. Finally, the poem enumerates the eschatological and unseen articles of faith: ma'diyyat (tawba?), etc. It explicitly mentions: hisab (reckoning), hashr (gathering), iqab (punishment), thawab (reward), nashr (resurrection), sirat (bridge), mizan (balance), hawd (pool), niran (fire), jinan (paradise), jinn, angels, prophets, houris, wildan (servants), awliya (saints). It states that everything brought by the Prophet (al-bashir) is like necessary knowledge (daruri) and that all rulings are encompassed in the word of Islam (kalimat al-islam).

Key Terms
جَائِز
فِعْل الصَّلَاح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is obligated to do what brings maximum benefit to creatures.
Correction: The poem states that whoever says this has committed a breach of etiquette; God's actions are not subject to external obligation.

Checkpoint
What does the poem say about someone who claims God must do what is most beneficial?
A) They are correct.
B) They are a believer.
C) They have committed a breach of etiquette.
D) They are a disbeliever.

Key Takeaway
God is not obligated to do what is most beneficial; His actions are free and unrestricted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking that God owes you something—whether good health, wealth, or answered duas? After learning that demanding obligation from God is a breach of etiquette, will you repent from such entitlement and commit to pure gratitude?
?
When you face hardship, do you secretly question God's justice or feel He is being unfair? Now that you know causing misery is ja'iz (possible) for Him, can you truly accept His decree without complaint? What specific situation will you surrender to Him today?
?
In your dua, do you ever demand rather than humbly ask? Reflect on a recent prayer—did you assume God had to answer? How will you change your supplication to reflect the adab this lesson teaches, acknowledging that He owes you nothing?

The Beatific Vision of Allah in Paradise
Sanusi affirms the beatific vision as rationally possible and scripturally proven.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that seeing Allah in Paradise is rationally possible and affirmed by revelation.

Core Idea
The beatific vision is a real, eternal sight of Allah in the hereafter.

The poem moves from divine actions to the blessed vision of Allah in Paradise. It affirms that this vision is possible by reason and confirmed by transmitted proofs from the Quran and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Read the vision passage
Read the line about ru'yat Allah. Note the command 'be certain'.
2
Identify the two proofs
The poem gives two justifications: rational possibility and scriptural proof.
Think about why both are needed.
3
Understand the nature of the vision
The vision is in Paradise, eternal, without physical limitations.
4
Recognize the stance
The Ash'ari position is that vision is possible by reason and confirmed by revelation.
Contrast with Mu'tazilite denial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Seeing Allah is impossible because He is not a body or in a place.

Checkpoint
Why is the beatific vision accepted according to the poem?
A) It is rationally necessary.
B) It is rationally impossible but believed anyway.
C) It is rationally possible and confirmed by revelation.
D) It is only metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
The beatific vision of Allah in Paradise is a real event, possible by reason and proven by revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You will see Allah forever in Paradise. Does this reality shape how you live today, or do you live as if this world is all there is? What one concrete change can you make today to orient your heart toward that eternal vision?
?
The poet commands absolute certainty. Are you truly certain that you will see Allah? If not, what doubts linger, and what step can you take to remove them based on the two proofs (reason and revelation) mentioned?
?
You have two proofs: rational possibility and scriptural evidence. Which one is weaker in your heart right now? How can you strengthen both so that your certainty becomes unshakeable?

Necessary Attributes of the Prophets
Sanusi enumerates the necessary attributes of prophets and their opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four essential attributes of prophets and their opposites.

Core Idea
Prophets are trustworthy, truthful, convey the message, and are intelligent; their opposites are impossible.

The poem now focuses on prophethood. It instructs us to describe all messengers with four essential qualities: trustworthiness, truthfulness, conveyance of the message, and intelligence. Their opposites are impossible, while normal human acts like eating are possible for them.

Guided Path
1
Read the prophetic attributes passage
Read the lines describing the prophets. Note the four attributes and the classification.
2
List the four necessary attributes
Write down: amanah, sidq, tabligh, fatanah.
Memorize them in Arabic.
3
Understand the opposites
The opposites of these are impossible for prophets. Think why.
This guards against accusing prophets of sin.
4
Note the possible attributes
Human actions like eating are possible for prophets.
Prophets are human.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets could possibly lie or betray.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT listed as a necessary attribute of prophets?
A) Trustworthiness
B) Truthfulness
C) Conveyance
D) Wealth

Key Takeaway
Prophets have four essential attributes: trustworthiness, truthfulness, conveyance, and intelligence; their opposites are impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The prophets are described as having complete amanah (trustworthiness). Think about the trusts Allah has placed upon you—your faith, your family, your abilities. In which area have you betrayed that trust? What is one small change you will make today to begin restoring it?
?
Sidq (truthfulness) is an essential attribute of prophets. When was the last time you chose to lie or exaggerate to avoid discomfort? How can you commit to complete honesty even when it is costly? Write down one specific situation coming up this week where you will speak the truth regardless of the outcome.
?
The prophets conveyed the entire message without concealing anything. Is there any part of your faith or obligation that you are shy to practice or share with others? What step can you take this week to openly live that aspect of Islam, even if it is difficult?

Divine Attributes
beginner
3 min
The Sending of Prophets as Divine Favor
Sanusi declares the sending of prophets as divine favor and mercy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God sends prophets out of grace, not obligation.

Core Idea
The sending of prophets is a pure favor and mercy from God.

After describing the attributes of prophets, the poem explains why they are sent. It states that their sending (irsal) is a favor (tafaddul) and mercy (rahmah) from God to the worlds. This reinforces that prophethood is a gift, not a requirement.

Guided Path
1
Read the line about sending
Read the single line about irsal being tafaddul and rahmah.
2
Understand the terms
Tafaddul means favor, rahmah means mercy. Note that both are voluntary.
3
Reflect on the implication
If sending is a favor, it is not obligatory upon God.
This aligns with Ash'ari theology.
4
Appreciate the praise
The line ends with 'Exalted is the Master of grace.'
A reminder of God's limitless generosity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَجَائِزٌ فِي حَقِّهِ الْإِيْجَادُ وَالتَّرْكُ وَالْإِشْقَاءُ وَالْإِسْعَادُ وَمَنْ يَقُلْ فِعْلَ الصَّلَاحِ وَجَبَا عَلَى الْإِلَهِ قَدْ أَسَاءَ الْأَدَبَا وَاجْزِمْ أَخِي بِرُؤْيَةِ الْإِلَهِ فِي جَنَّةِ الْخُلْدِ بِغَيْرِ تَنَاهِي إِذِ الْوُقُوعُ جَائِزٌ بِالْعَقْلِ وَقَدْ أَتَى فِيهِ دَلِيلُ النَّقْلِ وَصِفْ جَمِيعَ الرُّسُلِ بِالْأَمَانَةِ وَالصِّدْقِ وَالتَّبْلِيغِ وَالْفَطَانَةِ وَيَسْتَحِيْلُ ضِدُّهَا عَلَيْهِمُ وَجَائِزٌ كَالْأَكْلِ فِي حَقِّهِمُ إِرْسَالُهُمْ تَفَضُّلٌ وَرَحْمَةٌ لِلْعَالَمِينَ جَلَّ مُوْلِي النِّعْمَةِ وَيَلْزَمُ الْإِيْمَانُ بِالْحِسَابِ وَالْحَشْرِ وَالْعِقَابِ وَالثَّوَابِ وَالنَّشْرِ وَالصِّرَاطِ وَالْمِيزَانِ وَالْحَوْضِ وَالنِّيرَانِ وَالْجِنَانِ وَالْجِنِّ وَالْأَمْلَاكِ ثُمَّ الْأَنْبِيَا وَالْحُورِ وَالْوِلْدَانِ ثُمَّ الْأَوْلِيَا وَبِكُلِّ مَا جَاءَ عَنِ الْبَشِيرِ مِنْ كُلِّ حُكْمِ صَارَ كَالضَّرُورِي وَيَنْطَوِي فِي كِلْمَةِ الْإِسْلَامِ مَا قَدْ مَضَى مِنْ سَائِرِ الْأَحْكَامِ
And it is possible (ja'iz) concerning Him to bring into existence, to leave, to cause misery, and to cause happiness. And whoever says that performing the most beneficial action (fi'l al-salah) is obligatory upon God has committed a breach of etiquette (asa'a al-adab). And be certain, my brother, of seeing Allah (ru'yat Allah) in the Paradise of Eternity without end, since its occurrence is possible (ja'iz) by reason (aql) and indeed the transmitted proof (dalil al-naql) has come concerning it. And describe all the messengers with trustworthiness (amanah), truthfulness (sidq), conveyance (tabligh), and intelligence (fatanah). And their opposites are impossible upon them. And what is possible concerning them, like eating, their sending is a favor (tafaddul) and mercy (rahmah) to the worlds. Exalted is the Master of grace. And necessary is faith in the Reckoning (hisab), the Gathering (hashr), the Punishment (iqab), the Reward (thawab), the Resurrection (nashr), the Path (sirat), the Balance (mizan), the Pool (hawd), the Fire (niran), the Gardens (jinan), the Jinn, the Angels (amlak), then the Prophets (anbiya), the Houris (hur), the Servants (wildan), then the Saints (awliya). And in everything that came from the Bringer of Glad Tidings (al-bashir) of every ruling, it has become like necessary (daruri) knowledge. And in the word of Islam (kalimat al-islam) is encompassed what has passed from the rest of the rulings.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a summary of many doctrinal points. It begins with the possible (ja'iz) actions of God: giving existence, withholding (giving nonexistence), causing misery, and causing happiness. The Ash'ari position is that God is not obligated to do what is most beneficial (al-salah) for creation; the Mu'tazilite view that He is so obligated is declared a breach of etiquette (asa'a al-adab). Next, the poem affirms the beatific vision (ru'yat Allah) in Paradise, which is possible by reason and confirmed by revelation. Then it lists the necessary attributes of prophets: amanah (trustworthiness), sidq (truthfulness), tabligh (conveying the message), and fatanah (intelligence). Their opposites are impossible. Their human actions like eating are possible. Their sending is a favor and mercy from God. Finally, the poem enumerates the eschatological and unseen articles of faith: ma'diyyat (tawba?), etc. It explicitly mentions: hisab (reckoning), hashr (gathering), iqab (punishment), thawab (reward), nashr (resurrection), sirat (bridge), mizan (balance), hawd (pool), niran (fire), jinan (paradise), jinn, angels, prophets, houris, wildan (servants), awliya (saints). It states that everything brought by the Prophet (al-bashir) is like necessary knowledge (daruri) and that all rulings are encompassed in the word of Islam (kalimat al-islam).

Key Terms
تَفَضُّل
رَحْمَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God was obligated to send prophets to guide humanity.
Correction: The poem says it is a favor and mercy, implying it is voluntary on God's part.

Checkpoint
According to the poem, what is the nature of sending prophets?
A) An obligation upon God
B) A favor and mercy
C) A reward for people
D) A test for believers

Key Takeaway
Prophets are sent by God as a favor and mercy, not because of any obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as though the prophets' message is a personal favor to you, or have you taken it for granted? What will you do today to show gratitude for this mercy?
?
If God sent prophets purely out of generosity, where in your life have you been reluctant to respond to that gift? Name one specific area of obedience you have withheld, and commit to act on it this week.
?
The sending of prophets is an undeserved mercy. When was the last time you sincerely thanked God for His prophets? Write one concrete action you will take this week to honor that favor (e.g., study their story, follow a sunnah, teach their message).

Belief in Eschatology and the Comprehensive Word of Islam
Sanusi enumerates the articles of faith concerning the hereafter and other unseen beings, and affirms the comprehensive nature of the word of Islam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the essential eschatological and unseen articles of faith and that the word of Islam encompasses all rulings.

Core Idea
Faith in the hereafter, angels, jinn, and all prophetic teachings is an integral part of Islam.

The poem concludes by listing many eschatological and unseen realities that a believer must have faith in. It then states that everything reported by the Prophet is as certain as necessary knowledge, and the word of Islam encompasses all these rulings.

Guided Path
1
Read the eschatological list
Read from 'wa yalzamu al-iman' to the end. Notice the many items.
2
Categorize the items
Group them into events (hisab, hashr, etc.), places (niran, jinan), and beings (jinn, angels, etc.).
This helps memorize.
3
Understand the certainty
The poem says everything from the Prophet is like necessary knowledge.
It is not open to doubt.
4
Grasp the comprehensive nature
The word of Islam (shahada) encompasses all these rulings.
Accepting Islam means accepting all these.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Divine Attributes

The Seven Divine Attributes and Their Eternity
The poem enumerates seven divine attributes, specifies their domains, and affirms their eternity as identical with the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven essential divine attributes according to the Ash'ari tradition, their relationship to the essence, and why they are eternal.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are eternal by essence, not separate added entities.

This poetic passage from al-Kharidah introduces the core Ash'ari doctrine on the divine attributes. It lists seven attributes, defines their objects, and emphasizes that they are not distinct from God's essence. Understanding this is key to avoiding anthropomorphism and preserving tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem and translation
Read the Arabic lines once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the rhythm and flow.
The poem uses concise wording; note the technical terms.
2
Identify the seven attributes and their objects
Find each attribute mentioned: knowledge, speech, power, will, hearing, sight. Note what each is related to (all categories, all possible things, every existent seen).
Knowledge and speech are linked to 'all categories'; power and will to 'all possible things'; hearing and sight to 'every existent seen'.
3
Understand the eternity and identity with essence
The poem states all attributes are 'eternal by essence' because they are 'not other than the essence'. Reflect on why this is important to avoid multiplicity in God.
This rejects the view that attributes are separate beings added to the essence.
4
Grasp the nature of divine speech and the impossibility of opposite
Divine speech is not composed of letters or order like human speech. The opposite of these attributes is impossible; otherwise God would be known by other things (contingent).
This safeguards God's transcendence and uniqueness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منظومة الخريدة البهية فَالْعِلْمُ جَزْماً وَالكَلَامُ السَّامِي تَعَلَّقَ بِسَائِرِ الأَقْسَامِ وَقُدْرَةٌ إِرَادَةٌ تَعَلَّقَ بِالْمُمْكِنَاتِ كُلِّهَا أَخَا التَّقَى وَاجْزِمْ بِأَنَّ سَمْعَهُ وَالبَصَرَا تَعَلَّقَا بِكُلِّ مَوْجُوْدٍ يُرَى وَكُلُّها قَدِيْمَةٌ بِالذَّاتِ لِأَنَّهَا لَيْسَتْ بِغَيْرِ الذَّاتِ ثُمَّ الكَلَامُ لَيْسَ بِالحُرُوفِ وَلَيْسَ بِالتَّرْتِيْبِ كَالْمَأْلُوْفِ وَيَسْتَحِيْلُ ضِدُّ مَا تَقَدَّمَا مِنَ الصِّفَاتِ الشَّامِخَاتِ فَاعْلَمَا لِأَنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ مَوْصُوْفَا بِهَا لَكَانَ بِالسِّوَى مَعْرُوفًا
The poem of the shining pearl: Knowledge without doubt, and the sublime Speech, are related to all categories. Power and will are related to all possible things, O man of piety. And be certain that His Hearing and Sight are related to every existent that is seen. And all of them are eternal by essence, because they are not other than the essence. Then Speech is not [composed] of letters nor of order, like the familiar. And the opposite of what has preceded of the exalted attributes is impossible, so know. Because if He were not described with them, He would be known by [what is] other [than them].
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the divine attributes. The poet begins by stating that knowledge and speech (al-ʿilm wa al-kalām) are related to all categories (sāʾir al-aqsām), likely meaning all created things or all that can be known. Next, power (qudra) and will (irāda) are related to all possible things (al-mumkināt). Hearing (samʿ) and sight (baṣar) are related to every existent that is seen (kull mawjūd yurā). The poet then asserts that all these attributes are eternal by essence (qadīma bi al-dhāt) because they are not other than the essence (laysat bi-ghayr al-dhāt) — i.e., they are not separate entities but are identical with the divine essence in a way that avoids anthropomorphism. Then he specifies that divine speech is not composed of letters (ḥurūf) or order (tartīb) like human speech (ka al-maʾlūf). Finally, he states that the opposite of these attributes is impossible (mustaḥīl), explaining that if God were not described with them, He would be known by what is other (bi al-siwā), implying contingency.

Key Terms
علم
كلام
قدرة
إرادة
سمع
بصر
قديم بالذات
حروف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking divine attributes are separate beings added to the essence.
Correction: The poem clarifies they are 'not other than the essence' — they are eternal and identical with the essence, not independent entities.

Checkpoint
According to the poem, what is the relationship of divine attributes to the essence?
A) They are other than the essence.
B) They are eternal by essence.
C) They are created.
D) They are separable.

Key Takeaway
The seven divine attributes are eternal by essence, not separate from God, and have specific relationships to creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's attributes are eternal and not separate from His essence, how does that confront your tendency to think of God's qualities as independent traits rather than as the very reality of who He is? Will you pause today to worship Him not as having many qualities but as being one perfect Being?
?
The poem says that if God lacked these attributes, He would be 'known by other things' — implying dependency. Where in your life have you made God dependent on your understanding or limited Him to what you can conceive? What would it mean to let go of that today?
?
Divine speech is not letters or order — it is beyond human language. Are you satisfied to encounter God in the Qur'an as a created book, or do you fear the awe of an uncreated, eternal speech that addresses you directly? What would change if you approached the Qur'an as the very attribute of God speaking to you right now?

God's Independence and the Permissibility of His Actions
The poem asserts God's absolute independence and rejects obligating Him.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God is absolutely independent and that it is not obligatory for Him to do what is best for creation; rather, all His actions are permissible.

Core Idea
God's actions are never obligated; claiming otherwise is a breach of adab.

This poem asserts God's utter independence and freedom from any obligation. It clarifies that God may create or not, cause happiness or misery, without being bound by what creatures consider best. The passage then sternly rebukes anyone who claims that God must act in the best interest of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic poem once, then read the English translation. Notice the rhythm and the key terms.
The poem is concise; each line carries a doctrinal point.
2
Identify the two key theological claims
Look for the claim of God's absolute independence (لا يفتقر) and the list of permissible actions (جائز).
3
Understand the rebuke against obligation
The poet explicitly condemns those who say God must do what is best. Reflect on why this is considered bad manners.
This rejection is directed at the Mu'tazilite view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَكُلُّ مَنْ قَامَ بِهِ سِوَاهَا فَهْوَ الَّذِي فِي الفَقْرِ قَدْ تَنَاهَى وَالوَاحِدُ المَعْبُوْدُ لَا يَفْتَقِرُ لِغَيْرِهِ جَلَّ الغَنِيُّ المُقْتَدِرُ وَجَائِزٌ فِي حَقِّهِ الإِيْجَادُ وَالتَّرْكُ وَالإِشْقَاءُ وَالإِسْعَادُ وَمَنْ يَقُلْ فِعْلَ الصَّلَاحِ وَجَبًا عَلَى الإِلَهِ قَدْ أَسَاءَ الأَدَبَا
And whoever has something other than them established in him, then he is the one who has reached the utmost in poverty. And the One Worshipped does not need other than Him, exalted is the Rich, the Powerful. And it is possible with respect to Him to bring into existence, to leave, to cause misery, and to grant happiness. And whoever says that doing the best is obligatory upon God has indeed shown bad manners.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage builds on the previous discussion of attributes. The poet states that anyone who has something other than these attributes (i.e., any contingent being) is utterly poor (fī al-faqr qad tanāhā). Then he affirms that God, the One Worshipped, does not need anything other than Himself (lā yaftaqiru li-ghayrihi). Therefore, it is permissible (jāʾiz) for Him to create (ījād) or not create (tark), to cause misery (ishqāʾ) or happiness (isʿād). The poet then condemns those who say that doing what is best (fiʿl al-ṣalāḥ) is obligatory (wajaban) upon God, calling it bad manners (asāʾa al-adab). This rejection of the Muʿtazilite doctrine of wujūb al-ṣalāḥ affirms God's absolute freedom.

Key Terms
جواز
إيجاد
ترك
إشقاء
إسعاد
وجوب الصلاح
أدب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God must always do what is best for His creation.
Correction: God is not obligated to do anything; claiming so is a breach of adab, as the poem states.

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on saying that God must do what is best?
A) It is obligatory belief.
B) It is permissible.
C) It is bad manners.
D) It is recommended.

Key Takeaway
God's absolute independence means He is not obliged to act in any specific way; all His actions are permissible, and to claim otherwise is a breach of adab.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The poet calls it bad manners to claim God owes you anything. In what specific ways have you, this week, acted as if God must give you something—good health, success, or comfort? How will you repent and instead prostrate in gratitude?
?
If God could have left you in misery but instead granted you happiness, does your heart overflow with thanks? Write one concrete way you will thank Him today—in privacy, with no one else watching.
?
Contemplate that every breath you take is a gift, not an obligation. How does that change your urgency in prayer? Commit to one extra prostration tonight, confessing your need and His freedom.

Attaching Judgment to a Derived Term and the Objection
Al-Sawi discusses the implication of deriving judgment from a term and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that attaching a judgment to a derived term indicates causality, and how the objection about restricting praise to one attribute is answered by distinguishing the cause of deserving from the cause of informing.

Core Idea
Derived terms signal causality for reporting, not restricting praise.

Al-Sawi explains an important linguistic principle: when a judgment (like praise) is attached to a derived term (like 'illuminator'), this implies that the root meaning is the cause of the judgment. This raises a potential objection about restricting praise to one attribute, which Al-Sawi answers by distinguishing between the cause of deserving and the cause of reporting.

Guided Path
1
Read the prayer and the principle
Read the Arabic passage from 'نور قلوبنا' to 'بسبعين درجة'. Notice how Al-Sawi connects the phrase to the principle of derivation.
2
Understand the objection
Read the objection starting with 'فإن قيل'. Grasp the concern about restricting praise to the attribute of illumination.
3
Study the answer
Read Al-Sawi's answer and note the key distinction he makes between the cause of deserving praise and the cause of informing.
This resolves the restriction issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نور قلوبنا بمعرفة عقائد التوحيد، وحرَّر عقولنا. وتعليق الحكم بالمشتق يدلُّ على عليَّة ما منه الاشتقاق، فكأنه قال: الحمد لله لتنويره، فهو حمد في مقابلة نعمة فيثاب عليه ثواب الواجب الزائد على النفل بسبعين درجة . فإن قيل : تعليق الحكم بمشتقّ يفيد قصر الحمد على خصوص ذلك المشتقّ، مع أنَّه يستحقُ الحمد لذاته وصفاته. أجيب : بأنَّ التَّنوير ليس علَّةً لاستحقاقه المحامد، بل علَّةً لإخبار الشيخ بثبوت استحقاقه تعالى لجميع المحامد.
He illuminated our hearts with knowledge of the creeds of tawhid, and freed our intellects. And the attachment of the judgment to the derived term indicates the causality of that from which it is derived. So it is as if He said: Praise be to Allah for His illumination. So it is praise in return for a favor, and he is rewarded for it with the reward of the obligatory, which exceeds the supererogatory by seventy degrees. If it is said: The attachment of the judgment to a derived term implies restricting praise to that particular derived thing, while He deserves praise for His essence and attributes. The answer is: The illumination is not the cause of His deserving praise, but rather the cause of the shaykh's informing of the established deserving of praise by Him, Most High, for all praises.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the phrase 'nur qulubana...'. Al-Sawi first quotes the matn phrase: 'He illuminated our hearts with knowledge of the creeds of tawhid and freed our intellects.' Then he explains that when a judgment is attached to a derived term (mushtaqq), it indicates that the root of derivation is the cause ('illiyya). Thus, saying 'al-hamdu lillah alladhi nawwara' (Praise be to Allah who illuminated) implies that the praise is for the act of illumination, as if to say 'Praise be to Allah for His illumination.' Therefore, this praise is in return for a blessing and thus merits the reward of an obligatory act, which exceeds supererogatory acts by seventy degrees. Al-Sawi then raises an objection: attaching a judgment to a derived term (here 'nawwara') suggests that the praise is restricted to that particular attribute (illumination), whereas Allah deserves praise for His essence and all attributes. The answer is that illumination is not the cause of Allah's deserving praise; rather, it is the cause for the speaker (the shaykh) to inform of the fact that Allah deserves all praise. In other words, the act is a means for the speaker to acknowledge and report the preexisting deserving of praise.

Key Terms
تعليق الحكم
المشتق
العليّة
القصر
التنوير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The derivation restricts praise to the particular attribute mentioned.
Correction: The derivation only makes that attribute the occasion for reporting the preexisting deserving of all praise.

Checkpoint
What is the answer to the objection that attaching praise to a derived term restricts praise to that attribute?
A) The derivation is only metaphorical and not real.
B) The illumination is the cause of the speaker's report, not the cause of Allah's deserving praise.
C) The objection is invalid because Allah only deserves praise for His acts.
D) The derived term is used for emphasis only.

Key Takeaway
Derived terms indicate the cause for reporting, not a restriction on the inherent deserving of praise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you praise Allah, do you find yourself focusing on one blessing and forgetting that He deserves praise for His essence and all attributes? How can you expand your hamd to encompass His infinite perfection? What will you do to ensure your praise is not limited to what you receive?
?
The shaykh's praise was occasioned by a specific favor—illumination of hearts. Think of a specific favor you received recently. Are you praising Allah solely for that favor, or does it lead you to praise Him for who He is? What will you change about your attitude in gratitude so that your hamd reflects His infinite deserving, not just your finite blessings?

Understanding 'Nur' and 'Qulub' in Context
Al-Sawi defines 'nur', explains its types, and clarifies 'qulub' and related phrases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the lexical and theological meaning of 'nur' in the prayer, including its two types and the identification of 'hearts' with intellects.

Core Idea
Allah's 'nur' is spiritual illumination, not physical light.

In this passage, al-Sawi unpacks the opening phrase 'nawwara Allahu qulubana bima'rifatihi.' He clarifies that 'nur' is derived from 'tanwir' and refers to moral light, not sensory light. He also explains that 'qulub' (hearts) actually means intellects, grounding the prayer in the realm of knowledge. This lesson walks you through these key clarifications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get an overview. Notice the flow from defining 'nur' to explaining 'qulub' and then the grammatical notes.
The passage is dense; don't worry about every detail now.
2
Analyze the definition of 'nur'
Identify the two types of light (sensory and moral) and which one is intended. Note the Quranic reference (Quran 24:35) as support.
The intended meaning is moral light, which is knowledge and guidance.
3
Understand the explanation of 'qulubina'
See how al-Sawi equates 'our hearts' with 'our intellects'. Understand why intellects are called hearts – because they reside in the physical heart.
This shows that the prayer is for intellectual illumination.
4
Examine the grammatical connections
Look at how 'bima'rifati' (with knowledge) is connected to 'nawwara' as a cause, and how 'wa harrara' (and freed) is conjunct as cause-to-effect.
These connect the concepts of illumination and emancipation.
5
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider that praising Allah for illumination is praising an attribute of action (sifat fi'l) after attributing it to the Divine Essence, indicating praise for both essence and attributes.
This deepens the meaning of the prayer as a comprehensive praise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و(نور): مشتق من التَّنوير ؛ وهو إيجاد النُّور الحسيّ أو المعنوي، والمراد هنا : المعنوي، الذي ضرب الله تعالى مَثَله بقوله جلَّ مِن قائل: مَثَلُ نُورِهِ ... الآية ، فهو حمدٌ على صفة الفعل بعد إسناده للذات العليَّة ؛ إشارة لكونه تعالى محموداً لذاته ولصفاته . وقوله : (قلوبنا :أي عقولنا ؛ لأنَّ النُّور المعنوي ينسب للعقول، وسمِّيت العقول قلوباً ؛ لحلولها بها . قوله : (بمعرفة) : متعلق بـ(نور)، والباء سببية، وسيأتي معنى المعرفة، والعقائد، والتّوحيد. قوله : ( وحرَّر) : معطوف على (نور) عطف سبب على مسبب; فهو من جملة صلة الموصول.
And (nur) is derived from tanwir, which is bringing into existence of sensory or moral light, and what is intended here is the moral light, which Allah Most High exemplified in His saying, may He be exalted: 'The likeness of His light ... verse.' So it is praise for an attribute of action after attributing it to the Supreme Essence, indicating that He, Most High, is praised for His essence and His attributes. And His saying: (our hearts) i.e., our intellects, because moral light is attributed to intellects, and intellects are called hearts because they reside in them. His saying: (with knowledge) is connected to (illuminated), and the ba' is causal, and the meaning of knowledge, creeds, and tawhid will come later. His saying: (and freed) is conjunct to (illuminated) as a conjunction of cause to effect, so it is part of the relative clause.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a lexical and theological explanation of the term 'nur' (light) in the opening phrase. Al-Sawi states that 'nur' is derived from 'tanwir' (illumination), which means bringing light into existence. Light is of two types: sensory (hissi) and moral (ma'nawi). The intended meaning here is moral light, which is knowledge and guidance. He references the Quranic verse 'The likeness of His light' (Quran 24:35) as an example. The praise for illumination is praise for an attribute of action (sifat fi'l) after being attributed to the Divine Essence, indicating that Allah is praised for both His essence and attributes. Then he explains 'qulubina' (our hearts) as meaning 'our intellects' (uquul), because moral light pertains to the intellect, and the intellect is called 'heart' because it resides in the physical heart. Next, he comments on 'bima'rifati' (with knowledge): it is connected to 'nur' (illuminated) as the cause, and the details of ma'rifa, 'aqa'id, and tawhid are deferred. Finally, he explains 'wa harrara' (and freed): it is a conjunct to 'nawwara' (illuminated) as a cause-to-effect conjunction, so it is part of the relative clause describing Allah.

Key Terms
النور
التنوير
المعنوي
الحسي
العقول
القلوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'nur' in the prayer refers to physical light, such as sunlight or candlelight.
Correction: Al-Sawi explicitly states that the intended meaning is moral light (al-nur al-ma'nawi), which is spiritual illumination of the intellect, not sensory light.

Checkpoint
What does 'qulubina' (our hearts) refer to in this context?
A) The physical hearts
B) The emotions
C) The intellects
D) The souls

Key Takeaway
In the prayer, 'nur' means spiritual illumination, and 'hearts' refers to the intellect, highlighting that true divine guidance is intellectual and moral.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have experienced physical light countless times, but when was the last time you felt the moral light of divine guidance change a single decision in your life? The prayer asks Allah to illuminate our hearts with knowledge. What specific knowledge are you lacking that, if acquired, would bring that light into a dark area of your life? Commit to seeking one piece of that knowledge today.
?
This passage equates 'hearts' with 'intellects'. Do you truly use your intellect to recognize Allah's attributes and commands, or do you go through the motions of faith without reflection? The prayer is not just for a feeling but for understanding. Write down one attribute of Allah you will ponder deeply today, and ask yourself how that attribute should transform your actions.
?
The prayer includes being freed from slavery to other than Him. Think honestly: Is there any attachment—a habit, a desire, a person, a status—that has enslaved your heart and clouded your intellect from seeing the truth of tawhid? What is one tangible step you can take this week to break that attachment and let the light of Allah's guidance fill your mind?
