Defining Tawfiq and the Dispute over Qudra
Al-Sawi defines tawfiq and presents the disagreement between Imam al-Haramayn and al-Ash'ari on qudra.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of tawfiq (divine assistance) and the key disagreement between Imam al-Haramayn and al-Ash'ari regarding the nature of qudra (capacity), including the objection raised against al-Ash'ari and how it is resolved.

Core Idea
Tawfiq is not just help—it's about how power and motivation are created in the servant.

This passage from al-Sawi's commentary introduces the key term 'tawfiq' and immediately throws us into a classical kalam debate. At stake is the nature of human capacity before an action: does God create the ability and motivation, or only the act itself? Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of tawfiq
Start by reading the linguistic and terminological definitions. Notice that tawfiq involves two components: capacity (qudra) and motivation (dāʿiya).
The linguistic meaning is 'conformity of one thing to another'.
2
Compare the two positions
See how Imam al-Haramayn and al-Ash'ari differ in their definitions. Pay attention to what each means by 'qudra'—for Imam al-Haramayn it is soundness of means, for al-Ash'ari it is an accident that does not persist.
The difference is based on whether an accident can remain for two moments.
3
Examine the objection to al-Ash'ari
The objection is that before obedience the servant is obligated, but if power only exists at the moment of the act, then he would be incapable during the obligation—which is impossible.
This is a standard kalam argument against occasionalism.
4
Understand the response and resolution
The answer is that obligation only requires soundness of means, not the actual power accident. So the disagreement on obligation is verbal, but over naming 'soundness' as 'power' it is real. Al-Sawi sides with Imam al-Haramayn.
This shows how kalam refines terminology to resolve disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتوفيق معناه لغةً : موافقة الشَّيء للشَّيء، واصطلاحاً : خلق قدرة الطاعة والداعية إليها في العبد عند إمام الحرمين؛ فالمراد بالقدرة عنده سلامة الأسباب والآلات بناءً على أنَّ العرض يبقى زمانين؛ فالكافر غير موفّق؛ لعدم الداعية، ويشهد لذلك قوله تعالى: (فَمَن يُرِدِ اللَّهُ أَن يَهْدِيَهُ يَشْرَحْ صَدْرَهُ لِلإِسْلَمِ) ، أي : يجعل داعيته ورغبته ومحبته إليه، وعند الأشعري : هو خلق الطاعة في العبد. والمراد بالقدرة: العرض المقارن للطَّاعة، بناءً على أنَّ العرض لا يبقى زمانين، أُورِدَ عليه أنَّه قبل الطاعة مكلَّف، فيلزم عليه تكليف العاجز. أجيب : بأنَّ التَّكليف متوقف على سلامة الأسباب والآلات، فتحصل أن الخُلْف من جهة التكليف لفظي؛ لاتفاقهما على أنَّ التكليف متوقف على سلامة الأسباب والآلات وأمَّا من جهة تسمية السلامة قدرة أو لا فحقيقي. فعند إمام الحرمين يسمَّى قدرةً، وعند الأشعري لا يسمى قدرةً، بل القدرة عنده هي العرض المقارن للطَّاعة، والحقُّ في هذه المسألة مع إمام الحرمين دون الأشعري.
And tawfiq (divine assistance) linguistically means the conformity of one thing to another. Terminologically, according to Imam al-Haramayn, it is the creation of the capacity (qudra) for obedience and the motivation for it in the servant. By 'capacity' he means soundness of means and instruments, based on the position that the accident remains for two moments. Thus the unbeliever is not assisted (muwaffaq) because of lack of motivation. The verse bears witness to that: 'For whoever Allah intends to guide, He opens his breast to Islam' [6:125], meaning He makes his motivation, desire, and love for it. According to al-Ash'ari, it is the creation of the act of obedience in the servant. By 'capacity' he means the accident that accompanies obedience, based on the position that the accident does not remain for two moments. It was objected to him that before obedience he [the servant] is obligated, so it would entail obligating the incapable. Answer: Obligation depends on soundness of means and instruments. Thus the disagreement concerning obligation is verbal, because they agree that obligation depends on soundness of means and instruments. As for naming soundness 'capacity' or not, that is real. For Imam al-Haramayn calls it capacity, while al-Ash'ari does not call it capacity; rather capacity according to him is the accident accompanying obedience. The truth in this issue is with Imam al-Haramayn, not al-Ash'ari.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a theological definition and debate. Al-Sawi first defines tawfiq linguistically and terminologically according to Imam al-Haramayn, explaining that it involves creating both the capacity and motivation. He then presents al-Ash'ari's view that tawfiq is simply the creation of obedience itself. The key difference lies in the definition of qudra (power/capacity): Imam al-Haramayn considers it to be the soundness of means (which persists), while al-Ash'ari considers it an accident that does not persist. An objection is raised against al-Ash'ari: if power is only present with the act, then prior to the act the person is obligated but incapable, which is impossible. The answer is that obligation only requires soundness of means, not the actual power (accident). Thus the dispute is partly verbal, but regarding the naming of soundness as power, it is real. Al-Sawi sides with Imam al-Haramayn.

Key Terms
التوفيق
القدرة
العرض
إمام الحرمين
الأشعري
خلق الطاعة
سلامة الأسباب والآلات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that tawfiq is simply God's help or guidance without considering the technical definition of power (qudra).
Correction: Tawfiq involves a specific definition of power: either soundness of means (Imam al-Haramayn) or an accident accompanying the act (al-Ash'ari). The dispute affects how we understand divine assistance and human responsibility.

Checkpoint
What objection is raised against al-Ash'ari's definition of qudra as an accident that does not persist?
A) It implies that the unbeliever has no motivation
B) It entails obligating the incapable before obedience
C) It contradicts the verse about opening the breast
D) It makes obligation dependent on soundness of means

Key Takeaway
The dispute over qudra between Imam al-Haramayn and al-Ash'ari is partly verbal but has a real consequence in how we define divine assistance (tawfiq).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel a lack of motivation to pray or obey, do you attribute that to your own weakness, or do you recognize that even the desire to obey must be created in you by Allah? What would it mean to humbly ask Him for tawfiq in that moment?
?
The passage says the unbeliever is not given motivation (dā'iya) because of their rejection. Have you ever considered that some of your own spiritual laziness might be a sign that Allah has withheld motivation from you? What would you change to seek that motivation again?
?
If the very capacity to obey is a gift from Allah that He creates fresh in each moment, how seriously do you take the next act of obedience? Will you approach your next prayer as if it is entirely dependent on His ongoing creation of power and will in you?

The Ash'ari Meaning of Sharh al-Sadr
Al-Sawi explains the Quranic phrase and the Ash'ari definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari interpretation of the Quranic phrase 'sharh al-sadr' and its theological significance in defining divinely created obedience.

Core Idea
Sharh al-sadr is not mere inclination but God's creation of obedience.

In this lesson, we examine a concise definition from al-Sawi's commentary. The phrase 'sharh al-sadr' appears in the Quran, and al-Ash'ari gives it a precise theological meaning: the creation of obedience. This highlights the Ash'ari doctrine that all actions are directly from God. Let's explore the source passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the basic meaning.
The passage starts with the basmala, then explains the phrase.
2
Identify the lexical meaning
The passage explains 'yashrah sadrahu lil'islam' as God making motivation, desire, and love towards Islam.
This is the plain language meaning.
3
Identify the Ash'ari theological definition
Notice the shift: 'And according to al-Ash'ari: it is the creation of obedience in the servant.'
This is a specific theological position.
4
Reflect on the difference
Compare the lexical meaning (inclination) with the Ash'ari meaning (divine creation). Think about why al-Ash'ari might define it this way.
Consider the theological implications for human action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم) أي : أؤلّف، يَشْرَحْ صَدْرَهُ لِلإِسْلَمِ (١) ، أي : يجعل داعيته ورغبته ومحبته إليه، وعند الأشعري : هو خلق الطاعة في العبد.
(In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful) meaning: I compose. 'He expands his breast for Islam' (1), meaning: He makes his motivation, desire, and love towards it. And according to al-Ash'ari: it is the creation of obedience in the servant.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage defines the Quranic phrase 'yashrah sadrahu lil'islam'. The author first gives a lexical explanation: it means God makes one's inclination and love towards Islam. Then he cites the Ash'ari theological definition: it is the creation of obedience in the servant. This highlights the Ash'ari position that all acts, including faith, are directly created by God.

Key Terms

خلق الطاعة
الأشعري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'sharh al-sadr' is merely metaphorical, indicating a broad or open heart.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari gives a concrete meaning: it is God's creation of obedience in the servant, a direct divine act.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari defines 'sharh al-sadr' as God creating obedience in the servant, making it a direct act of divine creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a desire to obey Allah, do you pause to recognize that this desire—and the act itself—is created by Him? How does that awareness change your sense of humility and gratitude?
?
If every obedient act is directly created by Allah, then what is your role? Are you merely a passive recipient, or is there a partnership? How does this teaching affect your understanding of personal responsibility in worship?
?
Have you ever taken credit for your own obedience, thinking you earned it by your own effort? What would it look like to live each moment acknowledging that even your ability to obey is a gift from Allah? Can you make that your constant mindset?

Understanding 'al-Dhat' and 'al-Wajib al-Wujud' in the Basmala
Al-Sawi explains 'al-dhat' and 'al-wajib al-wujud' in the basmala.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of 'al-dhat' and 'al-wajib al-wujud' in the basmala, and why 'al-wajib al-wujud' is mentioned as an explanation of the named essence, not as part of the name.

Core Idea
God's essence is known, and 'Necessary Existence' explains it, not names it.

In his commentary on the basmala, Al-Sawi carefully unpacks each word. The first two phrases—'على الذات' and 'الواجب الوجود'—are not trivial. He explains what the definite article and the 'ta' at the end of 'dhat' signify, and why the attribute 'al-wajib al-wujud' is attached. This lesson will walk through his precise explanations, clarifying a common point of confusion about naming and describing God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the precise wording: 'أل للعهد' and 'تاؤها ليست للتأنيث'.
2
Identify the two main explanations
The passage has two parts: explanation of 'على الذات' and explanation of 'الواجب الوجود'. Note each definition and its nuance.
For 'الواجب الوجود', pay attention to the purpose clause.
3
Understand the distinction between naming and explaining
Al-Sawi emphasizes that 'الواجب الوجود' is not part of the name but clarifies which essence is named. Ask yourself why this distinction matters for Islamic theology.
This guards against misunderstanding that God's attributes are part of His essence in naming.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (على الذات (أل) للعهد; أي: الذات المعهودة، وهي : الخالقة للعالم، وتاؤها ليست للتأنيث، بل للوحدة. قوله : (الواجب الوجود) أي: الذَّات التي لا يمكن عدمها في الماضي ولا في الحال ولا في الاستقبال والغرض من ذكر واجب الوجود بيان الذات المسمَّى لا بيان اعتباره في المسمى; لأنَّ المسمَّى الذات وحدها لا الذات مع الوصف. قوله : (بنيتا للمبالغة) : أي : للدَّلالة على المبالغة مع إفادة دوام الرّحمة وثباتها، فاندفع ما يقال: إنَّ بناءهما للمبالغة ينافي كونهما صفتين مشبَّهتين . قوله: (من رحم; بالكسر :أي: من مصدر رحم على مذهب البصريين، أو من نفس رحم على مذهب الكوفيين
His statement: 'upon the essence'—the 'al' is for the covenant, meaning the well-known essence, and it is the one that creates the world, and its 'ta' is not for feminine but for singularity. His statement: 'the Necessary Existence'—meaning the essence whose non-existence is impossible in the past, present, and future; and the purpose of mentioning 'Necessary Existence' is to clarify the named essence, not to state its consideration in the naming, because the named is the essence alone, not the essence with the attribute. His statement: 'built for exaggeration'—meaning for indicating exaggeration along with conveying the permanence and stability of mercy, so what is said is refuted: that their being built for exaggeration contradicts their being two adjectives of the similar pattern. His statement: 'from rahima with kasra'—meaning from the infinitive of rahima according to the Basran school, or from the root rahim according to the Kufan school.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the basmala phrase. It first explains 'على الذات': the definite article 'al' in 'al-dhat' refers to the well-known essence (the Creator). The 'ta' at the end of 'dhat' is not feminine but indicates singularity. Next, it explains 'الواجب الوجود' as the essence whose non-existence is impossible at all times; the mention of this attribute clarifies the named essence but does not imply that the attribute is part of the name—the name refers to the essence alone. Then, it explains that 'الرحمن الرحيم' are adjectives built for exaggeration (صفتان مشبهتان بنيتا للمبالغة), indicating the intensity and permanence of mercy. This resolves an objection: the pattern of exaggeration does not conflict with their being adjectives of the similar pattern (صفتان مشبهتان). Finally, it provides a grammatical note on the root of 'رحم', giving the Basran and Kufan views on its derivation.

Correction: The commentary explicitly states that the mention of 'الواجب الوجود' is to clarify the named essence, not to be considered part of the name; the name refers to the essence alone.

Key Takeaway
In the basmala, 'على الذات' refers to the well-known Creator, and 'الواجب الوجود' explains that essence without being part of its name.

The Exaggeration Pattern and Root of 'al-Rahman' and 'al-Rahim'
Al-Sawi comments on 'الرحمن' and 'الرحيم' in the basmala.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the forms 'الرحمن' and 'الرحيم' are built for exaggeration and how they are derived from the root 'رحم'.

Core Idea
The names 'الرحمن' and 'الرحيم' indicate intense, permanent mercy, derived from 'رحم'.

After explaining the first part of the basmala, Al-Sawi turns to the two names 'الرحمن' and 'الرحيم'. These are not simple adjectives; their form indicates exaggeration and permanence. He also addresses a potential objection and provides a grammatical derivation. This lesson unpacks these subtle points.

The key is that exaggeration here does not mean hyperbole but intensity and constancy.
3

The Intensive Divine Names al-Rahman and al-Rahim
Grammatical and theological explanation of Rahman and Rahim as intensive adjectives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical derivation of al-Rahman and al-Rahim as intensive adjectives and the metaphorical use of mercy when attributed to Allah.

Core Idea
The names al-Rahman and al-Rahim are intensive adjectives derived from an intransitive verb, metaphorically expressing Allah's boundless mercy.

This passage from the commentary on al-Kharida explains how the divine names al-Rahman and al-Rahim are formed as intensive adjectives. Since intensive adjectives in Arabic are derived from intransitive verbs, but 'rahima' is transitive, two grammatical strategies are used to treat it as intransitive. Furthermore, the commentator clarifies that when mercy is attributed to Allah, it is a metaphorical expression for the actual bestowal of favor, not an emotion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage slowly. Then read the English translation. Notice the technical terms like 'sifa mushabbahah' and 'majaz mursal'.
The Arabic uses grammatical terminology; don't worry if it feels dense—the commentary will unpack it.
2
Identify the two methods for treating the verb as intransitive
The passage presents two ways to derive the intensive adjectives from the transitive verb 'rahima'. Find them and understand why such treatment is necessary.
These methods are grammatically permissible devices in Arabic morphology.
3
Understand the metaphorical interpretation of mercy
The commentator explains the linguistic meaning of 'rahma' and how it is applied to Allah: it is a metonymy where the cause (mercy) stands for the effect (bestowal of benefits).
This prevents anthropomorphism and aligns with Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و(الرَّحمن الرّحيم : صفتان مشبَّهتان بُنيتا للمبالغة من رحم - بالكسر – إما بتنزيله منزلة اللازم بأن يُقصد إثباته للفاعل فقط من غير اعتبار تعلقه بمفعول، وإما بجعله لازماً بأن ينقل إلى فعل - بالضم - وإنَّما احتيج لذلك؛ لأنَّ الصفة المشبَّهة إِنَّما تُصاغ من اللازم. والرَّحمة رقّةُ القلب؛ أي رأفته، وهي تستلزم التَّفضَّلَ والإحسان، فهو غايتها وهي مبدؤه، فيراد منها هنا الغاية. قوله : ( بأن يقصد إثباته) بيان وتصوير للتّنزيل . قوله : (بأن ينقل إلى فعل) : تصوير لجعله لازماً؛ لأنَّ (فعل) بالضَّمِّ، لا يكون إلا لازماً . قوله : (وإنَّما احتيج لذلك اسم الإشارة عائد على التّنزيل أو التَّحويل . قوله : (إِنَّما تصاغ من اللازم) : أي : لقول ابن مالك: وصَوْغُـهَـا مـن لازم لـحـاضـر . . . . . . . قوله : (والرَّحمة : رقّة القلب) : أي : في أصل وضع اللغة. قوله : (فهو غايتها) : أي : ثمرتها . وقوله : (وهي مبدؤه) : أي : منشؤه. قوله : ( فيراد منها هنا الغاية) : أي : ففيه مجاز مرسل من إطلاق السَّبب
And 'al-Rahman al-Rahim' are two intensive adjectives (sifat mushabbahah) built for exaggeration from 'rahima' (past tense verb, with kasra [broken i]), either by treating it as intransitive by intending to affirm it for the agent only without considering its connection to an object, or by making it intransitive by converting it to a verb of pattern 'fa'ula' (with dammah). And this was needed because the intensive adjective is only derived from an intransitive verb. Mercy is tenderness of the heart, i.e., compassion, and it entails beneficence and kindness. So He is its ultimate end and it is His beginning. So here what is meant by it is the ultimate end. His statement: ('by intending to affirm it') is an explanation and depiction of the treatment. His statement: ('by converting it to fa'ula') is a depiction of making it intransitive, because fa'ula (with dammah) is only intransitive. His statement: ('and this was needed') – the demonstrative pronoun refers to the treatment or the conversion. His statement: ('It is only derived from the intransitive') i.e., according to the saying of Ibn Malik: 'And deriving it from the intransitive for the present...' His statement: ('and mercy is tenderness of the heart') i.e., in the original coinage of the language. His statement: ('so He is its ultimate end') i.e., its fruit. And His statement: ('and it is His beginning') i.e., its origin. His statement: ('so here what is meant by it is the ultimate end') i.e., so in that is a metonymy (majaz mursal) of naming the cause.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and theological analysis of the divine names 'al-Rahman' and 'al-Rahim'. The commentator states that both are intensive adjectives (sifat mushabbahah) built for exaggeration, derived from the verb 'rahima' (to have mercy). Because intensive adjectives in Arabic are normally derived from intransitive verbs, but 'rahima' is transitive, two methods are proposed to treat it as intransitive: either by conceiving it as intransitive (focusing only on the subject without an object), or by converting it to the intransitive pattern 'fa'ula'. This ensures the derivation is valid. Then the commentator explains the meaning of 'rahma' (mercy): in its original linguistic sense, it is 'tenderness of the heart' or compassion, which necessarily leads to acts of kindness and beneficence. In reference to Allah, however, the term is used metaphorically (majaz mursal) where the cause (mercy as an attribute) stands for the effect (the actual bestowal of kindness), because Allah does not have a physical heart. Thus, 'al-Rahman' and 'al-Rahim' signify the abundant and continuous mercy of Allah, emphasizing the actual beneficence.

الرحيم
الصفة المشبهة
المبالغة
الرحمة
المجاز المرسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mercy in Allah means emotional tenderness like in humans.
Correction: Mercy attributed to Allah is a metaphorical expression for His act of bestowing favor, not an emotion.

Checkpoint

A) Because the words are derived from a transitive verb.
B) Because intensive adjectives are only derived from intransitive verbs in Arabic grammar.
C) Because 'al-Rahman' and 'al-Rahim' are not derived from a verb.
D) Because mercy is an attribute of action, not essence.

Key Takeaway
Al-Rahman and al-Rahim are intensive adjectives derived from an intransitive verb, and mercy in Allah's names is a figurative expression for His abundant favor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Attributes of Essence vs. Action: The Case of ar-Raḥma
Al-Sawi distinguishes between attributes of essence and action, using the example of الرحمة.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a divine name like ar-Raḥma can be interpreted either as an attribute of essence (eternal) or an attribute of action (created), and why this distinction matters for both theology and practice.

Core Idea
The same name of Allah can be eternal or temporal depending on interpretation.

In this passage, Al-Sawi explains a fundamental principle for understanding divine names: some names can refer either to Allah's eternal will to act (attribute of essence) or to the action itself (attribute of action). This distinction is not just theoretical—it has practical consequences for how we understand supplications. Using the example of ar-Raḥma, Al-Sawi shows how the meaning changes and why it matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, identifying the two different ways a divine name can be understood.
Pay attention to the terms 'murīd dhālika' (one who intends) and 'al-fāʿil' (the agent).
2
Distinguish the two types of attributes
Notice how Al-Sawi classifies the interpretation: if the name refers to the 'one who intends' bestowal, it is an attribute of essence; if it refers to the 'bestower' as agent, it is an attribute of action.
Attributes of essence are eternal, attributes of action are created (according to Ashʿarīs).
3
Apply the distinction to ar-Raḥman
Al-Sawi notes that ar-Raḥman is a name specific to Allah, then shows its application in a supplication.
4
Analyze the supplication example
Read the example 'Allahumma ijmaʿna fī mustaqarri raḥmatik'. Understand why it is permissible if raḥma is an attribute of action, but not if it is an attribute of essence.
This connects theology to everyday practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا كلُّ اسم من أسمائه تعالى يوهم ظاهره خلاف المراد ويراد منه غايته .
ثمَّ إن أريد مُريدَ ذلك؛ كمريد الإنعام فصفة ذات، وإن أريد الفاعل ؛ كالمنعم فصفة فعل.
وقدَّم (الرَّحمن لأنَّه خاص به تعالى، إذ لا يطلق على غيره تعالى، قوله: (وكذا كلُّ اسم ... إلخ) : أي : كصبور، ورؤوف، وحليم،
وودود.
قوله : (مريد ذلك ) : أي : التفضل والإحسان.
قوله : (فصفة ذات) : أي : فالرَّحمة : صفة ذات، وهي قديمة باتّفاق.
قوله : (وإن أريد الفاعل) : أي : اسم الفاعل .
وقوله (فصفة فعل) : أي : فالرَّحمة صفة فعل، وهي حادثة عند الأشاعرة، ويترتب على كلِّ حكم قول من قال : اللهم اجمعنا في مستقر رحمتك ؛ فإن أراد أنَّ الرَّحمة صفة فعل جاز؛ لأنَّ المعنى : اجمعنا في مستقر إنعامك؛ وهو الجنَّة، وإن أراد أنَّها صفة ذات لـم يـجـز؛ لأنَّ المعنى: اجمعنا في مستقر إرادتك؛ وهو ذاتك.
قوله : (إذ لا يطلق على غيره تعالى : أي : وأما قول الشاعر (۱) :
[ من البسيط]
(1) أورده الزمخشري في تفسيره) (۷۱)، وصدره (سموت بالمجد بابن الأكرمين
أباً .
And likewise every name of His, exalted is He, whose literal sense suggests something contrary to what is intended, and by it is intended its ultimate meaning. Then, if what is intended is the one who intends that (murīd dhālika) such as the one who intends bestowal, then it is an attribute of essence (ṣifat dhāt); and if what is intended is the agent (al-fāʿil) such as the bestower (al-munʿim), then it is an attribute of action (ṣifat fiʿl). And He introduced (ar-Raḥmān) because it is specific to Him, exalted, as it is not applied to other than Him. His saying: (And likewise every name... etc.) meaning: like Ṣabūr, Raʾūf, Ḥalīm, and Wadūd. His saying: (the one who intends that) meaning: the bestowal and bounty. His saying: (then it is an attribute of essence) meaning: then mercy is an attribute of essence, and it is eternal by consensus. His saying: (and if what is intended is the agent) meaning: the agent noun. And His saying: (then it is an attribute of action) meaning: then mercy is an attribute of action, and it is created (ḥāditha) according to the Ashʿarīs. And upon each ruling follows the saying of one who says: 'O Allah, gather us in the locus of Your mercy.' If he intends that mercy is an attribute of action, it is permissible, because the meaning is: gather us in the locus of Your bounty, which is Paradise. And if he intends that it is an attribute of essence, it is not permissible, because the meaning is: gather us in the locus of Your will, which is Your essence. His saying: (since it is not applied to other than Him) meaning: and as for the saying of the poet (1): [al-Basīt] (1) al-Zamakhsharī mentioned it in his tafsīr (71), and its first hemistich: 'You rose with glory to the son of the most noble father.'
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage analyzes the names of Allah, particularly ar-Raḥmān and ar-Raḥīm, and distinguishes between attributes of essence (صفة ذات) and attributes of action (صفة فعل). It begins by stating that every name of Allah that might be misunderstood in its literal sense is interpreted figuratively to its intended meaning. Then it makes a key distinction: if the name is taken to refer to the 'one who intends' (مريد) the action (e.g., the one who intends to bestow mercy), then it denotes an attribute of essence, which is eternal by consensus. If it is taken to refer to the 'agent' (فاعل) of the action (e.g., the bestower), then it is an attribute of action, which is created (حادثة) according to the Ashʿarī school. The passage applies this to the name ar-Raḥmān, which is unique to Allah. It then provides examples of other names like Ṣabūr, Raʾūf, Ḥalīm, and Wadūd that follow the same principle. The commentator explains the practical implication using the supplication 'اللهم اجمعنا في مستقر رحمتك' (O Allah, gather us in the locus of Your mercy). If one intends الرحمة as an attribute of action, meaning 'Your bounty,' then the locus is Paradise, and the supplication is permissible. If one intends it as an attribute of essence, meaning 'Your eternal will,' then the locus is Allah's essence, which is impossible, so the supplication is impermissible. The passage ends with a citation of a poet's verse, which is incomplete in the OCR, likely illustrating the non-exclusive use of the name.

Key Terms
صفة ذات
صفة فعل
رحمة
قديم
حادث
أشاعرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All divine attributes are either all eternal or all created; there is no flexibility in interpretation.
Correction: The same name (e.g., raḥma) can be interpreted either as an attribute of essence (eternal) or an attribute of action (created), depending on the intended meaning (murīd vs. fāʿil). This flexibility is essential for correctly understanding divine names and avoiding theological errors.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Sawi, what is the ruling on saying 'اللهم اجمعنا في مستقر رحمتك' if the speaker intends الرحمة as an attribute of action?
A) Permissible, because it means Paradise.
B) Impermissible, because it implies a place for an attribute.
C) Permissible, because it refers to God's essence.
D) It is a matter of dispute.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between attributes of essence and action is crucial for correctly interpreting divine names and avoiding theological errors in supplication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Bismillāh ir-Raḥmān ir-Raḥīm' before any action, do you consciously reflect on whether you are seeking the eternal mercy of His essence or the temporal mercy of His action? How might that awareness change your reliance on Him?
?
Consider a supplication you often make, like 'Rabbi irḥamnī' (My Lord, have mercy on me). Which interpretation of raḥma do you intend? If it is merely asking for a created bounty, are you satisfied with that? Or do you yearn for the eternal embrace of His mercy?
?
The lesson shows that a seemingly innocent supplication can be theologically problematic if misunderstood. Are there other common phrases you use that might carry implicit theological assumptions? Will you investigate them with the same care?

Interpreting Divine Names and Essential vs. Active Attributes
Al-Sawi explains the rule for interpreting divine names and distinguishes essential from active attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rule for interpreting divine names whose apparent meaning seems contrary to intended meaning, and the distinction between essential attributes (eternal) and active attributes (temporal among Ash'aris).

Core Idea
Divine names are interpreted by their ultimate purpose; essential attributes are eternal, active attributes are temporal.

Al-Sawi explains a key principle of Islamic theology: when a name of Allah appears to imply something inappropriate, we interpret it according to its ultimate purpose (ghāya). He then clarifies the difference between essential attributes (ṣifāt al-dhāt), which are eternal by consensus, and active attributes (ṣifāt al-fiʿl), which are considered temporally originated by the Ashʿarīs. The attribute of mercy serves as the central example.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rule for divine names
Read the rule: every divine name whose apparent meaning seems contrary to the intended meaning is interpreted by its ultimate purpose (ghāya).
Examples: Sabūr, Ra'ūf, Halīm, Wadūd.
2
Distinguish essential vs. active attributes
If the name is taken to refer to the attribute itself (e.g., the will to be merciful), it is ṣifat al-dhāt (eternal). If taken to refer to the agent (e.g., the One who is merciful), it is ṣifat al-fiʿl (temporal among Ash'aris).
Mercy (al-raḥma) can be either depending on interpretation.
3
Apply the distinction to a supplication
Consider the prayer: 'O Allah, gather us in the abiding place of Your mercy.' If mercy is active (beneficence), it means Paradise; if essential (will), it would mean the divine essence, which is not permissible.
The permissibility of the prayer hinges on the interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا كلُّ اسم من أسمائه تعالى يوهم ظاهره خلاف المراد ويراد منه غايته . ثمَّ إن أريد مُريدَ ذلك؛ كمريد الإنعام فصفة ذات، وإن أريد الفاعل ؛ كالمنعم فصفة فعل. قوله: (وكذا كلُّ اسم ... إلخ) : أي : كصبور، ورؤوف، وحليم، وودود. قوله : (مريد ذلك ) : أي : التفضل والإحسان. قوله : (فصفة ذات) : أي : فالرَّحمة : صفة ذات، وهي قديمة باتّفاق. قوله : (وإن أريد الفاعل) : أي : اسم الفاعل . وقوله (فصفة فعل) : أي : فالرَّحمة صفة فعل، وهي حادثة عند الأشاعرة، ويترتب على كلِّ حكم قول من قال : اللهم اجمعنا في مستقر رحمتك ؛ فإن أراد أنَّ الرَّحمة صفة فعل جاز؛ لأنَّ المعنى : اجمعنا في مستقر إنعامك ؛ وهو الجنَّة، وإن أراد أنَّها صفة ذات لـم يـجـز؛ لأنَّ المعنى: اجمعنا في مستقر إرادتك؛ وهو ذاتك.
And likewise, every name from among His names – may He be exalted – whose apparent meaning suggests something contrary to the intended meaning, and what is intended by it is its ultimate purpose. Then if what is intended is the one who intends that (attribute) – like the one who intends beneficence – then it is an attribute of the essence; and if what is intended is the agent – like the Beneficent – then it is an attribute of action. He said: '(And likewise every name ... etc.)' meaning: like Sabūr, Ra'ūf, Halīm, and Wadūd. He said: '(the one who intends that)' meaning: favor and benevolence. He said: '(so it is an attribute of the essence)' meaning: so mercy (al-raḥma) is an attribute of the essence, and it is eternal by consensus. He said: '(and if what is intended is the agent)' meaning: the noun of the agent. And He said: '(so it is an attribute of action)' meaning: so mercy is an attribute of action, and it is temporal according to the Ash'aris. And arising from each ruling is the saying of one who says: 'O Allah, gather us in the abiding place of Your mercy.' If he intends that mercy is an attribute of action, it is permissible because the meaning is: gather us in the abiding place of Your beneficence – which is Paradise. And if he intends that it is an attribute of the essence, it is not permissible because the meaning is: gather us in the abiding place of Your will – which is Your essence.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line of text that states a rule concerning divine names. The rule is that when a name of Allah, in its apparent form, seems to indicate something contrary to what is actually intended (like implying a bodily attribute or change), it must be interpreted according to its ultimate intended meaning, not its literal sense. The commentator then explains the distinction between two types of attributes: essential attributes (ṣifāt al-dhāt) and active attributes (ṣifāt al-fiʿl). The key example given is the attribute of mercy (al-raḥma). If one intends by the word 'mercy' the attribute itself (i.e., the will to be merciful), then it is an essential attribute, eternal by consensus. If one intends the agent (the one who is merciful), then it is an active attribute, which the Ashʿarīs consider temporal (ḥādith). The commentator then applies this distinction to a supplication: 'O Allah, gather us in the abiding place of Your mercy.' If mercy is taken as an active attribute (i.e., beneficence), the prayer is permissible because it asks to be placed in Paradise; but if taken as an essential attribute (i.e., divine will), it would mean asking to be gathered in Allah's essence, which is not permissible.

Key Terms
صفة ذات
صفة فعل
رحمة
قديم
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The distinction between essential and active attributes is about whether the attribute is created or uncreated by nature.
Correction: Essential attributes are eternal by consensus, while active attributes are considered temporally originated (ḥādith) by the Ashʿarīs. The distinction is about how the attribute relates to the essence, not its inherent createdness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the rule for interpreting divine names whose apparent meaning seems contrary to the intended meaning?
A) They are taken literally without interpretation.
B) They are interpreted according to their ultimate purpose (ghāya).
C) They are rejected as anthropomorphic.
D) They are considered metaphorical only for active attributes.

Key Takeaway
Divine names with apparent problematic meanings are interpreted by their ultimate purpose, and the distinction between essential and active attributes determines whether the attribute is eternal or temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you call upon Allah as 'Ar-Raheem' in your du'a, do you take a moment to consider what you are actually asking for? Are you seeking His temporal mercy (active attribute) in your life today, or are you merely reciting a name without awareness of its meaning?
?
This lesson warns that misunderstanding a divine attribute can lead to an impermissible supplication. Is there any prayer you have been making that might unknowingly be based on a flawed assumption about Allah's nature? Will you examine your du'as this week to ensure they align with sound belief?
?
The distinction between essential and active attributes shows that some of Allah's actions (like His mercy as beneficence) are temporal and directly connected to your present moment. Do you truly believe that His mercy is actively reaching you right now, or do you treat it as a distant concept? What would change in your daily reliance on Him if you internalized this?

The Exclusivity of the Name 'Al-Rahman'
Al-Sawi explains why الرحمن is mentioned first (khaṣṣ) and refutes the poet's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the divine name 'al-Rahman' is unique to Allah and how the objection from poetic usage is addressed.

Core Idea
Al-Rahman is exclusive to Allah in its definite form; indefinite usage is irrelevant.

The basmala begins with 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim', and scholars ask why 'al-Rahman' is mentioned before 'al-Rahim'. Al-Sawi explains that it is because 'al-Rahman' is a name specific to Allah, never applied to anyone else. But a poet once called a human 'rahmān', so how can it be exclusive? This lesson unpacks that objection and its refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage from al-Sawi's commentary carefully. Note the structure: first the claim, then the objection, then the rebuttals.
The passage contains a quote from a poet; don't get distracted by the incomplete line.
2
Identify the key claim
Find the phrase that states why al-Rahman is prefixed: 'لأنه خاص به تعالى، إذ لا يطلق على غيره'. Understand that this means the name is exclusive.
3
Examine the objection
The poet's line uses 'rahmān' for a human. Note that this seems to contradict the claim. Think about how the commentator might respond.
The poet's line is partially reconstructed; the exact wording is less important than the principle.
4
Understand the three rebuttals
The passage offers three possible answers: (1) the poet used an indefinite form, while the divine name is definite; (2) the poet was obstinate in disbelief; (3) the line is rhetorical. See how each defends the exclusivity.
The rebuttals are introduced with 'أو' – 'or', showing alternative explanations.
5
Reflect on theological significance
Consider why this matters: affirming that Allah's names are unique and cannot be applied to creation in the same sense. This protects tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقدَّم (الرَّحمن لأنَّه خاص به تعالى، إذ لا يطلق على غيره تعالى. قوله : (إذ لا يطلق على غيره تعالى : أي : وأما قول الشاعر (۱) : [من البسيط] (1) أورده الزمخشري في تفسيره) (۷۱)، وصدره (سموت بالمجد بابن الأكرمين أباً . وأنت غيث الورى لا زلت رحماناً في حق مسيلمة الكذاب فشاةٌ، أو لأنَّه منكر، والخاص بالله المعرَّف، أو من تعنّتهم في كفرهم.
And He prefixed (al-Raḥmān because He is specific to Him – may He be exalted – since it is not used for any other besides Him – may He be exalted). His statement: '(since it is not used for any other besides Him – may He be exalted)' means: and as for the saying of the poet (1): [from al-Basīṭ] (1) al-Zamakhsharī mentioned it in his commentary (71), and its first hemistich is: 'You rose to glory through the noblest of the noble fathers.' And: 'You are the rain cloud of creation, may you always be merciful (raḥmān) to the likes of Musaylima the liar, a sheep?!' Or because it is indefinite, and what is specific to Allah is the definite form, or because of their obstinacy in their disbelief.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the reason why the name al-Raḥmān is placed before al-Raḥīm in the basmala: because it is specific (khaṣṣ) to Allah – it is not applied to anyone else. The commentator then addresses an objection raised by a poet who used the term 'raḥmān' for a human being (addressing someone as 'raḥmān' meaning merciful). The commentator summarizes the objection: the poet said 'You are the rain cloud of creation, may you always be merciful (raḥmān) to the likes of Musaylima the liar, a sheep?!' (i.e., how can you call someone merciful who is harsh even to a liar?). The commentator then offers three possible rebuttals: (1) the poet's usage is in the context of something indefinite (nakira), whereas the specific name for Allah is the definite form (al-Raḥmān); (2) the application is due to the obstinacy of the disbelievers in their disbelief, i.e., they misuse the name; (3) the line may be a rhetorical question dismissing the idea. The exact rebuttal is left open, but the key point is that the divine name al-Raḥmān in its definite form is exclusive to Allah.

تقديم
منكر
معرَّف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The name al-Rahman can be applied to humans because a poet used it for a human.
Correction: The poet's usage is in the indefinite form, while the definite form 'al-Rahman' is exclusive to Allah; also, the poet's statement may be rhetorical or due to disbelief.

B) Because al-Raḥmān is specific to Allah and not used for others.
C) Because al-Raḥmān has more letters.
D) Because al-Raḥmān is an essential attribute.

Key Takeaway
The definite name 'al-Rahman' is exclusive to Allah, and any apparent contradictory use is either indefinite, rhetorical, or from disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The poet used the indefinite form 'rahmān' carelessly. In your daily speech, do you ever use divine attributes loosely (like 'God is merciful' without reverence)? What would it mean for your taqwa to guard your tongue from trivializing Allah's names?

The Eloquence of al-Raḥmān and Three Conditions for Increased Meaning
Al-Sawi explains the second reason (ablagh) and enumerates three conditions for increased meaning from increased letters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second reason for the precedence of al-Raḥmān over al-Raḥīm (its greater eloquence) and the three conditions that must be satisfied for an increase in letters to indicate an increase in meaning in divine names.

Core Idea
Eloquence in divine names reflects the magnitude of mercy, not just the number of letters.

In this passage, Al-Sawi explains why the name al-Raḥmān is more eloquent than al-Raḥīm: it signifies the bestowal of great bounties in both quantity and quality, whereas al-Raḥīm denotes smaller favors. He then introduces a key linguistic principle—that an increase in letters can indicate an increase in meaning—but only under three precise conditions. Understanding these conditions helps us appreciate how kalam uses linguistic reasoning to interpret divine attributes accurately.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's reasoning unfold naturally.
2
Identify the second reason (ablagh)
Locate where Al-Sawi states that al-Raḥmān is more eloquent. Understand that this eloquence is defined by bestowing great bounties (jalā'il al-ni'am) in quantity and quality.
Compare with al-Raḥīm, which bestows small bounties (daqā'iq).
3
Examine the three conditions
The principle 'increase in letters = increase in meaning' has three qualifications. Find each condition and its counterexample: (1) not innate attributes, (2) same type, (3) same derivation.
Take note of why examples like shariḥun and ḥadhirun fail the conditions.
4
Reflect on correct application
Consider how al-Raḥmān and al-Raḥīm satisfy all three conditions, so the increased letters of al-Raḥmān legitimately convey greater meaning.
This guards against misapplying the rule to other word pairs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأنَّه أبلغ، إذ معناه : المنعم بجلائل النعم كمّاً وكيفاً، بخلاف (الرحيم) فإنَّ معناه : المُنعِم بدقائقها كذلك، وجلائلُ النّعَم أصولها ؛ كالوجود ،والإيمان والعافية، والرّزق، والعقل، والسمع والبصر، وغير ذلك. قوله : (ولأنَّه أبلغ) معطوف على قوله : (لأنه خاص) : أي : فقدمه لأمرين . وقوله : (إذ معناه) : تعليل لأبلغيته . قوله : (كذلك) : أي : كمّاً ،وكيفاً، وهذا المعنى أشهر التفاسير وحجتهم في ذلك اختصاصه بالله تعالى وكون زيادة البناء تدلُّ على زيادة المعنى بشروط ثلاثة : أن يكون ذلك في غير الصفات الجبلية، فخرج نحو : (شره ونهم). وأن يتحد اللفظان في النَّوع ، فخرج نحو : (حذر وحاذر)، فالأول مع قلة حروفه أبلغ من الثاني؛ لكونه صفة مشبَّهة . وأن يتَّحدا في الاشتقاق، فخرج نحو (زمن وزمان)، فالمستوفي للشروط كرحمن ورحيم، وقطع وقطع . قوله : (وغير ذلك) : أي : كالشَّمٌ ، والذَّوق، واللمس، والنَّجاة من
And because He is more eloquent, for its meaning is: the One who bestows great bounties in quantity and quality, unlike (al-Raḥīm) for its meaning is: the One who bestows small bounties likewise. And the great bounties are their origins – like existence, faith, well-being, provision, intellect, hearing, sight, and other such things. His statement: '(and because He is more eloquent)' is conjoined to His statement: '(because He is specific)', meaning: so He prefixed it for two reasons. And His statement: '(for its meaning is)' is an explanation of its eloquence. His statement: '(likewise)' means: in quantity and quality, and this meaning is the most famous of the interpretations. And their proof for that is its specificity to Allah – may He be exalted – and that increased construction indicates increased meaning under three conditions: that it does not concern innate attributes, so excluded are examples like sharihun (greedy) and nahamun (rapacious); and that the two words are united in type, so excluded are examples like ḥadhirun (cautious) and ḥādhirun (on guard), for the first with fewer letters is more eloquent than the second because it is a resembling attribute; and that they are united in derivation, so excluded are examples like zamanun (time) and zamānun (time). So what fulfills the conditions is like Raḥmān and Raḥīm, and qaṭi‘ and qaṭi‘ (cut). His statement: '(and other such things)' means: like smell, taste, touch, and deliverance from...
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the second reason for prefixing al-Raḥmān over al-Raḥīm, namely that it is more eloquent (ablagh). Eloquence here means that the name al-Raḥmān conveys the bestowal of great bounties (jalā'il al-ni‘am) in both quantity and quality, whereas al-Raḥīm conveys the bestowal of small bounties (daqā'iq). The great bounties are the foundations: existence, faith, well-being, provision, intellect, hearing, sight, etc. The commentator then explains that the proof for this interpretation lies in the principle that an increase in the number of letters indicates an increase in meaning – but this principle is qualified by three conditions: (1) The words must not refer to innate or natural qualities (al-ṣifāt al-jibaliyya), so examples like 'sharihun' (greedy) and 'nahamun' (rapacious) are excluded because their longer forms do not intensify the meaning. (2) The two words must be of the same type (morphological pattern), so 'ḥadhirun' (cautious) and 'ḥādhirun' (on guard) are excluded because the shorter form (ḥadhirun) is actually more eloquent due to being a resembling adjective. (3) The two words must share the same derivation, so 'zamanun' and 'zamānun' (both meaning time) are excluded because they are from different roots. The names al-Raḥmān and al-Raḥīm meet all three conditions, so the increased letters of al-Raḥmān correspond to its greater meaning. The passage ends with a list of other bounties cut off at 'deliverance from'...

Key Terms
أبلغ
زيادة البناء
زيادة المعنى
صفات جبلية
اتحاد النوع
اتحاد الاشتقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Longer words always have stronger meaning than shorter ones.
Correction: The principle 'increase in letters = increase in meaning' only applies when three conditions are met: the words are not innate attributes, are of the same type, and share the same derivation. Otherwise, as shown in counterexamples, the shorter word may be more eloquent.

Checkpoint
What is the second reason given in this passage for the preference of 'al-Raḥmān' over 'al-Raḥīm'?
A) Because it is specific to Allah.
B) Because it is more eloquent (ablagh).
C) Because it has more letters.
D) Because it indicates essential attributes.

Key Takeaway
Al-Raḥmān is more eloquent than al-Raḥīm because it conveys great bounties, and the principle that longer words imply stronger meaning is valid only under precise conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah as al-Raḥmān, do you truly recognize that He bestows the greatest blessings like faith and intellect upon you? How has your awareness of these great bounties affected your gratitude today?

The condition that increased letters only indicate increased meaning when not about innate attributes challenges our assumptions. In what areas of your own understanding of Allah's names might you be making assumptions that the lesson corrects? Will you revisit your understanding of any name with this principle in mind?

How often do you limit your experience of Allah's mercy to small favors, forgetting the foundational blessings like existence and faith? The Distinction Between Rahman and Raheem
Al-Sawi distinguishes Rahman and Raheem by the level of blessings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the divine names al-Rahman and al-Raheem based on the type of blessings they denote.

Core Idea
Rahman denotes great blessings, Raheem denotes subtle ones.

In the basmala, Allah introduces Himself as both al-Rahman and al-Raheem. While both names derive from mercy, classical scholars have distinguished them by the scale and scope of the blessings. In this passage, al-Sawi explains that al-Rahman signifies the bestowal of great blessings (jalil al-ni'am) while al-Raheem signifies the bestowal of subtle blessings (daqiq al-ni'am). This distinction helps us appreciate the depth of Allah's mercy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the distinction
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the basic distinction between Rahman and Raheem.
2
Identify the two categories of blessings
Great blessings are roots (usul) and subtle blessings are branches (furu'). List examples from the text.
Great blessings include existence, faith, well-being, etc. Subtle ones include beauty, increase of faith, etc.
3
Understand the reason for the distinction
Note that the author also clarifies that the names are eternal, even though described by these acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معنى البسملة
ولأنَّه أبلغ، إذ معناه : المنعم بجلائل النعم كمّاً وكيفاً، بخلاف (الرحيم) فإنَّ معناه : المُنعِم بدقائقها كذلك، وجلائلُ النّعَم أصولها ؛ كالوجود ،والإيمان والعافية، والرّزق، والعقل، والسمع والبصر، وغير ذلك، ...
وأنت غيث الورى لا زلت رحماناً في حق مسيلمة الكذاب فشاةٌ، أو لأنَّه منكر، والخاص بالله المعرَّف، أو من تعنّتهم في كفرهم.
قوله : (ولأنَّه أبلغ) معطوف على قوله : (لأنه خاص) : أي : فقدمه لأمرين .
وقوله : (إذ معناه) : تعليل لأبلغيته .
قوله : (كذلك) : أي : كمّاً ،وكيفاً، وهذا المعنى أشهر التفاسير وحجتهم في ذلك اختصاصه بالله تعالى وكون زيادة البناء تدلُّ على زيادة المعنى بشروط ثلاثة :
أن يكون ذلك في غير الصفات الجبلية، فخرج نحو : (شره ونهم). وأن يتحد اللفظان في النَّوع ، فخرج نحو : (حذر وحاذر)، فالأول مع قلة حروفه أبلغ من الثاني؛ لكونه صفة مشبَّهة .
وأن يتَّحدا في الاشتقاق، فخرج نحو (زمن وزمان)، فالمستوفي للشروط كرحمن ورحيم، وقطع وقطع .
قوله : (وغير ذلك) : أي : كالشَّمٌ ، والذَّوق، واللمس، والنَّجاة من النَّار، ودخول الجنة.
ودقائقها فروعها ؛ كالجمال، وكثرة وزيادة الإيمان، ووفور العافية، وسَعَة الرزق ودقة العقل، وحِدَّة السَّمع والبصر وغير ذلك . والمعنى: أنَّه تعالى من حيث إنَّه مُنعم بجلائل النِّعَم يسمَّى الرَّحمن ، ومن حيث إنَّه مُنعم بدقائقها يسمَّى الرَّحيم .
قوله : (يسمَّى الرَّحمن) : أي : استدلَّ بها على اسمه الرَّحمن، وكذا يقال في قوله : (يسمى الرَّحيم)، وإلا فأسماؤه تعالى وأوصافه أزليَّة قديمة .
The meaning of the basmala. And because it is more comprehensive, for its meaning is: The Bestower of great blessings in quantity and quality, unlike (al-Raheem) whose meaning is: The Bestower of subtle ones likewise. Great blessings are their roots, such as existence, faith, well-being, provision, intellect, hearing, sight, and other things... And you are the rain of the people, may you remain a mercy [Rahman] upon the liar Musaylimah? A sheep? Or because it is indefinite, and the specific [name] with God is the definite, or because of their stubbornness in disbelief. His saying: (And because it is more comprehensive) is coordinated with his saying: (Because it is specific): i.e., he gave priority for two reasons. His saying: (for its meaning) is the causality for its comprehensiveness. His saying: (likewise): i.e., in quantity and quality. This meaning is the most famous of the interpretations, and their proof for that is its exclusivity to God Almighty and that increase in structure indicates increase in meaning, under three conditions: That it be not in innate attributes, thus excluded are like (sharih and naham). And that the two words be of the same type, thus excluded are like (hadhir and hadhir), for the first with fewer letters is more comprehensive than the second because it is an adjective of resemblance. And that they share the same derivation, thus excluded are like (zaman and zaman). What fulfills the conditions is like Rahman and Raheem, and qata'a and qatta'a. His saying: (and other things): i.e., like smell, taste, touch, salvation from the Fire, and entry into Paradise. And their subtle blessings are their branches, like beauty, abundance and increase of faith, abundance of well-being, breadth of provision, sharpness of intellect, keenness of hearing and sight, and other things. And the meaning: That He, exalted, is called al-Rahman from the perspective that He is the Bestower of great blessings, and is called al-Raheem from the perspective that He is the Bestower of subtle ones. His saying: (He is called al-Rahman): i.e., he used it as evidence for His name al-Rahman, and likewise is said in his saying: (He is called al-Raheem). Otherwise, His names and attributes are eternal and ancient.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the meanings of the divine names 'Rahman' and 'Raheem' as they appear in the basmala. The author first states that 'Rahman' is more comprehensive because it signifies the bestowal of great blessings (jalil al-ni'am) in both quantity and quality, while 'Raheem' signifies the bestowal of subtle blessings (daqiq al-ni'am). Great blessings are the roots (usul) such as existence, faith, well-being, provision, intellect, hearing, sight, and others. A poetic line is cited as an example or rhetorical flourish, though its exact meaning is unclear due to OCR issues. The commentary then breaks down the original author's phrase 'and because it is more comprehensive' (wa li-annahu ablagh), explaining that this is the second reason for prioritizing 'Rahman' over 'Raheem' in the basmala, after the reason of specificity. The phrase 'for its meaning' (idh ma'nahu) provides the cause for its comprehensiveness. The author then discusses the famous interpretation that 'Rahman' is exclusive to God and that the increase in morphological structure indicates an increase in meaning, subject to three conditions: (1) the forms must not be from innate or natural attributes (e.g., sharih and naham, meaning 'greedy' etc.), (2) the two words must be of the same type (e.g., hadhir and hadhir, where the shorter form is actually more intensive due to being a different type), and (3) they must share the same derivation (e.g., zaman and zaman are different derivations). The perfect example fulfilling all conditions is 'Rahman' and 'Raheem'. The commentary then expands on the phrase 'and other things' to include sensory blessings like smell, taste, touch, and the ultimate blessings of salvation from Hell and entry into Paradise. It continues to elaborate that subtle blessings are branches of the great ones, listing beauty, increase of faith, well-being, provision, sharpness of intellect, etc. Finally, it summarizes that God is called 'Rahman' with respect to bestowing great blessings and 'Raheem' with respect to bestowing subtle ones. A clarifying remark notes that the author cited these descriptive phrases as evidence for the divine names, but this does not imply the names are acquired; rather, God's names and attributes are eternal and ancient (azaliyyah qadimah).

Key Terms
الرحمن
الرحيم
جلائل النعم
دقائق النعم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rahman and Raheem are synonyms with no difference in meaning.
Correction: Rahman denotes bestowal of great blessings, Raheem denotes bestowal of subtle blessings. They are not synonyms.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary distinction between the names 'Rahman' and 'Raheem'?
A) Rahman is specific to God, Raheem is not.
B) Rahman denotes great blessings, Raheem denotes subtle blessings.
C) Rahman is used only in the basmala, Raheem is used elsewhere.
D) Rahman is more intensive in form but not in meaning.

Key Takeaway
Rahman refers to Allah's bestowal of great blessings, while Raheem refers to His bestowal of subtle blessings, both aspects of His infinite mercy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your life, which subtle blessings (like beauty or increased faith) have you been taking for granted? How can you actively thank Allah for them today?
?
The lesson says Ar-Rahman gives great blessings like existence and intellect. Have you ever truly pondered the gift of your own mind and its capacity to know Allah? What would change if you thanked Him for that every morning?
?
If Allah's mercy encompasses both great and subtle blessings, then there is no small favor from Him. What 'small' thing in your life right now are you neglecting to be grateful for? Write it down and commit to praising Allah for it specifically.

Three Conditions for Intensive Forms
Al-Sawi lists three conditions for the principle that increased form indicates increased meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three conditions required for an increase in morphological structure to indicate an increase in meaning, as exemplified by Rahman and Raheem.

Core Idea
Increase in structure indicates increase in meaning only under specific conditions.

The principle that 'increase in structure indicates increase in meaning' (زيادة البناء تدل على زيادة المعنى) is a key linguistic rule in understanding divine names. However, it is not automatic; al-Sawi outlines three essential conditions that must be met. These conditions are illustrated with examples from Arabic morphology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on conditions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation for the three conditions.
2
List each condition
Write down the three conditions: not in innate attributes, same type, same derivation. Note the examples for each.
Condition 1 excludes sharih and naham. Condition 2 excludes hadhir and hadhir. Condition 3 excludes zaman and zaman.
3
Apply to Rahman and Raheem
See why Rahman and Raheem are the perfect example fulfilling all conditions.
Also qata'a and qatta'a are mentioned as fulfilling the conditions.

The Divine Names al-Rahman and al-Rahim: Great and Subtle Blessings
Commentary on the divine names al-Rahman and al-Rahim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific distinction between the names al-Rahman and al-Rahim based on the scope of divine blessings.

Core Idea
Al-Rahman refers to great blessings, al-Rahim to subtle ones.

This passage explains how two of Allah's most beautiful names, al-Rahman and al-Rahim, while both rooted in mercy, are distinguished by the type of blessing they emphasize. This insight deepens our understanding of divine attributes and their manifestations in creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get an overview of the content.
Pay attention to the examples of subtle blessings listed.
2
Identify the distinction between the two names
Look for the sentence that defines al-Rahman as the Bestower of great blessings and al-Rahim as the Bestower of subtle blessings.
This is the core teaching of the passage.
3
Examine the linguistic notes
Notice the discussion on the word 'yaqulu' and the idafah construction 'raji rahmah'. Understand that the idafah is not obligatory.
The commentary clarifies that the meaning remains the same even if the construction changes.
4
Understand the explanation of hope and mercy
Read the gloss on 'raji' as hoping with means, and the interpretation of mercy as the attribute of action.
The author chose the more concise meaning.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why the distinction matters for understanding divine attributes and how it avoids the misconception that the names are synonymous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودقائقها فروعها ؛ كالجمال، وكثرة وزيادة الإيمان، ووفور العافية، وسَعَة الرزق ودقة العقل، وحِدَّة السَّمع والبصر وغير ذلك . والمعنى: أنَّه تعالى من حيث إنَّه مُنعم بجلائل النِّعَم يسمَّى الرَّحمن ، ومن حيث إنَّه مُنعم بدقائقها يسمَّى الرَّحيم . فخفِّف - (يقول) هو من باب نصر، فأصله يَقْول - بسكون فائه وضمّ عينه بنقل حركة العين إلى الفاء (راجي رحمة) بإضافة الوصف إلى معموله؛ أي: المؤمِّل المنتظر إنعام (القدير) النَّار، ودخول الجنة. قوله : (يسمَّى الرَّحمن) : أي : استدلَّ بها على اسمه الرَّحمن، وكذا يقال في قوله : (يسمى الرَّحيم)، وإلا فأسماؤه تعالى وأوصافه أزليَّة قديمة . قوله : (بإضافة الوصف إلى معموله الوصف هو قوله: (راجي) والمعمول قوله : (رحمة) وليست الإضافة متعيَّنة، بل يجوز تنوين (راجي) ونصب (رحمة) ولا يتغيّر الوزن ولا المعنى . قوله : (أي : المؤمل ... إلخ ) : تفسير لـ(الرَّاجي)؛ لأنَّ الرَّجاء هو الأمل مع الأخذ في الأسباب. قوله : (إنعام): تفسير للرَّحمة، فالمراد منها صفة الفعل، ويصح أن يراد منها هنا إرادة الإنعام أيضاً ؛ لأنَّه يلزم من إرادة الإنعام حصوله لا راد لما قضى وإنَّما اختار المعنى الأوّل ; لكونه أخصر .
And their subtleties are their branches, such as beauty, abundance and increase of faith, ample well-being, breadth of provision, acuteness of intellect, sharpness of hearing and sight, and so forth. The meaning: that He, exalted, insofar as He is the Bestower of great blessings, is called al-Rahman, and insofar as He is the Bestower of subtle blessings, is called al-Rahim. So lighten - (yaqulu) is from the paradigm nasara, so its original is yaqulu - with sukun on its fa and dammah on its ain, transferring the vowel of the ain to the fa. (raji rahmah) with the idafah of the descriptor to its object; i.e., the one hoping, expecting the bestowal of (al-Qadir) [al-Nar?], and entering Paradise. His statement: (He is called al-Rahman): i.e., he inferred from it His name al-Rahman, and similarly it is said in His statement: (He is called al-Rahim), otherwise His names and attributes are eternal and pre-eternal. His statement: (with the idafah of the descriptor to its object) - the descriptor is his word (raji) and the object is his word (rahmah); and the idafah is not obligatory, rather tanwin of (raji) and nasb of (rahmah) are permissible, and the meter and meaning do not change. His statement: (i.e., the one hoping... etc.) is an explanation of (al-raji); because hope is expectation accompanied by taking the means. His statement: (al-in'am) is an explanation of mercy; what is meant by it is the attribute of action, and it is also valid that what is meant here is the will to bestow, because from the will to bestow follows its occurrence, there is no repeller of what He has decreed, and he only chose the first meaning because it is more concise.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic and theological commentary on the terms "al-Rahman" and "al-Rahim" from the original text. It begins by listing examples of subtle blessings (dawa'iq) as branches of the great blessings, such as beauty, increase of faith, well-being, etc. Then it defines the two names: al-Rahman refers to God as the Bestower of great blessings, while al-Rahim refers to Him as the Bestower of subtle blessings. Next, the commentary addresses the word "yaqulu" (he says), giving its root and vowel pattern. It then explains the construction "raji rahmah" (hoping for mercy) as an idafah (construct phrase) where the descriptor is "raji" and the object is "rahmah"; it notes that the idafah is not fixed—one could use tanwin and nasb instead without changing meaning. The commentary then glosses "al-raji" as "the one hoping" emphasizing that hope involves taking the means (al-asbab). Finally, it explains "in'am" (bestowal) as the intended meaning of "rahmah" (mercy), i.e., the attribute of action (sifat al-fi'l), and notes that it could also mean the will to bestow, but the author chose the former as more concise. The overall point is to clarify the distinction between the two names and the precise meanings of related terms.

الرحمن
الرحيم
الراجي
الرحمة
إضافة الوصف إلى معموله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think al-Rahman and al-Rahim are synonyms with no difference.
Correction: The passage shows they refer to different aspects of divine grace: al-Rahman for great blessings, al-Rahim for subtle blessings.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between the names al-Rahman and al-Rahim?
A) They are synonyms with no difference.
B) Al-Rahman refers to great blessings, al-Rahim to subtle blessings.
C) Al-Rahman is for mercy, al-Rahim for severity.
D) They refer to the same attribute.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between al-Rahman and al-Rahim lies in the scale of blessings: al-Rahman for great, al-Rahim for subtle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you recently experienced a 'subtle blessing' like acuteness of intellect or sharp hearing? Did you thank Allah for it specifically, or do you tend to overlook such gifts?
?
The passage distinguishes between hoping and taking the means. Are you combining sincere hope with active effort in seeking Allah's mercy, or are you passive? What one area of your life needs this balance right now?
?
If Allah's names are eternal, what does it mean for you that He is always al-Rahman and al-Rahim, even when you are not experiencing obvious blessings? How should that change your trust in Him today?

The Divine Name al-Qadir: Meaning and Grammatical Nuances
Commentary on the divine name al-Qadir, exploring its two possible grammatical forms and clarifying the concept of multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the divine name al-Qadir and the grammatical and theological significance of its interpretation as a descriptive adjective versus an intensive form.

Core Idea
Al-Qadir means the One with permanent power, but its intensive form highlights the vastness of that power over all creation.

This lesson examines the divine name al-Qadir, one of the beautiful names of Allah. The commentary explains that this name can be understood as describing Allah's eternal power or as emphasizing the extensiveness of that power through its attachments to all possible things. Understanding these nuances helps us grasp both the unity of Allah's attribute of power and its universal application.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the structure of the commentary sink in.
Notice the two possible interpretations introduced at the beginning.
2
Identify the two grammatical interpretations
Find where the passage explains 'al-Qadir' as a sifah mushabbahah (descriptive adjective) meaning 'permanent of power' and as a sighah mubalaghah (intensive form) meaning 'much power'.
The passage uses the terms 'daim al-qudra' and 'kathir al-qudra'.
3
Understand the clarification about multiplicity
Read the part where it repels the misunderstanding that power has multiplicity. Note how it explains that multiplicity is with respect to the ability (iqtidar) and the attachments to all possible things.
This resolves the apparent contradiction between the oneness of power and its numerous effects.
4
Reflect on the intensive form and attachments
Consider the final sentence: 'thus it becomes an intensive form' with respect to the attachments. Reflect on why the intensive form is appropriate for al-Qadir.
The power is one but its objects are many, so the form emphasizes the greatness of its reach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي : دائم القدرة فهو صفة مشبَّهة، أو الكثير القدرة بمعنى الاقتدار، فيكون صيغة مبالغة . قوله : (أي: دائم القدرة : فالقدير من أسمائه تعالى، ومعناه: ذو القدرة الدائمة. قوله : (بمعنى الاقتدار دفع به ما يراد من أنَّ القدرة واحدة لا تعدد فيها، وإيضاحه أنَّ الكثرة باعتبار الاقتدار ؛ وهو عموم تعلق القدرة بسائر الممكنات . قوله : ( فيكون صيغة مبالغة) : أي : باعتبار التعلقات.
Meaning: permanent of power, so it is a descriptive adjective (sifah mushabbahah), or the one of much power in the sense of ability (iqtidar), thus it becomes an intensive form (sighah mubalaghah). His statement: (i.e., permanent of power: for al-Qadir is among His names, exalted, and its meaning is the possessor of permanent power. His statement: (in the sense of ability) repels what might be understood, namely that power is one with no multiplicity in it, and its clarification is that multiplicity is with respect to ability; and it is the universal attachment of power to all possible things. His statement: (thus it becomes an intensive form): i.e., with respect to the attachments.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and theological commentary on the divine name "al-Qadir" (the All-Powerful). It begins by explaining that "al-Qadir" can be interpreted as either a descriptive adjective (sifah mushabbahah) meaning "one who has permanent power," or as an intensive form (sighah mubalaghah) meaning "one of great power" in the sense of ability (iqtidar). The commentary then clarifies that the meaning "permanent of power" is the intended one: al-Qadir is a name of God meaning the possessor of eternal power. Next, it addresses a potential misunderstanding: since God's power (qudrah) is one attribute, it might be thought that it does not have multiplicity. The response is that the multiplicity is with respect to the ability (iqtidar), i.e., the universal attachment of that single power to all possible things (mumkinat). Finally, it reiterates that the intensive form is understood with regard to the attachments (ta'alliqat), meaning that although the power itself is one, its effects on countless objects are many.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Understanding 'kathir al-qudra' (much power) as meaning multiple powers or multiplicity within the attribute of power itself.
Correction: The multiplicity is in the objects of power (iqtidar), not in the attribute. Allah's power is one but attaches to everything possible.

Checkpoint

D) It is a synonym for 'daim al-qudra'.

Key Takeaway
The divine name al-Qadir emphasizes Allah's eternal power and its universal scope, with the intensive form highlighting the multiplicity of its effects rather than the attribute itself.

Meaning of the Divine Name al-Qadir: Descriptive or Exaggerative
The commentator defines the meaning of al-Qadir as a divine name.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible meanings of the divine name al-Qadir and the grammatical reasoning behind each interpretation.

Core Idea
Al-Qadir means either 'eternal in power' or 'abundant in power'—a key distinction in Islamic theology.

In this passage, the commentator explains the divine name al-Qadir. At first glance, it might seem straightforward, but there is a subtle grammatical and theological nuance. Does it describe a single, perpetual attribute, or does it indicate a multiplicity of power that attaches to all possible things? The passage lays out both possibilities and clarifies why the form is considered exaggerative.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the flow of the commentator's explanation.
Notice the two interpretations introduced at the beginning.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Note that al-Qadir is explained as either 'daim al-qudra' (eternal in power) or 'kathir al-qudra' (abundant in power).
3
Understand the grammatical forms
Distinguish between 'sifa mushabbaha' (descriptive attribute) and 'sigha mubalagha' (exaggerative form).
The form fa‘il can be either; context determines which.
4
Grasp the theological reasoning
The commentator explains that the multiplicity is not in power itself but in its attachments (ta‘alluqat) to all possible things.
This refutes the misconception that divine power is one and cannot have multiple relations.
5
Check your understanding
Use the checkpoint question to test your grasp of the main point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمة الإمام الدردير (79) أي : دائم القدرة فهو صفة مشبَّهة، أو الكثير القدرة بمعنى الاقتدار، فيكون صيغة مبالغة . قوله : (أي: دائم القدرة : فالقدير من أسمائه تعالى، ومعناه: ذو القدرة الدائمة. قوله : (بمعنى الاقتدار دفع به ما يراد من أنَّ القدرة واحدة لا تعدد فيها، وإيضاحه أنَّ الكثرة باعتبار الاقتدار ؛ وهو عموم تعلق القدرة بسائر الممكنات . قوله : ( فيكون صيغة مبالغة) : أي : باعتبار التعلقات.
Translation of Imam al-Dardir (79): That is, eternal in power, so it is a descriptive attribute, or abundant in power in the sense of potency, thus it becomes an exaggerative form. His saying: (That is, eternal in power: for al-Qadir is among His names, Most High, and its meaning is: possessor of perpetual power. His saying: (In the sense of potency) he repels thereby what might be understood, that power is one with no multiplicity in it, and its clarification is that multiplicity is with respect to potency; and it is the universal attachment of power to all possible things. His saying: (Thus it becomes an exaggerative form): that is, with respect to attachments.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage defines the divine name al-Qadir, explaining its meaning and grammatical form. The author begins by stating that al-Qadir means 'daim al-qudra' (eternal in power), so it is a 'sifa mushabbaha' (descriptive adjective). Alternatively, it could mean 'kathir al-qudra' (abundant in power) as a form of exaggeration, i.e., 'sigha mubalagha'. He then comments on the phrase 'daim al-qudra' from the base text, clarifying that al-Qadir is one of Allah's names and means 'possessor of perpetual power'. Next, he discusses the interpretation 'in the sense of potency' (bi-ma'na al-iqtidar), explaining that this usage prevents the misunderstanding that Allah's power is one and has no multiplicity. Rather, multiplicity applies to the attachments (ta'allaqat) of power to all possible beings, as power relates universally to everything possible. Finally, he confirms that the form 'Fa'il' here is an exaggerative pattern due to the multiplicity of attachments, not because power itself is multiple.
