The Linguistic Derivation of 'al-Aliyy'
The author explains the linguistic derivation of 'al-Aliyy' from 'uluww' and the morphological transformation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic derivation of the divine name 'al-Aliyy' and the exceptional morphological rule involved in its formation.

Core Idea
The name 'al-Aliyy' comes from 'uluww' with a rule-breaking but light-favored transformation.

This lesson examines how the divine attribute 'al-Aliyy' is linguistically derived from the root meaning of elevation. The author explains the steps of morphological derivation and notes a special phonetic rule that prioritizes lightness over the standard pattern. Understanding this derivation deepens appreciation for the precision of Arabic in expressing divine names.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Pay attention to the technical morphological terms.
2
Identify the root and transformation
Note that the root is 'aliy (عليو) and that the waw is turned into a ya and assimilated. Understand the phonetic context.
3
Understand the rule and its exception
The commentator states that this transformation is against the usual rule. The rule is that the assimilated letter conforms to the assimilating letter. Here, because ya is lighter, the waw is changed.
Compare with the example of 'sayyid' mentioned.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this linguistic detail shows the precision of Arabic in divine names and that even morphological rules can have exceptions for phonetic ease.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(العليّ) من العُلُوّ، وهو الرّفعة، فأصله: عليو، اجتمعت الياء والواو، وسبقت إحداهما بالسكون. قوله : (فأصله) : مفرّع على قوله : (من العلو) أي: فلامه واو. قوله : (عليو) : بفتح العين وكسر اللام، وسكون الياء. فقلبت الواو ياء، وأدغمت فيها الياء. قوله: (فقلبت الواو ياء ... إلخ) : هذا على خلاف القاعدة، بل القاعدة أنَّ المدغم هو الذي يقلب ويردُّ من جنس المدغم فيه، لكن لما كانت الياء أخفَّ من الواو قلبت الواو ياء وأدغمت في الياء، وتقدَّم نظيره في تصريف سيد .
(Al-‘Aliyy) is from al-‘uluww, which is elevation. Its root is ‘aliy, the ya and waw came together, and one of them was preceded by a sukun. He said: (its root) is derived from his saying 'from al-‘uluww', meaning its lam (final letter) is waw. He said: (عليو) with fath on the ‘ayn, kasr on the lam, and sukun on the ya. So the waw was turned into a ya and the ya was assimilated into it. He said: (so the waw was turned into a ya ... etc.) This is against the rule. The rule is that the assimilated letter is the one turned and made to conform to the assimilating letter. But because the ya is lighter than the waw, the waw was turned into a ya and assimilated into it. Its analog has preceded in the conjugation of 'sayyid'.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic explanation of the derivation of the divine name 'al-‘Aliyy'. The author first states that the name is derived from 'al-‘uluww' meaning elevation, and its root is ‘aliy (عليو). He then explains the morphological transformation: the letters ya and waw came together, and one of them (the waw) is preceded by a sukun. According to Arabic phonetic rules, the waw is turned into a ya and then the ya is assimilated (idgham). The commentator notes that this transformation is contrary to the usual rule, which dictates that the assimilated letter (here the ya, because it is the one coming after the waw? Actually, the rule is that the letter being assimilated is turned to match the assimilating letter; here, since the ya is assimilating, the waw should have been kept? The commentator clarifies: the usual rule is that the consonant that disappears (the one assimilated) is turned into the sound of the consonant that remains. In this case, the waw is turned into ya and then the ya is assimilated, so the waw is actually the one changed. This is because ya is lighter (akhaff) than waw, so it was preferable to preserve the ya and change the waw. This also occurs in the derivation of 'sayyid' from *sawyid. The passage provides an example of the precision of Arabic morphology in the names of Allah.

القاعدة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The transformation of waw to ya in 'al-Aliyy' is arbitrary.
Correction: It follows a rule prioritizing lighter sound (ya) over standard pattern.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

The author emphasizes that this derivation is not arbitrary but motivated by ease and harmony. What would change if you paused to internalize its meaning every time you say it?

The Spiritual Meaning of Allah's Transcendence and Oneness
The author defines the spiritual meaning of Allah's 'uluww and al-Wahid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the spiritual meaning of Allah's transcendence (al-‘uluww) and His oneness (al-Wahid), and why these attributes entail negation of all imperfection and any partner.

Core Idea
Allah's vastness is spiritual, not physical, and His oneness excludes any partner in essence, attributes, or actions.

In this passage, the author unpacks the meanings of two key divine names: al-‘Aliyy and al-Wahid. He clarifies that Allah’s transcendence is not about physical height but about being completely above any deficiency. This leads directly to the meaning of oneness as the negation of any partner. Understanding these terms correctly protects against both anthropomorphism and polytheism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage includes both the original matn and a commentary (sharh). The commentary explains key phrases.
2
Understand the two definitions of 'uluww
The passage presents two interpretations of Allah's transcendence: one focusing only on negation of deficiency, and another that also includes affirmation of perfection. Identify both and see how they differ.
The second interpretation makes 'al-‘Aliyy' an all-encompassing name.
3
Understand the definition of al-Wahid
The name 'al-Wahid' is defined as the One transcendent above a partner in essence, attributes, and actions. Note that this oneness is absolute and not just numerical.
The commentary adds that oneness negates the five well-known types of multiplicity (al-kumum al-khams).
4
Connect the two names
The author says that al-Wahid is a result (natiija) of the meaning of al-‘Aliyy. Reflect on why this logical connection makes sense: because true transcendence implies uniqueness and exclusivity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معنى العلو. وعلوه تعالى معنوي عبارة عن تنزيهه تعالى عن كلِّ نَقْص، فيتضمن اتصافه تعالى بجميع صفات السلوب. ولك أن تقول : علوه تعالى عبارةٌ عن تنزيهه عن كلِّ نَقْص واتصافه بكلِّ كمال، فيشمل صفات المعاني أيضاً. (الواحد) أي : المنزَّه عن الشَّريك في الذات والصفات والأفعال. قوله : (وعلوه تعالى معنوي) : أي : لا حسّي؛ لاستحالته عليه تعالى. قوله : (عبارة) : أي : لفظ يعبّر به ويدلُّ به على أنَّه تعالى منزّه. قوله : (فيتضمن) : أي : فالعلي يتضمن ... إلخ . قوله : (بجميع صفات السُّلوب): جمع سلب بمعنى : نفى. قوله : (ولك أن تقول : أي : في معنى العليّ، وهو بهذا المعنى من الأسماء الجامعة. قوله : (الواحد) : ذكر الواحد وما بعده نتيجة معنى العلي. قوله : (المنزّه عن الشَّريك) : أي : ففي معنى الوحدانية نفي الكموم الخمسة المشهورة .
The meaning of al-‘uluww. And His, exalted, ‘uluww is spiritual, an expression for His transcendence above every deficiency, so it entails His being described with all attributes of negation. And you may say: His ‘uluww is an expression for His transcendence above every deficiency and His being described with every perfection, so it encompasses the attributes of meanings as well. (al-Wahid) meaning: the one who is transcendent above a partner in essence, attributes, and actions. He said: (and His ‘uluww is spiritual) meaning: not physical, because of its impossibility for Him. He said: (expression) meaning: a word by which one expresses and indicates that He, exalted, is transcendent. He said: (so it entails) meaning: so al-‘Aliyy entails ... etc. He said: (with all attributes of negation) the plural of salb meaning negation. He said: (and you may say) meaning: concerning the meaning of al-‘Aliyy, and it is in this meaning from the all-encompassing names. He said: (al-Wahid) it mentions al-Wahid and what follows it as a result of the meaning of al-‘Aliyy. He said: (the one transcendent above a partner) meaning: so in the meaning of oneness there is negation of the five well-known quants.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a theological definition of two central divine names. First, the author explains that 'uluww is not physical but spiritual (ma'nawi), meaning that Allah is above all deficiency (naqs). This implies that He possesses all negative attributes (sifatal-salub). An alternative interpretation is given that includes both negative and positive aspects: transcendence above imperfection and possession of all perfection, which includes the positive attributes (sifat al-ma'ani). Then the name 'al-Wahid' is defined as the One who is transcendent above any partner in essence, attributes, and actions. The commentary clarifies that 'spiritual' means not spatial, as spatial 'uluww is impossible for Allah. It also explains that the expression 'transcendence' indicates His purity. The phrase 'all attributes of negation' refers to the plural of 'salb' meaning negation. The commentator notes that the alternative interpretation makes 'al-‘Aliyy' an all-encompassing name (ism jami'). Finally, he states that 'al-Wahid' is a consequence of the meaning of 'al-‘Aliyy' and that oneness implies negation of the five known types of multiplicity (al-kumum al-khams). This guards against anthropomorphic or polytheistic misunderstandings.

Correction: The text stresses that 'uluww is spiritual (ma'nawi) and not physical, because physical elevation is impossible for Allah. Oneness means being absolutely unique without any partner, not a numerical oneness that allows comparison.

The Derivation and First Interpretation of 'Ali
Sanusi derives 'Ali and gives first interpretation as moral transcendence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the derivation of the divine name 'Ali and the first interpretation as moral transcendence through negation.

Core Idea
The name 'Ali primarily signifies Allah's transcendence above all imperfection.

This lesson introduces the divine name al-'Ali, exploring its linguistic derivation and its theological meaning. The first interpretation focuses on 'Ali as expressing Allah's transcendence over every deficiency, which entails a set of negative attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Notice the morphological transformation of the root 'ly: 'waw turned into ya'.
The commentary explains this is for lightness.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage states that 'Ali is moral elevation (ma'nawi), not physical. Understand that moral means conceptual, not spatial.
3
Understand the meaning of moral elevation
It is an expression of being exalted above every deficiency. That is the core of the first interpretation.
4
Note the implication
This entails all attributes of negation (sifāt al-sulūb), meaning the negation of imperfections from Allah.
These attributes include, for example, that Allah is not ignorant, not weak, etc.
5
Reflect on significance
Why is this the first interpretation? It lays the foundation for understanding other divine names that affirm perfections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقلبت الواو ياء، وأدغمت فيها الياء.
وعلوه تعالى معنوي عبارة عن تنزيهه تعالى عن كلِّ نَقْص، فيتضمن اتصافه تعالى بجميع صفات السلوب.
ولك أن تقول: علوه تعالى عبارةٌ عن تنزيهه عن كلِّ نَقْص واتصافه بكلِّ كمال، فيشمل صفات المعاني أيضاً.
قوله: (فقلبت الواو ياء ... إلخ): هذا على خلاف القاعدة، بل القاعدة أنَّ المدغم هو الذي يقلب ويردُّ من جنس المدغم فيه، لكن لما كانت الياء أخفَّ من الواو قلبت الواو ياء وأدغمت في الياء، وتقدَّم نظيره في تصريف سيد.
قوله: (وعلوه تعالى معنوي): أي: لا حسّي؛ لاستحالته عليه تعالى.
قوله: (عبارة): أي: لفظ يعبّر به ويدلُّ به على أنَّه تعالى منزّه.
قوله: (فيتضمن): أي: فالعلي يتضمن ... إلخ.
قوله: (بجميع صفات السُّلوب): جمع سلب بمعنى: نفى.
قوله: (ولك أن تقول): أي في معنى العليّ، وهو بهذا المعنى من الأسماء الجامعة.
The waw was transformed into a ya and then assimilated into the ya. His elevation (ʿulūw) – exalted is He – is moral (maʿnawī), which is an expression of His being exalted above every deficiency, so it entails His being described with all attributes of negation (ṣifāt al-sulūb). It is also possible for you to say: His elevation is an expression of His being exalted above every deficiency and His being described with every perfection, so it includes the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) as well. His saying: (So the waw was transformed into a ya... etc.): This is contrary to the rule; rather the rule is that the letter which becomes the assimilated one is the one that is changed and brought into conformity with the assimilated letter, but because the ya is lighter than the waw, the waw was transformed into a ya and assimilated into the ya, and an analogous case has preceded in the declension of sayyid. His saying: (And His elevation – exalted is He – is moral) means: not physical, because that is impossible for Him. His saying: (an expression) means: a word by which it is expressed and by which it indicates that He is exalted. His saying: (so it entails) means: so al-ʿAlī entails... etc. His saying: (with all attributes of negation): the plural of salb meaning negation. His saying: (and it is possible for you to say) means: concerning the meaning of al-ʿAlī, and in this meaning it is one of the comprehensive names (al-asmāʾ al-jāmiʿa).
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage defines the divine name al-'Ali and explains its derivation and meaning. The author first notes the morphological derivation of 'Ali from the root 'ly, with the waw transformed into ya. He then gives two interpretations: first, that 'Ali signifies transcendence above all deficiency, which entails all negative attributes; second, that it also includes possession of all perfections, thus encompassing both negative and positive attributes. The commentary then clarifies each phrase: the elevation is moral, not physical; the word 'ibara means an expression indicating transcendence; 'yatadammanu' means the name entails those attributes; 'sulub' is plural of salb meaning negation. Finally, he notes that al-'Ali in the broader sense is a comprehensive name.

Key Terms
علو (ʿulūw)
معنوي (maʿnawī)
سلوب (sulūb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'Ali refers to physical height or spatial elevation.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that 'Ali is moral (ma'nawi), not physical, because physical elevation is impossible for Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the first interpretation, what does the name 'Ali entail?
A) Allah's physical elevation
B) Allah's description with all negative attributes
C) Allah's possession of all perfections
D) Allah's essence being unknowable

Key Takeaway
The name 'Ali primarily signifies Allah's transcendence above all imperfection, entailing all negative attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does knowing that 'Ali refers to moral transcendence, not physical height, change the way you imagine Allah? Have you ever caught yourself thinking of Allah in physical or spatial terms, and what can you do to replace that with the proper understanding?
?
This lesson teaches that Allah's name 'Ali entails all negative attributes – that we describe Him by what He is not. How does this affect your daily awareness of Allah? Does it increase your reverence or make you feel distant? What can you do to feel both awe and closeness?
?
If Allah is exalted above every deficiency, what deficiencies in your own character are you still holding onto? How can the name 'Ali inspire you to purify yourself and rise above moral shortcomings?

The Second Interpretation: 'Ali as a Comprehensive Name
Sanusi gives second interpretation of 'Ali as including both negation and perfection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second, more comprehensive interpretation of 'Ali, which includes both negation of deficiencies and affirmation of perfections.

Core Idea
'Ali encompasses both Allah's transcendence and His possession of all perfections.

This lesson presents an alternative, broader understanding of the name 'Ali. While the first interpretation restricted its meaning to negative attributes, this second interpretation adds that 'Ali also signifies Allah's possession of all perfections, making it a comprehensive name.

Guided Path
1
Read the second interpretation
Read the Arabic: 'wa laka an taqula: 'uluwuhu ta'ala 'ibaratun 'an tanzihihi 'an kulli naqsin wa ittisaafihi bi kulli kamaal.'
This interpretation is introduced as a possibility (lak an taqul).
2
Compare with the first interpretation
Notice that the first interpretation only mentioned 'exalted above deficiency', while the second adds 'and described with every perfection'.
This addition includes positive attributes (sifāt al-ma'ānī).
3
Understand 'comprehensive name'
The commentary says that in this meaning, 'Ali is one of the comprehensive names (al-asmā' al-jāmi'a).
Comprehensive names imply multiple attributes, both negative and positive.
4
Reflect on significance
Why might Sanusi offer this as an option? It shows that a single name can convey both transcendence and immanence.

The Divine Names al-Wahid and al-Fard: Absolute Oneness
Sanusi defines al-Wahid and al-Fard and explains their synonymy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the names al-Wahid and al-Fard affirm Allah's absolute oneness and transcendence beyond any partner in essence, attributes, and actions.

Core Idea
Allah's oneness means no partner in any way — in essence, attributes, or actions.

Sanusi introduces two of Allah's most fundamental names, al-Wahid and al-Fard. Both emphasize that Allah is utterly unique and free from any multiplicity or partnership. This short passage lays the foundation for understanding tawhid as more than numerical oneness.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definitions
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the definitions of al-Wahid and al-Fard. Pay attention to the phrasing: al-Wahid is 'transcendent above partner' while al-Fard is 'unique in essence, attributes, and actions'.
The definitions are nearly synonymous, hinting at their close relationship.
2
Notice the commentary's connection
The commentary explains that al-Wahid is a result of the meaning of al-Ali. Think about how Allah's absolute highness leads to the concept of absolute oneness.
3
Reflect on the five multiplicities
The passage mentions 'the negation of the five well-known multiplicities'. Reflect on what kinds of multiplicity (numerical, categorical, etc.) are being negated by tawhid.
This deepens the understanding that oneness is not just numerical but qualitative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الواحد) أي: المنزَّه عن الشَّريك في الذات والصفات والأفعال.
(الفردِ) أي: الواحد ذاتاً وصفات وأفعالاً.
قوله: (الواحد) : ذكر الواحد وما بعده نتيجة معنى العلي.
قوله: (المنزّه عن الشَّريك) : أي: ففي معنى الوحدانية نفي الكموم الخمسة المشهورة.
قوله: (أي: الواحد ... إلخ): فيكون الفرد مرادفاً للواحد.
(al-Wāḥid) meaning: the one who is transcendent above having a partner in essence, attributes, and actions. (al-Fard) meaning: the one who is unique in essence, attributes, and actions. His saying: (al-Wāḥid) : Mentioning al-Wāḥid and what follows is a result of the meaning of al-ʿAlī. His saying: (transcendent above partner) means: for in the meaning of oneness (waḥdāniyya) is the negation of the five well-known multiplicities. His saying: (meaning: al-Wāḥid... etc.): so al-Fard is synonymous with al-Wāḥid.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage defines two divine names, al-Wāḥid and al-Fard. It states that both signify uniqueness and transcendence above any partner. The commentary explains that al-Wāḥid is a result of the meaning of al-ʿAlī, and that the negation of partner involves negating 'the five well-known multiplicities' (likely referring to numerical or categorical multiplicities). The passage then clarifies that al-Fard is synonymous with al-Wāḥid.

Key Terms
الواحد (al-Wāḥid)
الفرد (al-Fard)
الشريك (al-sharīk)
الوحدانية (al-waḥdāniyya)
الكموم الخمسة (al-kumūm al-khamsa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Wahid and al-Fard refer only to numerical oneness, not including attributes and actions.
Correction: The passage explicitly defines both names as encompassing transcendence above partner in essence, attributes, and actions, and the commentary links oneness to the negation of all five multiplicities.

Checkpoint

B) Negation of five specific multiplicities
C) Only negation of a co-equal
D) Only in essence

Key Takeaway
The names al-Wahid and al-Fard both affirm Allah's absolute uniqueness and freedom from any partner in all respects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The names al-Wāḥid and al-Fard affirm that Allah alone is unique in every way. Is there any person, goal, or desire that you have unconsciously elevated to a 'partner' in your heart? How will you dethrone it this week?
?
Since Allah has no partner in essence, attributes, or actions, does your worship reflect that? When you last prayed, were you fully present before the One and Only, or were your thoughts divided? What will you do to make your next salah more mindful of His absolute oneness?

al-ʿĀlim: The All-Encompassing Knower
Sanusi enumerates the scope of divine knowledge in three categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's attribute of knowledge encompasses all things — past, present, future, possible, impossible, and necessary — and why this is essential for a comprehensive understanding of divine omniscience.

Core Idea
Allah's knowledge covers everything, including what never happens.

Sanusi now turns to one of the most fundamental divine attributes: knowledge. The name al-ʿĀlim tells us that nothing escapes Allah's awareness. But the scope of this knowledge is even more remarkable: it includes not only existing things but also possibilities that never materialize and even logical impossibilities. This passage unfolds that scope in a concise but powerful way.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Read the Arabic sentence slowly. Notice how Sanusi divides knowledge into three categories: what will be, what will not be, and what is occurring.
The phrase 'bima ya'kūnu wa mā lā yakūnu' is a classic expression.
2
Examine the commentary breakdown
The commentary unpacks each part. Pay attention to the terms al-mustaqbalāt, al-mustaḥīlāt, al-jāʾizāt, and al-wājibāt.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: why does divine knowledge need to include impossibilities? What does this tell us about the nature of Allah's knowledge?
This guards against the misconception that God only knows actuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(العالم) بما يكون وما لا يكون وبما هو كائن؛ أي: موجود.
قوله: (العالم بما يكون) أي: المحيط علمه أزلاً بالمستقبلات.
وقوله: (وما لا يكون) أي من المستحيلات والجائزات.
وقوله: (وبما هو كائن) أي من الواجبات والجائزات.
(al-ʿĀlim) of what will be and what will not be and of what is occurring; i.e., existing. His saying: (al-ʿĀlim of what will be) means: encompassing in His knowledge eternally (azalan) the future things. His saying: (and what will not be) means: including impossibilities and possibilities (al-mustaḥīlāt wa al-jāʾizāt). His saying: (and of what is occurring) means: including necessary things (al-wājibāt) and possibilities.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage defines the divine name al-ʿĀlim (the Knower). It states that His knowledge encompasses all things: past, present, future, possible, impossible, necessary. The commentary clarifies that 'what will be' refers to future contingents, 'what will not be' includes both impossible and possible but non-existent things, and 'what is occurring' includes necessary existents and contingent existents. This shows the absolute and all-encompassing nature of divine knowledge.

Key Terms
العالم
المحيط
المستقبلات
المستحيلات
الجائزات
الواجبات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Allah only knows actual things that exist or will exist.
Correction: Sanusi shows that Allah's knowledge includes even impossibilities and non-actual possibilities.

Checkpoint
What does 'what will not be' include according to the commentary?
A) Only impossibilities
B) Only possibilities
C) Both impossibilities and possibilities
D) Only past things

Key Takeaway
Allah's knowledge encompasses all things without exception, including what never happens and what cannot happen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that Allah knows every possible mistake you could make and every possible good deed you could do, how does that affect your planning for the future? Do you rely on your own planning or seek His guidance? Commit to one change today.
?
If Allah knows what will never happen, including your worst hypothetical sins, does that give you hope or fear? How does this knowledge change the way you think about repentance? Today, ask Allah to forgive you for sins He knows you never committed but you regret even thinking about.
?
The passage says Allah knows impossibilities—things that cannot happen. Does that make you feel watched or safe? Write down one impossibility about Allah that you are certain of (e.g., He cannot have a partner) and thank Him for that.

Al-Ghanī: The Comprehensive Name of Independence
Sanusi defines al-Ghanī as absolute independence entailing all attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ghanī (the Self-Sufficient) is considered a comprehensive divine name that encompasses both negative and positive attributes.

Core Idea
God's absolute self-sufficiency includes all perfections and negates all needs.

In this lesson, we examine Sanusi's definition of al-Ghanī. This name is not merely about independence; it is a comprehensive name that implies every other attribute. Understanding this clarifies how one divine name can summarize many.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the English translation. Notice the list of things God does not need: locus, particulariser, helper, minister, etc.
The passage is dense; let the enumeration guide your understanding.
2
Understand the negations
Reflect on why Sanusi mentions these specific negations. Each one addresses a type of need that creatures have. God is free from all of them.
The commentary explains: no locus because He is self-subsisting; no particulariser because He is not contingent; no helper because He is not incapable.
3
Connect to comprehensive attributes
Note the key phrase: 'absolute richness entails His being described with all negative and perfect attributes.' This makes al-Ghanī a comprehensive name.
A comprehensive name (al-ism al-jāmiʿ) is one that implies many attributes.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp why al-Ghanī is comprehensive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الغني) عن كلِّ شيء، فلا يفتقر إلى محلّ ولا مخصص ولا معين ولا وزير ولا غير ذلك، فالغنى المطلق يتضمن اتصافه تعالى بجميع الصفات السَّلبيّة والكماليَّة.
قوله: (فلا يفتقر إلى (محل) : أي: لقيامه بنفسه، فليس صفة تقوم بمحل، ولا حادثاً يحتاج لموجد، ولا عاجزاً يفتقر لمعين، وعطف الوزير على المعين مرادف.
قوله: (ولا غير ذلك) أي من كلِّ ما يفتقر له الحوادث.
قوله: (فالغنى المطلق) : مفرّع على ما فسر به الغنى؛ أي: فالغنى في حقه مطلق، وهو يتضمن اتصافه ... إلخ، فهو من الأسماء الجامعة.
(al-Ghanī) independent of everything, so He is not in need of a locus (maḥall) nor a particulariser (mukhaṣṣiṣ) nor a helper (muʿīn) nor a minister (wazīr) nor anything else. Thus absolute richness entails His being described with all negative and perfect attributes. His saying: (so He is not in need of a locus) means: because He is self-subsisting, so He is not an attribute subsisting in a locus, nor an originated thing needing an originator, nor an incapable needing a helper, and the conjunction of 'minister' with 'helper' is synonymous. His saying: (nor anything else) means: of everything that originated things (al-ḥawādith) are in need of. His saying: (so absolute richness) is branching off from what richness was interpreted with; i.e., richness in His right is absolute, and it entails His being described... etc., so it is one of the comprehensive names (al-asmāʾ al-jāmiʿa).
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage defines al-Ghanī (the Self-Sufficient). It states that God is independent of everything, not needing a physical place, a cause for particularisation, or any assistance. This absolute independence implies that He possesses all negative attributes (absence of need) and all perfect attributes. The commentary elaborates: He is not like an accident needing a substance, not a contingent needing a creator, not weak needing a helper. The term 'wazīr' is synonymous with 'muʿīn' (helper). The phrase 'nor anything else' covers all needs of contingent things. The author concludes that al-Ghanī is a comprehensive name because it includes both negative and positive attributes.

Key Terms
الغني (al-Ghanī)
محل (maḥall)
مخصص (mukhaṣṣiṣ)
معين (muʿīn)
وزير (wazīr)
الغنى المطلق (al-ghinā al-muṭlaq)
الأسماء الجامعة (al-asmāʾ al-jāmiʿa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's self-sufficiency means He has no positive attributes, only negations.
Correction: The text explicitly states that absolute richness entails all negative and perfect attributes, so it includes both negations of need and affirmations of perfection.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why is al-Ghanī considered a comprehensive name?
A) Because it only implies negative attributes
B) Because it entails all negative and perfect attributes
C) Because it refers to wealth
D) Because it is a synonym for al-Qadir

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghanī is a comprehensive divine name that encompasses both the negation of all needs and the affirmation of all perfections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does your heart's dependence on created things (e.g., people's approval, material security) reveal a failure to truly believe that Allah is al-Ghanī, independent of everything? What is one specific attachment you need to break today because of this truth?
?
If absolute richness entails all perfections, then every good you have comes from Him alone. How does that change the way you view your achievements and your failures? Will you thank Him for every breath as a gift from the Independent One?
?
The passage says God is not in need of a helper or minister. Do you live as though you need to 'help Allah' by your deeds? How can you reconcile your duty to act with the reality that Allah is al-Ghanī, needing nothing from you?

Al-Mājid: The Generous, Abundant Giver
Sanusi interprets al-Mājid as the Generous and Abundant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Mājid is interpreted as the Generous and Abundant Giver and is classified among the beautiful names of God.

Core Idea
Al-Mājid means the Generous Giver, a name of divine beauty.

This lesson explores the divine name al-Mājid as understood in the tradition of Sunni theology. The concise passage from Sanusi gives a clear interpretation, linking it to generosity and abundance. We will examine its meaning and its classification as a name of beauty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the brevity and the interpretive nature of the statement.
2
Understand the meaning of al-Mājid
The passage interprets al-Mājid as 'the Generous, the Abundant in giving'. Reflect on the richness of this attribute.
The term 'wāsiʿ al-ʿaṭāʾ' emphasizes limitless giving.
3
Note the classification as a name of beauty
The commentary explicitly states that this makes al-Mājid one of the beautiful names (al-asmāʾ al-jamāliyya). Understand what this category implies.
Beautiful names highlight God's grace and kindness, as opposed to names of majesty.
4
Reflect on the takeaway
Consider how this understanding of al-Mājid enriches your appreciation of God's generosity and beauty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الماجدِ) قيل: معناه الكريم الواسع العطاء.
قوله: (قيل معناه الكريم ... إلخ) أي: فيكون من الأسماء الجمالية.
(al-Mājid) it is said: its meaning is the Generous, the Abundant in giving. His saying: (it is said its meaning is the Generous... etc.) means: so it is one of the beautiful names (al-asmāʾ al-jamāliyya).
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This brief passage gives one interpretation of al-Mājid as meaning 'the Generous, the Abundant in giving'. The commentary notes that this makes it one of the 'beautiful names' (al-asmāʾ al-jamāliyya), i.e., names that emphasize God's graciousness and beauty, as opposed to names of majesty (jalāliyya).

Key Terms
الماجد (al-Mājid)
الكريم (al-Karīm)
الواسع العطاء (al-wāsiʿ al-ʿaṭāʾ)
الأسماء الجمالية (al-asmāʾ al-jamāliyya)

The Divine Names: al-'Aalim, al-Fard, al-Ghani, al-Maajid
Al-Sawi explains four divine names with their implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the divine names al-'Aalim, al-Fard, al-Ghani, and al-Maajid each reflect Allah's absolute perfection and transcendence, and how they combine negative and positive attributes.

Core Idea
Allah's names each affirm perfection and negate imperfection.

In this passage, al-Sawi unpacks four names from the poem that might seem simple but reveal profound theological truths. Each name is defined not just by what it says, but by what it negates. As you read, notice how each name leads to a chain of implications that reinforce tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four names
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the translation. Notice how each name is introduced and then expanded upon.
The names are given in parentheses at the beginning.
2
Analyze al-'Aalim (The All-Knowing)
Observe how al-Sawi explains that Allah's knowledge encompasses all categories: what will be, what will not be, and what is. Understand the implications for divine omniscience.
3
Analyze al-Fard (The Unique)
See how al-Fard is synonymous with al-Wahid but emphasizes oneness in essence, attributes, and actions. Note the negations: no need for a locus, determiner, assistant, or vizier.
This shows Allah's absolute independence.
4
Analyze al-Ghani and al-Maajid
Understand that al-Ghani implies absolute self-sufficiency, encompassing both negative and perfectional attributes. For al-Maajid, note the two interpretations and how it is a result of al-Ghani.
Al-Maajid is either a name of beauty or a comprehensive name.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(العالم) بما يكون وما لا يكون وبما هو كائن؛ أي: موجود. (الفردِ) أي: الواحد ذاتاً وصفات وأفعالاً . (الغني) عن كلِّ شيء، فلا يفتقر إلى محلّ ولا مخصص ولا معين ولا وزير ولا غير ذلك، فالغنى المطلق يتضمن اتصافه تعالى بجميع الصفات السَّلبيّة والكماليَّة . (الماجدِ) قيل : معناه الكريم الواسع العطاء، قوله : (العالم بما يكون أي : المحيط علمه أزلاً بالمستقبلات. وقوله : (وما لا يكون أي من المستحيلات والجائزات. وقوله : ( وبما هو كائن أي من الواجبات والجائزات. قوله : (أي : الواحد ... إلخ) : فيكون الفرد مرادفاً للواحد. وقوله : (فلا يفتقر إلى (محل) : أي : لقيامه بنفسه، فليس صفة تقوم بمحل، ولا حادثاً يحتاج لموجد، ولا عاجزاً يفتقر لمعين، وعطف الوزير على المعين مرادف . قوله : (ولا غير ذلك أي من كلِّ ما يفتقر له الحوادث. قوله : فالغنى المطلق) : مفرّع على ما فسر به الغنى؛ أي: فالغنى في حقه مطلق، وهو يتضمن ، وهو يتضمن اتصافه ... إلخ، فهو من الأسماء الجامعة. قوله :(قيل معناه الكريم ... إلخ) أي: فيكون من الأسماء : الجمالية . وقيل : الشَّريف العظيم . قوله : (وقيل : الشَّريف ... إلخ) : أي : فيكون من الأسماء الجامعة، وعلى كل هو نتيجة الاسم الذي قبله
(The All-Knowing) of what will be and what will not be and what is; i.e., existing. (The Unique) i.e., the One in essence, attributes, and actions. (The Self-Sufficient) from everything, so He is not in need of a locus, nor a determiner, nor an assistant, nor a vizier, nor anything else. Thus absolute self-sufficiency entails His being described with all negative and perfectional attributes. (The Glorious) it is said: its meaning is the Generous, the Abundant in giving. His saying: (The All-Knowing of what will be) i.e., His knowledge encompasses the future from eternity. And His saying: (and what will not be) i.e., of impossibilities and contingencies. And His saying: (and what is) i.e., of necessities and contingencies. His saying: (i.e., the One ... etc.) so 'al-Fard' is synonymous with 'al-Wahid'. And His saying: (so He is not in need of a locus) i.e., because He subsists by Himself, so He is not an attribute subsisting in a locus, nor an event needing an originator, nor an incapable needing an assistant. And the conjunction of 'vizier' with 'assistant' is synonymous. His saying: (and nothing else) i.e., of everything that contingent things need. His saying: (so absolute self-sufficiency) is a ramification of what self-sufficiency was interpreted by; i.e., so self-sufficiency with respect to Him is absolute, and it entails, and it entails His being described ... etc., so it is among the comprehensive names. His saying: (it is said its meaning is the Generous ... etc.) i.e., so it is among the names of beauty. And it is said: the Noble, the Mighty. His saying: (and it is said: the Noble ... etc.) i.e., so it is among the comprehensive names, and in any case it is a result of the name preceding it.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of four divine names from the poem: al-'Aalim (the All-Knowing), al-Fard (the Unique), al-Ghani (the Self-Sufficient), and al-Maajid (the Glorious). The commentator, al-Sawi, defines each name and draws out its theological implications.

First, al-'Aalim: Allah knows everything—what will be, what will not be, and what is. The commentator specifies that 'what will be' refers to future events known from eternity, 'what will not be' covers impossible and contingent things, and 'what is' includes necessary and contingent existents. This shows the comprehensiveness of divine knowledge.

Second, al-Fard: This is synonymous with al-Wahid, but emphasizes oneness in essence, attributes, and actions. The commentator clarifies that being 'not in need of a locus' means Allah is not an attribute subsisting in something else, nor a contingent needing a creator, nor an incapable needing an assistant. The mention of 'vizier' reinforces the idea of no need for assistance.

Third, al-Ghani: Absolute self-sufficiency implies that Allah is free from any need for a locus, determiner, assistant, or anything else. This name encompasses both negative attributes (e.g., not needing a place) and perfectional attributes (e.g., complete independence). The commentator notes that this name is comprehensive, gathering many attributes.

Fourth, al-Maajid: Two interpretations are given: 'the Generous, the Abundant in giving' (a name of beauty) and 'the Noble, the Mighty' (a comprehensive name). The commentator concludes that both interpretations are valid, and al-Maajid is a result of the preceding name (al-Ghani), because generosity and nobility stem from self-sufficiency.

Overall, this passage shows how each divine name reflects multiple facets of tawhid, combining negative and positive attributes.

الفرد

الأسماء الجامعة
الصفات السلبية
الصفات الكمالية

Misconception: Some think 'al-Fard' refers only to oneness of essence, but it includes oneness of attributes and actions as well.
Correction: Al-Sawi explicitly says: 'al-Fard i.e., the One in essence, attributes, and actions.'

Checkpoint

D) That He is one in essence only

Key Takeaway
The four names together show that Allah is perfectly knowing, absolutely one, entirely self-sufficient, and gloriously generous and noble.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that Allah is al-'Aalim, knowing even what will not be, how does that challenge your tendency to worry about possibilities that never happen? Will you trust His knowledge and abandon that anxiety today?
?
Al-Fard means Allah is unique in essence, attributes, and actions. Where do you still seek validation, help, or power from others as if they were independent? What step will you take to redirect that reliance solely to Him?
?
Since al-Ghani is absolutely self-sufficient, what need in your life do you still try to fill through means other than Allah? Will you repent for that reliance and commit to seeking sufficiency from Him alone?

The Meaning of Latifa and Its Application to Allah
Defines 'latifa' and explains its application to Allah's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic meaning of 'latifa', its three possible senses, and why it is interpreted as Allah's knowledge of hidden matters.

Core Idea
When a word implies something unworthy of Allah, we take its necessary implication.

Sanusi now explains the term 'latifa' (subtle) used in the text to describe Allah. He unpacks its linguistic roots, distinguishes three possible literal meanings, and then shows why none are suitable for Allah. Instead, the term is understood through its necessary implication: that Allah knows the most hidden things. This lesson will guide you through the reasoning and the grammatical structure of the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the literal meaning.
Pay attention to the three possible meanings listed.
2
Understand the three literal meanings
The author explains that 'latifa' can mean small in size, thin in substance, or transparent like glass. Note how these are distinct.
These meanings come from the root 'luṭf' meaning thinness or smallness.
3
See why these meanings are not for Allah
Allah cannot have a physical body or size, so these literal meanings are impossible. The text says they are 'impossible for Allah'.
4
Learn the principle of necessary implication
When a word suggests something inappropriate for Allah, we interpret it by its necessary implication (lāzim). Here, the necessary meaning is that Allah knows hidden matters.
This principle was introduced earlier in the text.
5
Appreciate the grammatical and stylistic points
The passage also notes that 'latifa' is a severed attribute (na't maqṭū') and discusses a possible laff wa nashr structure for the three meanings.
This shows the author's attention to linguistic precision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هي (لطيفة) من اللُّطف، وهو ضد الكثافة من (لطف) كـ (گرم) ، دقّ أو رقّ، فاللطيف الصَّغير الحجم أو الرَّقيقُ القوام، أو الشَّفَّافُ الذي لا يحجب ما وراءه كالزُّجاج ، فإذا أطلق بهذا المعنى على الله تعالى فمعناه : العالم بخفيَّات الأمور، لما مرّ من أنَّ اللفظ إذا أوهم خلاف المراد في حقه تعالى يراد منه لازمه . قوله : (هي لطيفة : قدَّر الضمير ؛ إشارة إلى أنَّ (لطيفة) خبر مبتدأ محذوف، فهو نعت مقطوع ؛ لئلا يتوهم أنَّ تلك الأوصاف المذكورة تعدُّ من جملة الاسم. قوله : (دقّ) : أي : صغر حجمه . وقوله : (أو رقّ) : ضدُّ غلظ قوله : (الصَّغير الحجم) راجع لـ(دق). وقوله : (أو الرقيق القوام): راجع لـ(رق). وقوله : (أو الشَّفاف) : لم يبين ما يرجع له، فحقه أن يقول بعد قوله: (أو رق) أو شفّ، فيكون في الكلام ل ون نشر مرتب، والمعاني متغايرة؛ : فإنَّه لا يلزم من الصغر الرّقة، ولا من الرّقة الشَّفافية، ولا من الشَّفافية الصغر. قوله : (إذا أوهم خلاف المراد أي وهذه المعاني مستحيلة على الله
It is (laṭīfah) from al-luṭf, which is the opposite of density, from (laṭufa) like (karuma), meaning it became small or thin. So al-laṭīf is the small in size or the thin in substance, or the transparent that does not block what is behind it, like glass. If it is used in this meaning for Allah, then its meaning is: the Knower of hidden matters, according to what has passed that if a word implies something contrary to what is intended concerning Him, then its necessary meaning is intended. His saying: (It is laṭīfah: he supplied the pronoun, indicating that (laṭīfah) is a predicate of an omitted subject, so it is a severed attribute, lest it be thought that those mentioned attributes are part of the name. His saying: (daqqa): meaning its size became small. And his saying: (or raqqa): the opposite of being thick. His saying: (the small in size) refers to (daqqa). And his saying: (or the thin in substance) refers to (raqqa). And his saying: (or the transparent): he did not explain what it refers to, so it should be that after his saying: (or raqqa) he says or shaffa, so that in the speech there is a reverse-ordered paronomasia (laff wa nashr murattab), and the meanings are different; for smallness does not necessarily entail thinness, nor thinness entail transparency, nor transparency entail smallness. His saying: (if it implies contrary to what is intended): meaning, and these meanings are impossible for Allah...
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'laṭīfah' (subtle) used in the text and explains its application to Allah. The author explains that 'luṭf' is the opposite of density, with two possible meanings: smallness (daqqa) or thinness (raqqa). From these come three possible meanings of 'laṭīf': small in size, thin in substance, or transparent like glass. When applied to Allah, none of these literal meanings are suitable, so the term is interpreted according to its necessary implication (lāzim), which is that Allah knows hidden matters. The passage also includes grammatical analysis: 'laṭīfah' is a predicate of an omitted subject (huwa), functioning as a severed attribute (naʿt maqṭūʿ). It further discusses the relationship between the three meanings (smallness, thinness, transparency), noting that they are distinct and not necessarily implied by one another, and that the author could have arranged them in a reverse-ordered paronomasia (laff wa nashr murattab). The passage ends with the principle that if a word implies something inappropriate for Allah, its necessary meaning is intended.

The Subject Matter of Tawhid: God's Essence and Attributes
Al-Sawi defines the subject matter of tawhid as God's essence with respect to attributes, and explains the technical sense of 'subject'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the science of tawhid defines its subject matter as God's essence considered with respect to His attributes, and why the term 'accidents' is used leniently when referring to divine attributes.

Core Idea
The subject of tawhid is God's essence, considered as the substrate for His perfect and negative attributes.

Every science has a subject matter about which it investigates essential properties. For tawhid, that subject is God's essence, but not in a bare sense—it is considered only in relation to His attributes. In this passage, Al-Sawi clarifies this technical definition and addresses a potential confusion about using the term 'accidents' for divine attributes, emphasizing that it is a lenient expression.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the translation. Let the author's explanation of the subject matter sink in before diving into details.
2
Identify the two attribute categories
Notice that the passage mentions two types of attributes: perfect attributes (ṣifāt kamāliyya) and negative attributes (ṣifāt tanzīhiyya). Understand how they are predicated on the essence.
The essence is like a grammatical subject, and attributes are predicates.
3
Connect 'subject' across disciplines
The passage links the term 'subject' (mawḍūʿ) in logic to 'predicated upon' (musnad ilayhi) in rhetoric and 'subject' (mubtadaʾ) in grammar. See how this unified concept helps define the scope of any science.
This interdisciplinar reflection shows the precision of the definition.
4
Understand the leniency in 'accidents'
The author states that calling God's attributes 'accidents' is a leniency (tasammuḥ) because accidents are traits of contingent beings. Reflect on why the term is still used and what it means for the study of tawhid.
This prevents a common misunderstanding that God's attributes are accidental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وموضوعه : ذات الإله : أي موضوع هذا العلم ذات الإله من : حيث إثبات الصفات الكماليَّة والتنزيهيَّة؛ بأن تجعل ذات الإله موضوعاً تُحمل عليه الصفات، بحيث تقول : ذات الإله يجب لها الوجود والقدم والقدرة . . . إلى آخرها فيكون المراد بالموضوع المصطلح عليه عند المناطقة، المعبّر عنه بالمسند إليه عند البيانيين وبالمبتدأ عند النحويين، فموضوع كلِّ فنّ ما يبحث فيه عن عوارضه الذَّاتيَّة، وإن كان التَّعبير بالعوارض في هذا الفنّ تسمحاً، إذ المراد منها هنا صفاته تعالى، ويستحيل وصفها بالعوارض، إذ هي من سمات الحوادث، وهي مستحيلة على ذاته تعالى وعلى صفاته .
His saying: (and its subject matter is the essence of God) i.e., the subject matter of this science is the essence of God (dhāt al-ilāh) from the perspective of establishing the attributes of perfection (al-ṣifāt al-kamāliyya) and negative attributes (al-tanzīhiyya); such that the essence of God is made a subject (mawḍūʿ) upon which the attributes are predicated, so that you say: The essence of God necessitates for itself existence, pre-eternity, power, etc., up to the rest. Thus what is meant by 'subject matter' is the technical term used by the logicians (al-munāṭiqah), expressed as 'predicated upon' (al-musnad ilayhi) by the rhetoricians and as 'subject' (al-mubtadaʾ) by the grammarians. So the subject matter of every art is that about which its essential accidents (ʿawāriḍ dhātiyya) are investigated, although the expression 'accidents' in this art is a leniency, because what is meant here by them are His attributes (ṣifātuhu taʿālā), and it is impossible to describe them as accidents, for they are characteristics of contingent beings, and they are impossible for His essence and His attributes.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage explains the subject matter (mawḍūʿ) of the science of tawhid. The commentator states that the subject matter is the essence of God (dhāt Allāh), but not in an absolute sense; rather, it is considered specifically with respect to establishing two types of attributes: perfect attributes (ṣifāt kamāliyya, e.g., existence, pre-eternity, power) and negative attributes (ṣifāt tanzīhiyya, i.e., those that negate imperfections). The essence is treated as a subject (mawḍūʿ) upon which attributes are predicated, much like a grammatical subject. The commentator then draws a parallel to logic, rhetoric, and grammar: the term 'subject' (mawḍūʿ) in logic corresponds to the 'predicated upon' (musnad ilayhi) in rhetoric and the 'subject' (mubtadaʾ) in grammar. He then gives the general principle: the subject of any discipline is that about which the discipline investigates its 'essential accidents' (ʿawāriḍ dhātiyya). However, he notes that applying the term 'accidents' (aʿrāḍ) to God's attributes is a figurative leniency (tasammuḥ), because accidents are characteristics of contingent beings, whereas God's attributes are eternal and necessary, not accidental. Thus the subject matter is God's essence qua substrate for necessary, impossible, and possible predications, but always understood in a way that preserves divine transcendence.

الصفات الكمالية

العوارض الذاتية
المناطقة
المسند إليه
المبتدأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's attributes are accidental like created things.
Correction: The passage explains that using the term 'accidents' for divine attributes is a leniency; attributes are eternal and necessary, not accidental.

Checkpoint

A) Because God's attributes are not real
B) Because accidents are characteristics of contingent beings, and God's attributes are eternal and necessary
C) Because the logicians forbid it
D) Because the subject matter is not really God's essence

Key Takeaway
The subject matter of tawhid is God's essence as the substrate for His eternal and necessary attributes, and calling these attributes 'accidents' is a figurative expression, not a literal description.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Spiritual Highness of Allah and the Meaning of Knowledge Terms
Defines 'high in status', explains synonymy of ma'rifah and 'ilm, defines idrak and jazim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of Allah's 'high in status' as spiritual transcendence, the relationship between ma'rifah and 'ilm, and the definitions of idrak and jazim.

Core Idea
Allah’s highness is spiritual; ma'rifah and 'ilm are synonymous; perception divides into decisive and non-decisive.

This passage from al-Sawi's commentary clarifies key terms from the matn. It explains what it means for Allah to be 'high in status'—a spiritual transcendence, not physical elevation. It then addresses the relationship between ma'rifah and 'ilm, settling the correct view that they are synonymous, and finally defines the concepts of idrak (perception) and jazim (decisive cognition). Understanding these definitions is foundational for theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Understand Allah’s spiritual highness
Read the first sentence explaining 'al-aliy bil-manzilah'. Notice that highness is spiritual, meaning free from defects and qualified with perfections, not physical.
Physical highness is impossible for Allah.
2
Grasp the synonymy of ma'rifah and 'ilm
Read the statements about 'with one meaning'. Understand the correct view that they are synonymous, and the two reasons why Allah is not described with ma'rifah.
The alternative view (ma'rifah more specific) is also mentioned but rejected.
3
Define idrak and jazim
Read the final part defining idrak as genus and jazim as differentia. Learn that non-decisive cognition includes conjecture, doubt, and illusion.
This classification is essential for epistemology in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (العليُّ بالمنزلة) أي علوّاً معنوياً، وهو التَّنزه عن النقائص والاتصاف بالكمالات، لا حسّيّاً لاستحالته في حقه تعالى، والمراد بالمنزلة : المرتبة المعنوية.
قوله : (بمعنى واحد) أي: وعليه فعدم اتصافه تعالى بالمعرفة، إمَّا لعدم ورودها، أو لإيهامها سبق الجهل.
وقوله : (على الصَّحيح) مقابله : أنَّ المعرفة أخصُّ من العلم؛ لتعلقها بالبسائط والجزئيات، وتعلقه بالبسائط والمركبات والجزئيات والكليات،
وعليه : فعدم اتصافه تعالى بالمعرفة ظاهر لقصورها
قوله : (وهو : الإدراك) : جنس يشمل الجازم وغيره.
وقوله : (الجازم) : فصل مخرج لغير الجازم ؛ كالظَّنِّ والشَّكِّ والوهم.
His statement: (the High in status) meaning a highness that is spiritual, and it is being free from defects and qualified with perfections, not sensory highness because of its impossibility with respect to Him, the Most High. And by 'status' is meant the spiritual rank. His statement: (with one meaning) i.e., accordingly, His being not described with ma'rifah is either because it has not been transmitted, or because it implies prior ignorance. And His statement: (according to the correct view) its opposite: that ma'rifah is more specific than 'ilm, because it is related to simples and particulars, while 'ilm is related to simples, composites, particulars, and universals. Accordingly, His being not described with ma'rifah is apparent due to its deficiency. His statement: (and it is perception) is a genus that includes both decisive and other. And His statement: (the decisive) is a differentia that excludes other than decisive, like conjecture, doubt, and illusion.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage provides a commentary on several key terms from the matn. First, it explains 'al-aliy bil-manzilah': this means highness in a spiritual sense, which is being transcendent above deficiencies and characterized by perfections. It is not physical highness, as that is impossible for Allah. Then it discusses the phrase 'bi ma'nan wahid' (with one meaning) referring to the relationship between ma'rifah and 'ilm. According to the correct view, they are synonymous. The consequence of this synonymy is that Allah is not described as having 'ma'rifah' either because the term is not transmitted in revelation or because it might imply prior ignorance (since ma'rifah often presupposes previous ignorance in human language). The alternative view is that ma'rifah is more specific than 'ilm, applying only to simples and particulars, while 'ilm encompasses both simples and composites, particulars and universals. According to that view, it is clear why Allah is not described with ma'rifah—because it is deficient. Finally, the passage defines the word 'al-idrak' (perception) as a genus that includes both decisive and non-decisive types, and 'al-jazim' (the decisive) is the differentia that excludes non-decisive cognition like conjecture (zann), doubt (shakk), and illusion (wahm).

Key Terms
العلي بالمنزلة
المعرفة
العلم
الإدراك
الجازم
الظن
الشك
الوهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Allah not being described with 'ma'rifah' indicates a deficiency in His knowledge.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it is either because the term is not used in revelation or to avoid implying prior ignorance, not because of any deficiency.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct view regarding the relationship between ma'rifah and 'ilm?
A) Ma'rifah is more general than 'ilm.
B) They are synonymous (bi ma'nan wahid).
C) 'Ilm is more specific than ma'rifah.
D) There is no relationship between them.

Key Takeaway
Allah’s highness is spiritual transcendence; ma'rifah and 'ilm are synonymous, and perception is either decisive or non-decisive.

Refuting the Objection: The Need for a Time Specifier
Refutation of the fifth objection using divine free choice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth objection against temporal creation and why it fails because it denies divine free choice.

Core Idea
Divine free choice is the sufficient reason; no external cause is needed.

This lesson tackles a sophisticated objection: if the world is created in time, there must be a cause that selects its precise moment. The objectors argue that God alone cannot be that cause, leading to an infinite regress or an eternal world. The refutation exposes the hidden assumption that God acts by necessity, and affirms that His free will is the ultimate preponderator.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection's logic
Read the first part of the source passage where the objectors argue that a temporal thing needs a necessitator (mūjib) to specify its time, and that God cannot be that cause because then the world would be eternal.
Notice the chain: if God were sufficient cause, effect would be co-eternal.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The objectors conclude that the necessitator must be either eternal (proving their claim) or temporal (leading to infinite regress). See how they try to force a dilemma.
Both options are intended to support their denial of temporal creation.
3
Grasp the refutation's core
The author responds sharply: this misguidance stems from denying divine choice (al-ikhtiyār). Choice itself is the preponderator (al-murajjiḥ) that specifies without needing another cause.
The refutation does not accept the assumption that causation must be necessary.
4
Appreciate the Quranic support
Two verses are quoted: 'Your Lord creates what He wills and chooses' (28:68) and 'He is not questioned about what He does' (21:23). These affirm God's absolute freedom.
The verses ground the argument in revelation.
5
Recognize intrinsic choice
The author concludes that choice is intrinsic (dhātī) to God and does not require a necessitator. This blocks the infinite regress.
Intrinsic means it is part of His essence, not an external factor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامسة : قالوا : لو كان حادثاً ؛ لاحتاج لموجب يخصصه بوقت حدوثه دون غيره، وذلك الموجب ليس مجرد الصانع، إذ لو كفي علَّة ؛ لزم مصاحبة المعلول له فيلزمه القدم فتعيَّن أنّ الموجب أمر آخر ؛ فإما قديم فيتم مطلوبنا، أو حادث فيحتاج أيضاً لموجب، وهكذا . قلنا : هو ضلال جاءكم من نفي الاختيار الذي هو المرجح في كلِّ حادث، وَرَبُّكَ يَخْلُقُ مَا يَشَاءُ وَيَختار )) (١) ، لَا يُسْتَلُ عَمَّا يَفْعَلُ ) (۲) وتنزّه عن ضيق التَّأثير بالتَّعليل أو الطبع والاختيار ذاتي لا يحتاج الموجب.
The fifth: They said: If it were temporal (ḥādith), it would need a necessitator (mūjib) that specifies it with a time of its happening rather than another. And that necessitator is not merely the Maker (al-ṣāniʿ), because if He sufficed as a cause (ʿilla), then the effect (maʿlūl) would accompany Him, thus eternity (al-qidam) would follow. So it is determined that the necessitator is something else; either it is eternal, then our desired conclusion is reached, or it is temporal, then it too needs a necessitator, and so on. We said: This is misguidance that came to you from denying choice (al-ikhtiyār), which is the preponderator (al-murajjiḥ) in every temporal thing. 'And your Lord creates what He wills and chooses' (28:68); 'He is not questioned about what He does' (21:23). And He is exalted above the narrowness of effect through causation (al-taʿlīl) or nature (al-ṭabʿ). And choice (al-ikhtiyār) is intrinsic (dhātī), it does not need a necessitator (mūjib).
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection against temporal origination and its refutation. The objectors claim that a temporal thing requires a specific cause (mūjib) to determine its time of occurrence. The mere Maker (al-ṣāniʿ) cannot be that cause, because if He were sufficient, the effect would be co-existent with Him (since a cause necessitates its effect), leading to the world being eternal. Therefore, the specifier must be something else—either eternal (which proves the world's eternity, as the objectors desire) or temporal (leading to infinite regress). The refutation (قلنا) states that this argument is misguided because it denies free choice (al-ikhtiyār). In fact, divine will is the preponderator (al-murajjiḥ) that specifies the time without needing another cause. The author quotes two Quranic verses: 'Your Lord creates what He wills and chooses' (28:68) and 'He is not questioned about what He does' (21:23). God is exalted above the constraints of natural causation or necessitation. Choice is intrinsic to God (dhātī) and does not itself require a necessitator. Thus, the objection collapses because it assumed a purely causal model, ignoring voluntary agency.

Key Terms
موجب
اختيار
مرجح
علة
معلول
تأثير
تعليل
طبع
ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's action must be necessitated by a cause, so creation requires an external specifier for its time.
Correction: God's free choice is the sufficient preponderator; no external cause is needed. Choice is intrinsic to His essence.

Checkpoint
What does the author identify as the root error in the fifth objection?
A) Denial of God's existence.
B) Denial of divine free choice (al-ikhtiyār).
C) Confusion between essence and attributes.
D) Assuming the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The fifth objection is refuted by affirming that God's free choice is the preponderator that specifies the time of creation without requiring another cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where God's timing seems 'off' to you, do you catch yourself demanding a logical reason or cause? How would this lesson change your inner response? Pause right now and confess to God any area where you have doubted His freedom to choose.

Consider a specific prayer you are waiting to be answered. In your heart, have you assumed God needs a 'necessitator'—a reason or condition—to act? Will you surrender that assumption today and trust that His choice alone is enough? Name one concrete step you will take to stop questioning His timing.
?
The objection in this passage is called 'misguidance' for denying God's free choice. Is there any part of your life where you secretly believe that God must act according to your plans or logic? Write down that area and commit to repeating: 'He is not questioned about what He does.' Then leave it before Him.

The Fifth Objection: The Particularizer Must Be Eternal or Temporal
Al-Sawi records the fifth objection and answer about the need for a particularizer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth philosophical objection against the contingency of the world and the Ashʿarī response that grounds particularization in divine will.

Core Idea
Divine will stops the regress: God's choice needs no other particularizer.

This lesson examines the fifth objection raised by philosophers against the Ashʿarī proof for a temporal world. The objection argues that the world's specific time requires a particularizer, which must be either eternal or temporal, leading to a dilemma. Al-Sawi presents the objection and then gives the Ashʿarī answer rooted in God's free will.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation, focusing on the argument that a particularizer is needed and cannot be merely the Maker.
Notice the logical structure: if the Maker sufficed, the effect would be eternal.
2
Identify the dilemma
The objection forces a choice: the particularizer is either eternal (second eternal) or temporal (infinite regress). See how this challenges the Ashʿarī position.
3
Study the Ashʿarī answer
Al-Sawi’s response denies the premise that God acts by necessity. He asserts that divine will (ikhtiyār) is the preponderating factor that needs no further particularizer.
The key term 'اختيار' (choice) is central.
4
Reflect on the Quranic proofs
The answer quotes two verses emphasizing God's sovereign will and freedom from questioning. Consider how these support the idea of an essential, uncaused choice.
Quran 28:68 and 21:23.
5
Understand the concept of essential choice
Al-Sawi concludes that choice is an essential attribute (dhātī) of God, not requiring a cause. This stops the regress without falling into the objection's trap.
Compare with the objection's assumption that particularization must be caused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حدوثه دون غيره، وذلك الموجب ليس مجرد الصانع، إذ لو كفي علَّة؛ لزم مصاحبة المعلول له فيلزمه القدم فتعيَّن أنّ الموجب أمر آخر؛ فإما قديم فيتم مطلوبنا، أو حادث فيحتاج أيضاً لموجب، وهكذا قلنا : هو ضلال جاءكم من نفي الاختيار الذي هو المرجح في كلِّ حادث، وَرَبُّكَ يَخْلُقُ مَا يَشَاءُ وَيَختار )) ، لَا يُسْتَلُ عَمَّا يَفْعَلُ )) وتنزّه عن ضيق التَّأثير بالتَّعليل أو الطبع والاختيار ذاتي لا يحتاج الموجب.
Its occurrence specific to it, and that particularizer is not merely the Maker, for if He sufficed as a cause, the effect would accompany Him, necessitating its eternity. So it is determined that the particularizer is something else; either it is eternal, then our claim is fulfilled, or it is temporal, then it also needs a particularizer, and so on. We said: This is misguidance that has come to you from negating choice (ikhtiyār), which is the preponderating factor in every temporal event. 'And your Lord creates what He wills and chooses' (Quran 28:68). 'He is not questioned about what He does' (Quran 21:23). He is transcendent above the narrowness of effect through causation or natural disposition, and choice is essential, not needing a particularizer.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth philosophical objection against the contingency of the world and the Ashʿarī response. The objection argues that the world's specific time of occurrence requires a particularizer (mūjib). This particularizer cannot be simply the Maker, because if the Maker were a mere cause, the effect would be eternal with Him. Hence the particularizer must be something else—either eternal (which would be a second eternal, proving the philosophers' point) or temporal (leading to infinite regress). The answer refutes this by affirming divine will (irāda) and choice (ikhtiyār). God’s action is not necessitated by a cause; He freely chooses to create at a specific time. The Quranic verses cited emphasize God’s absolute freedom and sovereignty. The objection's flaw is that it assumes efficient causation without will. In Ashʿarī theology, choice is an essential attribute (dhātī) of God that does not itself require a particularizer.

Key Terms
موجب (mūjib): particularizer/causative factor
اختيار (ikhtiyār): free choice/will
علَّة (ʿilla): cause

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ashʿarī answer simply replaces one cause with another, still requiring a regress.
Correction: The answer posits that divine will is an essential attribute that is self-sufficient; it does not need a further cause, stopping the regress absolutely.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, what is the fundamental error in the fifth objection?
A) It assumes the world is eternal.
B) It denies that God has free choice and will.
C) It requires an infinite number of particularizers.
D) It confuses the Maker with the particularizer.

Key Takeaway
The fifth objection is refuted by affirming that God's essential will is the self-sufficient particularizer, requiring no further cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The philosophers demanded a rational explanation for why God created the world at a specific time. Are there areas of your life where you demand God explain himself to you? How will you practice saying 'He is not questioned about what He does' when trials come without explanation?
?
You claim to believe that God's will is self-sufficient and requires no cause. But examine your daily prayers: do you sometimes feel your du'a needs a 'reason' to be answered? Will you pray with the conviction that God's choice alone is the particularizer, trusting that His wisdom precedes your understanding?

The Seventh Objection: Does Creation Imply Change in God?
Al-Sawi records the seventh objection and its answer distinguishing change of actions from change of essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ashʿarī theologians distinguish between change in actions (taghayyur afʿāl) and change in essence (taghayyur dhāt) to defend divine immutability while affirming temporal creation.

Core Idea
God's becoming Creator is a change in action, not in His essence.

The objection that creation implies a change in God is one of the oldest theological challenges. Al-Sawi records it and gives the classic Ashʿarī answer: a distinction between essential attributes (immutable) and attributes of action (temporal). This lesson unpacks that distinction so you can see how it preserves divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. The objection states: if the world is temporal, then in pre-eternity God was not Maker, so by creating He becomes Maker — this would be a change, which is impossible for God.
Notice the logical structure: premise -> implication -> conclusion.
2
Identify the answer's key distinction
The answer says: 'This is a change of actions, not of essence or essential attributes.' Underline the two types of change: taghayyur afʿāl (change in actions) and taghayyur dhāt/ṣifāt dhātiyya (change in essence/essential attributes).
3
Understand why change in actions is allowed
Reflect: why is change in actions permissible for God? Because actions (afʿāl) are relational — they depend on the existence of the created thing. Essential attributes (like power, knowledge) are intrinsic and unchanging. The attribute 'Creator' is an attribute of action, not essence.
This distinction is central to Ashʿarī theology.
4
Test your understanding with the check point
Answer the multiple-choice question to confirm you can distinguish the type of change at issue.
If you get it wrong, re-read the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابعة : قالوا : لو كان حادثاً ؛ لكان الصَّانع في الأزل غير صانع، فبإحداثه يطرأ له كونه صانعاً، والتغير عليه تعالى محال . قلنا : هذا تغير أفعال، وهو غير ممتنع بخلاف تغير الذات والصفات الذاتية.
The seventh: They said: If it were temporal, then the Maker in pre-eternity would be non-Maker; by bringing it into existence, His being Maker arises, and change upon Him, exalted, is impossible. We said: This is a change of actions (afʿāl), which is not impossible, unlike change of essence (dhāt) and essential attributes (ṣifāt dhātiyya).
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh objection and its answer. The objection: if the world is temporal (ḥādith), then in pre-eternity (azal) God was not Creator; when He creates, the attribute of 'being Creator' arises in Him, which would be a change (taghayyur) in God, and change is impossible for the Creator. The answer distinguishes: the change is in actions (afʿāl), i.e., in the relation between God and the world, not in God's essence or essential attributes (dhāt wa ṣifāt dhātiyya). God's attribute of being Creator (al-khāliqiyya) is an attribute of action (ṣifa fiʿliyya), which allows temporal arising (ḥudūth) without implying change in the essence. This distinction preserves divine immutability while allowing temporal creation.

Key Terms
تغير أفعال (taghayyur afʿāl): change in actions
صفات ذاتية (ṣifāt dhātiyya): essential attributes
الصانع (al-ṣāniʿ): the Maker

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any attribute that arises temporally in God implies a change in His essence.
Correction: Ashʿarīs distinguish attributes of action (which arise temporally) from attributes of essence (which are eternal). Only the latter define God's essence; change in actions does not affect the essence.

Checkpoint
What kind of change does the answer attribute to God when He becomes Creator of the temporal world?
A) Change in essence (dhāt).
B) Change in essential attributes (ṣifāt dhātiyya).
C) Change in actions (afʿāl).
D) No change at all.

Key Takeaway
The Ashʿarī answer to the change objection is that God's becoming Creator is a change in actions, not in essence, thus preserving divine immutability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God as your personal Creator, do you ever unconsciously imagine that He 'became' something new when He created you? How does this lesson's distinction between actions and essence challenge that mental picture, and what will you do to correct it?
?
The objection in this passage assumes that any new action would change God. In your own life, have you ever doubted God’s consistency because of new circumstances? How can this lesson reassure you that God’s actions never alter His faithful essence?
?
If God’s attribute of being Creator is an action that arose in time, does that make you feel any less secure in His eternal plan for you? Be honest—and then commit to trusting His unchanging character, not just His changing actions.
