The Primacy of the Attribute of Existence
Commentary on the prioritization of Ilahiyyat and the attribute of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author gives precedence to Ilahiyyat and the attribute of existence, and how the commentary clarifies the terms 'wasf' and 'sifa'.

Core Idea
The attribute of existence is the foundation of all divine attributes.

This passage from the commentary explains the rationale behind ordering topics in Ilahiyyat. It shows why existence is given priority and clarifies two possible meanings of the key term 'wasf'. The commentary helps us see the depth behind simple phrases.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Let the author's reasoning come through before analysis.
Notice the compact style typical of commentaries.
2
Understand the prioritization
Identify the reasons given for prioritizing Ilahiyyat, then within it the necessary, and then existence. Note the logical flow.
Existence is given priority because everything else derives from it.
3
Analyze the commentary on 'wasf'
The commentator offers two interpretations: 'wasf' as verbal noun (masdar) and 'wasf' as attribute (sifa). Understand both.
This clarifies the phrase 'i.e., His being described'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مطلب في الإلهيات] . . . . . شرع الآن يفصل ما أجمله مقدِّماً للإلهيَّات، لتعلقها بالحقِّ، وما تعلق به مقدَّم على غيره، وبدأ من الإلهيَّات بالواجب لشرفه، مقدماً للوجود لأصالته؛ فإنَّ ما سواه مفرَّع عليه . قوله : (أي : اتّصافه) : أشار بذلك إلى أنَّ الوصف باق على مصدريَّته . قوله : (بالوجود) : أي : الذاتي . قوله : ( ويصح أن يراد أيضاً بالوصف الصفة) : أي : فالمراد المعنى الأسمى.
Section on Ilahiyyat] ... He now explains in detail what he summarized, giving precedence to Ilahiyyat because of its connection to the Truth, and what is connected to Him is given precedence over other things. And he began from Ilahiyyat with the necessary (wajib) due to its honor, giving precedence to existence due to its primacy; for everything else is derived from it. His statement: 'i.e., His being described': he indicated by that that the verbal noun (al-wasf) remains in its masdar sense. His statement: 'by existence': i.e., essential (al-dhati). His statement: 'and it is also valid that by al-wasf is meant al-sifa (the attribute)': i.e., then the intended meaning is the loftiest sense.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining why the author prioritizes Ilahiyyat and, within them, the necessary attribute of existence. The commentator notes that the phrase 'i.e., His being described' shows that the word 'wasf' is used in its masdar (verbal noun) meaning. Then 'by existence' refers to essential existence, meaning God's existence is intrinsic. Finally, an alternative interpretation is given: the word 'wasf' can also mean 'sifa' (attribute), referring to the attribute of existence itself, which is the loftiest meaning.

Key Terms
صفة الوجود
الوصف
الذاتي
المعنى الأسمى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might confuse 'wasf' as verbal noun with 'sifa' as attribute, thinking they are the same.
Correction: The text clarifies that 'wasf' can mean either the act of describing (masdar) or the attribute itself, depending on context.

Checkpoint
What does 'bi al-wujud' (by existence) mean according to the commentary?
A) Existence through another
B) Essential existence
C) Conceptual existence
D) Temporal existence

Key Takeaway
The attribute of existence is essential (dhati) and is the foundation from which all other attributes derive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How might your worship change if you constantly reminded yourself that everything about Him flows from His essential existence?

The author prioritizes knowledge of God (Ilahiyyat) over other topics because of its connection to the Truth. In your own pursuit of knowledge, do you give knowing Allah the highest priority? What practical steps can you take today to refocus your learning on Him?

How does recognizing existence as the intrinsic foundation confront that error? Will you meditate on this truth to deepen your understanding of God's oneness?

The Partitive 'Min' and General Belief in Infinite Attributes
Al-Sawi explains the partitive min and the obligation of general belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the partitive 'min' in the context of divine attributes and the obligation to believe in God's infinite perfections in general.

Core Idea
We must believe in God's infinite attributes generally, but only detailed belief for attributes with proof.

This passage opens a commentary on the divine attributes by clarifying a grammatical nuance: the word 'min' here indicates that only some attributes are mentioned. More importantly, it establishes a key principle: while God's perfections are limitless, our belief in them can be general or detailed depending on evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grammatical point about 'min'
Read the first part of the passage where 'min' is explained as partitive. Note that it means 'some of' the attributes, not all.
2
Understand the infinity of attributes
The passage states that God's existential attributes are without end. This establishes the doctrine of infinite divine perfections.
3
Distinguish between general and detailed belief
The passage clarifies that we are only obligated to believe in the general fact of infinite perfections; detailed belief is required only for attributes supported by specific evidence.
This distinction is important to avoid overburdening believers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أي: بعض الصِّفات: أشار بذلك إلى أنَّ (من) تبعيضيَّة. قوله : ( لأنَّ صفاته تعالى الكمالية لا تتناهى) : أي : صفاته الوجودية لا تتناهى، إلا أنَّه لا يجب علينا تفصيل ما لم يَقُم عليه الدليل بالخصوص، بل الواجب أن نعتقد أنَّ كمالاته تعالى لا تتناهى على الإجمال، وأما ما قام عليه الدليل بخصوصه فيجب اعتقاده تفصيلاً، وهو ثلاثة عشر صفة وأضدادها، بناء على مذهب الأشعري والمحققين من أنَّ المعنوية ليست بصفات زائدة على المعاني، وأنَّ الحقَّ أن لا حال، وعليه فالوجود عين ذات الموجود ليس بصفة زائدة عليها، وفي عدّه من الصفات تسامح باعتبار أنَّ الذات توصف به في اللفظ ، فيقال: ذات الله موجودة، فليتأمل. نهاية لها في الذهن ولا في نفس الأمر ، والله يعلمها تفصيلاً، وأنَّها لا نهاية لها .
His statement: (i.e., some of the attributes): he indicated by that that 'min' is partitive. His statement: (because His perfect attributes, Exalted, are without end): i.e., His existential attributes are without end, except that it is not obligatory upon us to detail what has not been established by individual evidence. Rather, it is obligatory to believe that His perfections, Exalted, are without end in general. As for what has been established by individual evidence, then it is obligatory to believe it in detail, and it is thirteen attributes and their opposites, based on the school of al-Ash'ari and the verifiers (al-muhaqqiqun) that the attributes of meaning-relations (al-ma'nawiyya) are not additional attributes beyond the meaning-attributes (al-ma'ani), and that the truth is that there is no mode (hal). Accordingly, existence is the identical essence of the existent, not an additional attribute upon it. And in counting it among the attributes is looseness (tasamuh), considering that the essence is described by it in speech, so it is said: 'The essence of God is existent.' So let him reflect. They have no end in the mind nor in external reality, and God knows them in detail, and that they have no end.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the attributes. It begins by explaining that the word 'min' in the phrase 'min wajibat' is partitive, meaning 'some' of the necessary attributes. Then it comments on the infinity of God's perfect attributes, clarifying that they are without end. However, we are not obligated to believe in each attribute in detail unless there is specific evidence; rather, we must believe in God's perfections in general. The attributes that have specific evidence are thirteen in number, along with their opposites. This enumeration is based on the school of al-Ash'ari and the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) who hold that the attributes of meaning-relations (sifat ma'nawiyya) are not additional attributes beyond the meaning-attributes (sifat ma'ani), and that there is no such thing as a mode (hal). Consequently, existence is identical with the essence, not a separate attribute. Counting existence as an attribute is a form of linguistic looseness (tasamuh) because in speech we say 'the essence of God is existent,' describing the essence by existence. The passage ends with a reminder that the attributes have no end in the mind or in reality, and that God knows them in detail.

Key Terms
من تبعيضيَّة
كمالية
على الإجمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all of God's attributes must be believed in detail.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only those supported by specific evidence require detailed belief.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the word 'min' indicate in the phrase 'min wajibat'?
A) Partitive (i.e., 'some of')
B) Explanatory
C) Redundancy
D) Negative

Key Takeaway
The obligation to believe in God's infinite perfections is general, but detailed belief is only required for attributes with evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Allāh is the All-Knowing, All-Powerful,' do you truly feel the weight that His knowledge and power are infinite, beyond any limit you can conceive? Or do you silently imagine them as just 'very big'? Today, commit to repeating at least one of His attributes and pausing to affirm its infinity in your heart.
?
Are you ever troubled by not knowing all the details of God's attributes? This lesson teaches that you are only required to believe generally in what has no specific proof. Can you accept that your intellect cannot encompass His perfection, and trust in His wisdom to reveal only what you need? Make a silent prayer of reliance on His infinite majesty.

The Thirteen Attributes and the Ash'ari Position
Al-Sawi details the thirteen attributes and the Ash'ari view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which attributes require detailed belief and the Ash'ari view that existence is not a separate attribute.

Core Idea
Thirteen attributes and their opposites require detailed belief, based on the Ash'ari position.

After establishing the principle of general belief in infinite perfections, the author now specifies the attributes that require detailed belief due to specific evidence. These are thirteen attributes and their opposites, according to the Ash'ari school and the verifiers. The passage also addresses a subtle point about the status of existence as an attribute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the thirteen attributes
The passage mentions 'thirteen attributes and their opposites' that must be believed in detail. Note that these are a specific set.
2
Understand the Ash'ari basis
This enumeration is based on the school of al-Ash'ari and the verifiers (muhaqqiqun). This is the theological framework.
3
Grasp the distinction between meaning-attributes and meaning-relation attributes
The passage states that 'ma'nawiyya' attributes are not additional; they are just relations of the essential 'ma'ani' attributes.
4
Recognize that existence is identical to essence
The passage asserts that existence is the identical essence of the existent, not an additional attribute. Counting it as an attribute is linguistic looseness (tasamuh).
This corrects a potential misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أي: بعض الصِّفات: أشار بذلك إلى أنَّ (من) تبعيضيَّة. قوله : ( لأنَّ صفاته تعالى الكمالية لا تتناهى) : أي : صفاته الوجودية لا تتناهى، إلا أنَّه لا يجب علينا تفصيل ما لم يَقُم عليه الدليل بالخصوص، بل الواجب أن نعتقد أنَّ كمالاته تعالى لا تتناهى على الإجمال، وأما ما قام عليه الدليل بخصوصه فيجب اعتقاده تفصيلاً، وهو ثلاثة عشر صفة وأضدادها، بناء على مذهب الأشعري والمحققين من أنَّ المعنوية ليست بصفات زائدة على المعاني، وأنَّ الحقَّ أن لا حال، وعليه فالوجود عين ذات الموجود ليس بصفة زائدة عليها، وفي عدّه من الصفات تسامح باعتبار أنَّ الذات توصف به في اللفظ ، فيقال: ذات الله موجودة، فليتأمل. نهاية لها في الذهن ولا في نفس الأمر ، والله يعلمها تفصيلاً، وأنَّها لا نهاية لها .
His statement: (i.e., some of the attributes): he indicated by that that 'min' is partitive. His statement: (because His perfect attributes, Exalted, are without end): i.e., His existential attributes are without end, except that it is not obligatory upon us to detail what has not been established by individual evidence. Rather, it is obligatory to believe that His perfections, Exalted, are without end in general. As for what has been established by individual evidence, then it is obligatory to believe it in detail, and it is thirteen attributes and their opposites, based on the school of al-Ash'ari and the verifiers (al-muhaqqiqun) that the attributes of meaning-relations (al-ma'nawiyya) are not additional attributes beyond the meaning-attributes (al-ma'ani), and that the truth is that there is no mode (hal). Accordingly, existence is the identical essence of the existent, not an additional attribute upon it. And in counting it among the attributes is looseness (tasamuh), considering that the essence is described by it in speech, so it is said: 'The essence of God is existent.' So let him reflect. They have no end in the mind nor in external reality, and God knows them in detail, and that they have no end.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the attributes. It begins by explaining that the word 'min' in the phrase 'min wajibat' is partitive, meaning 'some' of the necessary attributes. Then it comments on the infinity of God's perfect attributes, clarifying that they are without end. However, we are not obligated to believe in each attribute in detail unless there is specific evidence; rather, we must believe in God's perfections in general. The attributes that have specific evidence are thirteen in number, along with their opposites. This enumeration is based on the school of al-Ash'ari and the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) who hold that the attributes of meaning-relations (sifat ma'nawiyya) are not additional attributes beyond the meaning-attributes (sifat ma'ani), and that there is no such thing as a mode (hal). Consequently, existence is identical with the essence, not a separate attribute. Counting existence as an attribute is a form of linguistic looseness (tasamuh) because in speech we say 'the essence of God is existent,' describing the essence by existence. The passage ends with a reminder that the attributes have no end in the mind or in reality, and that God knows them in detail.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah exists,' do you truly believe that existence is not an additional attribute but identical to His essence? How does that deepen your understanding of His oneness and simplicity? Will you pause today to reflect on this truth, letting it reshape how you think of His presence?
?
The passage says the attributes have no end and only God knows them in detail. Are you content to rely on God's knowledge for the things you cannot comprehend? Can you humbly accept that your intellect has limits and commit to trusting His wisdom over your own reasoning?
?

For example, does knowing that God's attributes are not separate from His essence bring you closer to Him in prayer? What change will you make today to align your heart with this belief?

Existence is Identical to Essence: The Ash'ari Position and the Linguistic License
Al-Sawi argues for the identity of existence and essence and explains the linguistic license.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ash'ari and the muhaqqiqun hold that existence is identical to the essence, not an additional attribute, and how referring to it as an attribute is a linguistic license.

Core Idea
Existence is not separate from essence; calling it an attribute is only metaphorical.

Al-Sawi continues his discussion of divine attributes by addressing a key Ash'ari doctrine: that existence is not an attribute additional to the essence. This passage explains the reasoning behind this view and clarifies why we may still speak of existence as an attribute in a linguistic sense. It also touches on the broader framework of what must be believed in detail versus general.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the context
Begin by reading the entire Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice that it starts with a general principle about believing in detail only what has proof, then moves to the specific case of the thirteen attributes.
The passage is from al-Sawi's supercommentary, so it includes both the original matn and his explanation.
2
Identify the main claim about existence
Find the sentence 'فالوجود عين ذات الموجود ليس بصفة زائدة عليها'. This is the core thesis: existence is identical to essence, not an extra attribute.
This is a direct consequence of the Ash'ari rejection of states (ahwal).
3
Follow the proof against existence being additional
The passage presents a dilemma: if existence were additional, it would either be existent (leading to infinite regress) or non-existent (impossible to describe existence with non-existence). Work through this reasoning step by step.
This is a classic kalam proof known as the 'tasalsul' (infinite regress) argument.
4
Understand the license (tasamuh)
Note that despite this, we can still speak of existence as an attribute in a metaphorical sense (majaz mursal). The command 'falyatamallal' hints that some scholars hold a different view.
This distinction between real and metaphorical attribution is important in Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلا أنَّه لا يجب علينا تفصيل ما لم يَقُم عليه الدليل بالخصوص، بل الواجب أن نعتقد أنَّ كمالاته تعالى لا تتناهى على الإجمال، وأما ما قام عليه الدليل بخصوصه فيجب اعتقاده تفصيلاً، وهو ثلاثة عشر صفة وأضدادها، بناء على مذهب الأشعري والمحققين من أنَّ المعنوية ليست بصفات زائدة على المعاني، وأنَّ الحقَّ أن لا حال، وعليه فالوجود عين ذات الموجود ليس بصفة زائدة عليها، وفي عدّه من الصفات تسامح باعتبار أنَّ الذات توصف به في اللفظ ، فيقال: ذات الله موجودة، فليتأمل. نهاية لها في الذهن ولا في نفس الأمر ، والله يعلمها تفصيلاً، وأنَّها لا نهاية لها . قوله : (والمحققين ) أي : كالقاضي أبي بكر الباقلاني، وإمام الحرمين. قوله: (فالوجود عين ذات الموجود) تفريع على ما ذهب إليه الأشعري والمحققون. وحاصل ما قالوه أنَّ وجود كلِّ شيء عينه، إذ لو كان زائداً على الذَّات لا يخلو إما أن يكون موجوداً أو لا، والأوّل يوجب التسلسل، والثَّاني يلزم عليه اتصاف الوجود بنقيضه، وهو العدم، وهو محال. قوله : (وفي عدّه من الصِّفات تسامح ) : أي : مجاز مرسل علاقته المجاورة. قوله : (فليتأمل) أمر بالتَّأمُّل ؛ إشارة إلى أنَّ الحقَّ خلاف هذا، وأنَّ
Except that it is not obligatory upon us to detail what no specific proof has been established for; rather, it is obligatory to believe that His perfections (may He be exalted) are not finite in a general way. As for what a specific proof has been established for, it is obligatory to believe it in detail, and it is thirteen attributes and their opposites, according to the school of al-Ash'ari and the scholars (al-muhaqqiqin) that the 'attributive' (al-ma'nawiyya) are not attributes additional to the meanings, and that the truth is that there is no state (hāl), and accordingly existence is identical to the essence of the existent, not an attribute additional to it. Counting it among the attributes is a tolerance (tasāmuḥ) considering that the essence is described by it in wording, so it is said: 'The essence of God is existent'; so let it be pondered. ... It has no end in the mind nor in external reality, and God knows it in detail, and that it has no end. His statement: 'and the scholars' i.e., like al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani and Imam al-Haramayn. His statement: 'existence is identical to the essence of the existent' is a branching from what al-Ash'ari and the scholars held. The gist of what they said is that the existence of everything is identical to it, for if it were additional to the essence, it would not be free from either being existent or not. The first entails infinite regress (tasalsul), and the second entails that existence is described by its opposite, which is non-existence, and that is impossible. His statement: 'and counting it among the attributes is a tolerance' means: it is a transferred metaphor (majāz mursal) with the relation of adjacency. His statement: 'so let it be pondered' is a command to ponder, indicating that the truth is contrary to this, and that...
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument on the nature of existence as an attribute of God. It begins by stating the general principle that we must believe in detail only those attributes for which specific proof exists, while others are believed in general. Among those detailed are thirteen attributes and their opposites, following the Ash'ari and verified scholars who hold that the 'attributive' (al-ma'nawiyya) are not additional to the meanings and that there is no intermediate state (hāl). Consequently, existence is identical to the essence, not an additional attribute; calling it an attribute is a linguistic license. The commentary then explains the proof: if existence were additional, it would either be existent (leading to infinite regress) or non-existent (impossible to describe existence with non-existence). Finally, the tolerance is explained as a transferred metaphor, and the command to ponder hints that the truth opposes this opinion, though the full refutation is cut off.

Key Terms
وجود
ذات
صفة
تسامح
مجاز مرسل
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many people think that existence is a separate attribute from the essence in God, as it is for created beings.
Correction: The Ash'ari and muhaqqiqun deny this; existence is identical to the essence. Calling it an attribute is a linguistic license.

Checkpoint
Why do the Ash'aris and muhaqqiqun hold that existence cannot be an additional attribute to the essence?
A) Because the Quran says so.
B) Because it would lead to either infinite regress or describing existence with non-existence.
C) Because existence is a mental concept only.
D) Because attributes are all eternal.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari school teaches that existence is identical to essence; using 'attribute' for it is a figurative expression.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm 'Allah exists,' do you sense that you are adding something to His essence, or do you realize that His existence is His very reality? How would your worship change if you truly internalized that there is no gap between who He is and that He is?
?
The proof in this lesson shows that infinite regress is impossible when attributing existence. Are there areas in your faith where you rely on endless justifications rather than resting in the simple reality of God? What would it take to stop that chain and simply say 'He is'?
?
If calling existence an attribute is only a linguistic concession, then what are you really saying when you recite 'Allahu Akbar'? Is your heart aware of the reality behind the words, or are you content with a metaphorical understanding that keeps God at a safe distance?

The Ash'ari View on Al-Sifat Al-Ma'nawiyya and the Theory of Ahwal
Al-Sawi explains the Ash'ari view on al-sifat al-ma'nawiyya and contrasts it with the proponents of ahwal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position on al-sifat al-ma'nawiyya as mental considerations and how it differs from the theory of ahwal.

Core Idea
Ash'ari attributes are mental constructs, not real entities.

This lesson clarifies a subtle point in Islamic theology: the nature of relational attributes. Al-Sawi explains that for al-Ash'ari, these are purely mental considerations, while the proponents of ahwal see them as intermediary states. We'll explore the distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms: 'اعتباري', 'نفس الأمر', 'حال', 'واسطة'.
The Arabic has some OCR uncertainties; focus on the overall meaning.
2
Identify the two positions
Find where al-Ash'ari's view is stated (mental consideration only) and where the proponents of ahwal are described (intermediary between existence and non-existence).
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters: it affects how we understand divine attributes and their relation to God's essence.
This connects to broader debates in kalam about the reality of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفات المعنوية أمور اعتباريَّة لا بدَّ من اعتبارها في الذهن وإن لم يكن لها ثبوت من خارج الأذهان ونفس الأمر ، فالأشعري وإن كان ينفي ثبوتها في نفس الأمر [؟] لا الأمر لا ينفي [؟] لاعتبارها في الأذهان، ومن يقول بالأحوال يقول: بأنَّها واسطة بين الوجود والعدم، فالصفة الوجودية عندهم ما يصح أن ترى، والحال ثابتة في الخارج ولا يصح أن ترى .
The 'attributive attributes' (al-ṣifāt al-ma'nawiyya) are matters of consideration (umūr i'tibāriyya) that must be considered in the mind, even if they have no established reality outside the minds and in external reality (nafs al-amr). Al-Ash'ari, although he denies their existence in external reality, [؟] does not deny [؟] their consideration in the minds. And one who asserts 'states' (aḥwāl) says that they are an intermediary (wāsiṭa) between existence and non-existence. So the existential attribute (al-ṣifa al-wujūdiyya) according to them is that which can be seen, while the state (ḥāl) is established in the external world but cannot be seen.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage clarifies the technical term 'al-sifat al-ma'nawiyya' (often translated as 'attributive states' or 'relational attributes'). It states that for al-Ash'ari, these are mental considerations (i'tibariyya) that have no actual existence in external reality (nafs al-amr), though we must conceive of them in the mind. The Ash'ari position denies their objective existence but affirms their mental consideration. In contrast, the proponents of 'ahwal' (states) consider them as intermediary entities between existence and non-existence. According to them, an existential attribute (like existence itself) can be seen, whereas a state (hal) is real in the external world but cannot be perceived by the senses.

Key Terms
صفات معنوية
اعتباري
حال
واسطة
نفس الأمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ash'aris completely deny any reality to attributes.
Correction: Ash'aris affirm attributes as mental considerations while denying them external existence; they are not entirely unreal.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Ash'ari view on al-sifat al-ma'nawiyya?
A) They are real entities in the external world.
B) They are mental considerations with no external reality.
C) They are intermediary between existence and non-existence.
D) They are identical to the essence.

Key Takeaway
Al-sifat al-ma'nawiyya are real as mental considerations but not as external entities, a view distinct from the ahwal theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat Allah's attributes as mere intellectual concepts that have no impact on your daily life, or do you live as if they are real and present? What would change if you truly believed His attributes are not just mental constructs but shape your reality?
?
When you think about Allah's attribute of mercy, do you reduce it to an idea in your mind, or does it compel you to act mercifully right now? What concrete step will you take today to embody that attribute in your treatment of others?
?
This lesson distinguishes between mental consideration and external reality. In your own faith, have you ever settled for a theoretical belief about Allah without letting that belief transform your actions? Confess one area where your knowledge has not become practice, and commit to one change.

Qualificative Attributes as Mental Considerations
Sanusi explains the Ash'ari view of attributes as mental considerations and contrasts it with states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that qualificative attributes are mental considerations without external existence, and the contrasting theory of states as intermediaries.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are real in the mind, not as separate entities outside.

This passage from Sanusi clarifies a key Ash'ari position: the qualificative attributes (like power and will) are not independent existents alongside God's essence, but rather concepts we necessarily form in our minds. Sanusi also contrasts this with the theory of 'states' (أحوال). Let's read his precise words and unpack the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the key terms: الصفات المعنوية, أمور اعتباريَّة, نفس الأمر, أحوال.
The Arabic uses technical terms that are essential for understanding kalam.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage presents the Ash'ari view first, then contrasts it with the proponents of states (أحوال). Note how each defines the ontological status of attributes.
3
Understand 'mental consideration'
The Ash'ari uses 'أمور اعتباريَّة' to mean something that exists only in the mind, not in external reality. But it is not negated either—it is a necessary conceptual tool.
This is different from saying attributes are unreal or illusions.
4
Grasp the concept of 'state'
For the proponents of أحوال, a state is an intermediary between existence and non-existence—it is established externally but cannot be seen, unlike an existential attribute which can be seen.
This is a more nuanced metaphysical category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفات المعنوية أمور اعتباريَّة لا بدَّ من اعتبارها في الذهن وإن لم يكن لها ثبوت من خارج الأذهان ونفس الأمر ، فالأشعري وإن كان ينفي ثبوتها في نفس الأمر لا الأمر لا ينفي لاعتبارها في الأذهان، ومن يقول بالأحوال يقول: بأنَّها واسطة بين الوجود والعدم، فالصفة الوجودية عندهم ما يصح أن ترى، والحال ثابتة في الخارج ولا يصح أن ترى .
The qualificative attributes (الصفات المعنوية) are matters of consideration (أمور اعتباريَّة) that must be considered in the mind, even if they have no establishment outside the minds and in reality itself (نفس الأمر). So the Ash'ari, although he negates their establishment in reality itself, does not negate their consideration in the minds. And whoever speaks of states (أحوال) says that they are an intermediary between existence and non-existence. So the existential attribute (الصفة الوجودية) according to them is what is valid to be seen, while the state (الحال) is established externally but is not valid to be seen.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage classifies the qualificative attributes (الصفات المعنوية) such as power, will, etc. It states that they are mental considerations (أمور اعتباريَّة) that must be assumed in the mind for conceptual analysis, even though they have no external, mind-independent existence. This is the Ash'ari position: these attributes are not real entities in the external world, but they are not negated either—they are considered in the mind. The passage then contrasts this with the doctrine of 'states' (أحوال), held by some theologians, who consider them as intermediates between existence and non-existence. For them, an existential attribute is what can be seen (like power when manifested), while a state is established externally but cannot be seen. The passage clarifies the epistemological and ontological status of divine attributes within Ash'ari theology.

Key Terms
الصفات المعنوية
أمور اعتباريَّة
نفس الأمر
الأشعري
أحوال
الصفة الوجودية
الحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari denies all attributes of God, leaving Him without qualities.
Correction: The Ash'ari affirms attributes as real mental considerations corresponding to God's perfection; he only denies they are distinct entities existing externally.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position described, what is the status of the qualificative attributes (الصفات المعنوية)?
A) They have real existence outside the mind.
B) They are mental considerations with no external existence.
C) They are identical with God's essence.
D) They are states seen in the external world.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari affirms that qualificative attributes are necessary mental considerations without being distinct external entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's attributes like 'All-Knowing' or 'All-Powerful', do you ever imagine them as separate 'things' added to His essence? How does the Ash'ari view of attributes as mental considerations challenge that mental picture, and what will you do to correct it?
?
The Ash'ari affirms that attributes are necessary mental considerations even though they have no external existence. Have you ever felt that understanding tawhid requires denying attributes altogether? How does this lesson help you affirm attributes without compromising tawhid? What concrete step will you take to align your belief with this balanced view?
?
The concept of 'states' says attributes are neither existent nor non-existent. Does this make you uneasy because it seems abstract? How can you deepen your trust that Allah's attributes are real despite being beyond full human comprehension? What prayer or reflection can you make today to live in light of this truth?

Divine Names and the Purpose of Creation: al-Wadud to al-Hakim
Al-Sawi defines five divine names and links them to worship and solitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of five divine names—al-Wadud, al-Ali, al-Azim, al-Alim, al-Hakim—and how they guide one to the purpose of creation: worship, love, and seclusion for reflection.

Core Idea
Knowing Allah’s names leads to worship, love, and spiritual solitude.

In this passage, al-Sawi unpacks five key divine names from the Kharida, each illuminating an aspect of Allah’s attributes. He then ties them together to show how proper understanding directs the believer toward the very purpose of existence—worship—and cultivates a love that is deepened through seclusion. This short commentary connects theology to practice.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: an initial summary phrase followed by line-by-line commentary on each name.
The passage begins by listing five names together before explaining them individually.
2
Examine each name definition
For each of the five names, identify how al-Sawi defines it. Pay special attention to the nuance for al-Ali: 'high in rank, not in place'.
This guards against imagining Allah as occupying physical space.
3
Connect names to purpose
Note the phrase 'so you are guided to that for which you were created.' How does knowledge of these names lead to worship? Reflect on the Quranic verse cited.
The verse from Surat al-Dhariyat (51:56) is a key proof for the purpose of creation.
4
Trace the outcome: love and solitude
Observe how the commentary moves from worship to abundant love and gratitude, and then to the role of seclusion. Why does seclusion help? Consider the quote from Ibn 'Ata'illah.
Seclusion is not isolationism but a means to focus the heart on reflection.

The Loving, the Most High, the Great, the All-Knowing, the All-Wise, so you are guided to that for which you were created, then you ascend to the abundance of His love and gratitude, and that results in the eruption of other than Him. His saying: (al-Wadud) i.e., the lover of His servants, the beloved to them. And His saying: (al-Ali) i.e., in rank, not in place, because that is impossible for Him. And His saying: (al-Azim) i.e., described with majesty and grandeur in reality, not... His saying: (al-Alim) i.e., described with perfect knowledge pertaining to necessities, possibilities, and impossibilities. And His saying: (al-Hakim) i.e., described with wisdom, which is the perfection of things in a proportionate manner. His saying: (to that for which you were created) i.e., which is worship, He said: 'And I did not create jinn and humans except to worship Me' (51:56). His saying: (the abundance of His love) from the addition of love to the described; i.e., His abundant love, i.e., extra. His saying: (and that results in… etc.) i.e., and that is aided by seclusion from people, Ibn 'Ata'illah al-Sakandari said in his aphorisms: 'Nothing benefits the heart like seclusion by which it enters the field of reflection.'

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on divine names. It lists five names: al-Wadud (the Loving), al-Ali (the Most High), al-Azim (the Great), al-Alim (the All-Knowing), al-Hakim (the All-Wise). The commentator explains each: al-Wadud means He loves His servants and is beloved to them; al-Ali means high in rank, not physical location; al-Azim means truly great and majestic; al-Alim means having perfect knowledge of all categories (necessary, possible, impossible); al-Hakim means possessing wisdom – the perfection of things in due proportion. Then the passage says that by knowing these names, one is guided to the purpose of creation: worship (citing Qur'an 51:56). Then one ascends to abundant love and gratitude, which is helped by isolation from people, quoting Ibn 'Ata'illah on the benefit of seclusion for reflection.

?
When life doesn't go as you planned, do you trust that al-Hakim is perfectly wise? Can you submit to His decree right now, even if you don't understand it, and thank Him for it?

Definitions of Five Divine Names: al-Wadud, al-Ali, al-Azim, al-Alim, al-Hakim
Al-Sawi defines five divine names with clarifications on transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of five divine names as defined by Al-Sawi, focusing on their implications for Allah's transcendence and perfection.

Core Idea
God's names reveal His perfection and transcend beyond literal physical meanings.

Al-Sawi defines five beautiful names of Allah, each clarifying a specific attribute. These definitions guard against common misunderstandings, such as thinking 'al-Ali' implies physical height. Through these concise explanations, we learn that Allah's attributes are real and perfect, yet incomparable to creation.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Wadud
Read the definition of al-Wadud: 'the loving of His servants, the beloved to them.' Note that it includes both active love (He loves) and passive love (He is loved).
2
Understand al-Ali
Read the definition of al-Ali: 'in rank, not in place, because of its impossibility for Him.' Understand that Allah's highness is a matter of status and honor, not spatial elevation.
3
Understand al-Azim
Read the definition of al-Azim: 'described with greatness and majesty in truth without [metaphor].' This affirms that Allah's greatness is real and not figurative.
4
Understand al-Alim
Read the definition of al-Alim: 'described with perfect knowledge pertaining to the necessary, the possible, and the impossible.' Note that His knowledge encompasses all categories of existence and non-existence.
5
Understand al-Hakim
Read the definition of al-Hakim: 'described with wisdom, which is perfecting things in a proportionate manner.' Wisdom implies perfect design and suitability in creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله :(الودود) أي المحبُّ لعباده، المحبوب لهم.
وقوله : (العليُّ ) : أي : بالمنزلة لا بالمكان لاستحالته عليه .
وقوله :(العظيم) أي الموصوف بالعظمة والجلال على الحقيقة دون
وقوله : (العليم) : أي : الموصوف بالعلم التّام المتعلّق بالواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات.
وقوله : (الحكيم أي الموصوف بالحكمة؛ وهي الإتقان للأشياء على وجه التناسب.
His saying: (al-Wadud) meaning the loving of His servants, the beloved to them. And His saying: (al-Ali) meaning in rank, not in place, because of its impossibility for Him. And His saying: (al-Azim) meaning described with greatness and majesty in truth without [metaphor]. And His saying: (al-Alim) meaning described with perfect knowledge pertaining to the necessary, the possible, and the impossible. And His saying: (al-Hakim) meaning described with wisdom, which is perfecting things in a proportionate manner.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a set of definitions for five divine names from the commentary. Al-Sawi explains each name in a way that affirms transcendence. For 'al-Wadud', he defines it as both active and passive love: God loves His servants and is beloved by them. For 'al-Ali', he clarifies that 'highness' is in rank (sharaf) and not in physical space, as spatial location is impossible for God. For 'al-Azim', he says it means described with true greatness and majesty, not metaphorical. For 'al-Alim', he specifies it is perfect knowledge that encompasses all three modalities: necessary (wajibat), possible (ja'izat), and impossible (mustahilat). For 'al-Hakim', he defines wisdom as the perfect execution of things with proportion and suitability.

Misconception: Some think 'al-Ali' means Allah is physically high in space.
Correction: Al-Sawi clarifies it is highness in rank, not place, because spatial location is impossible for Allah.

Checkpoint

B) As highness in rank, not place
C) As metaphorical greatness
D) As being above the throne

Key Takeaway
Al-Sawi's definitions of divine names consistently guard against literal, physical interpretations, affirming Allah's transcendence and perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Ali is high in rank, not in place. When you feel distant from God, do you imagine Him as far away in space, or do you realize the distance is in your heart? How can you bridge that rank gap?
?
Al-Hakim perfects everything with proportion. Think of a situation in your life that feels unfair or chaotic. Can you trust that God's wisdom is perfectly proportioned even when you cannot see it? Will you surrender your confusion to Him today?

The Creation of Man: Evidence of Divine Power and Mercy
Al-Sawi expounds on the stages of human creation and links them to divine love.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the detailed stages of human creation, from drop to soul, demonstrate Allah's infinite power, wisdom, and merciful love, as explained through the hadith of the hidden treasure.

Core Idea
Human creation is a direct expression of divine love.

This passage from al-Sawi's commentary takes you on a journey through the miracle of human development — from a drop in the womb to a fully formed being with senses, intellect, and soul. More than just a description, it reveals that every stage is a manifestation of God's love, as confirmed by the Prophet's saying and the famous hadith qudsī: 'I was a hidden treasure...' The detailed glosses help us appreciate both the physical and spiritual dimensions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation, paying attention to the sequence of creation from the nutfah to the rūh. Notice the commentator's clarifications in parentheses.
2
Identify the stages and timings
Find where the commentator specifies the time intervals for each stage (e.g., nutfah to 'alaqah after 40 days, rūh after 4 months). Understand that these are not random but divinely ordained.
This shows God's precise planning.
3
Reflect on the symbolism of body parts
Note the symbolic interpretation of the fingers as spelling the name 'Allah' and the tongue as a translator of the heart. Consider how even physical details point to spiritual truths.
The neck is called 'the bearer of the throne of the head'.
4
Connect to divine love and mercy
Read the concluding part where the author links all creation to God's original love. Ponder the hadith of the hidden treasure and the Prophet's love for women, perfume, and prayer.
This is the central thesis: creation flows from love.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في قرار مكين، فخلق تلك النُّطفة علقة، ثمَّ خلق العلقة مضغة، ثمَّ مدَّها وصوَّرها في أحسن صورة، فجعل الرّأس في أحسن خلقة، وخلق العين والأذن والأنف، وصوَّر الوجه في أحسن صورة، وأودعها من الجمال والكمال ما لا يخفى، ثمَّ أودع البصر في العين، والسمع في الأذن، والشَّمَّ في الأنف، وخلق الفم وزيَّنه بالشَّفتين، وخلق اللسان وخلق فيه الذَّوق، وجعله جنداً من جنوده تعالى يُترجم عمَّا في الفؤاد من العلوم والمعارف، وجعل الرقبة حاملة لعرش الرأس في حسن بديع، وجعل فيها المنفذ الموصل للأكل والشرب إلى المعدة، وأودع البطن من الأمعاء والمصارين والقلب والكبد وغيرها مما لا يعلم حقيقته إلا هو تعالى وخلق الأيدي وخلق فيها الأكف والأصابع وجعل مفاصلها وأبدعها، والأرجل كذلك، وخلق العظام وكساها لحماً، ثمَّ نفخ فيك الرُّوح - وهي سر عظيم عجيب من أسراره تعالى – فتحركت في بطن أمك، وما زال بك رؤوفاً رحيماً. فدل هذا على المحبَّة الأصليَّة الصَّادرة منه تعالى أراد خلق الخلق، يشهد له حديث: كنت كنزاً مخفياً، فأحببت أن أعرف فخلقت الخلق، فالخلق ناشئون من المحبَّة أوَّلاً وآخراً، ولهذا السِّرِّ العظيم قال عليه الصَّلاة والسَّلام: حُبِّب إليَّ من دنياكم ثلاث: النِّساءُ والطيب، وجعلت قرَّة عيني في الصَّلاة. قوله: (في قرار مكين): أي: وهو الرحم. قوله: (فخلق تلك النطفة علقة): أي: بعد أربعين يوماً. وقوله: (ثم خلق العلقة مضغة): أي: كذلك. قوله: (وجعله): أي: اللسان. قوله: (لعرش الرأس من إضافة المشبه به للمشبه؛ أي: للرأس الشبيهة بالعرش في العلوّ والارتفاع ومحاسن البدن. قوله: (والمصارين) عطف تفسير على (الأمعاء). قوله: (وخلق فيها الأكفَّ والأصابع): أي: لقضاء الحوائج والاعتبار وتذكر اسم الله؛ فإنَّ الأصابع جلالة؛ الخنصر الألف، والبنصر والوسطى اللامان والسَّبَّابة مع الإبهام الهاء. قال بعض العارفين: لقد بسطت في بحر جسمك بسطةً أشارت إليها بالوفاء الأصابعُ. قوله: (ثمَّ نفخ فيك الرُّوح): أي: بعد مضي أربعة أشهر. قوله: (وهي سرّ عظيم) أي به قوام الجسد، سارية فيه كسريان الماء في العود الأخضر.
In a firm resting place, then He created that drop of fluid into a clot, then He created the clot into a chewed lump, then He extended it and formed it into the best form, and made the head in the best creation, and created the eye, the ear, and the nose, and formed the face into the best form, and placed in it of beauty and perfection what is not hidden. Then He placed sight in the eye, hearing in the ear, and smell in the nose, and created the mouth and adorned it with the lips, and created the tongue and created in it taste, and made it a soldier from His soldiers that translates what is in the heart of sciences and cognitions. And He made the neck the bearer of the throne of the head in wonderful beauty, and made in it the passage that connects eating and drinking to the stomach, and deposited in the belly of the intestines and bowels and the heart and the liver and other things whose reality none knows except Him. And He created the hands and created in them the palms and fingers and made their joints and originated them wonderfully, and the legs likewise. And He created the bones and clothed them with flesh. Then He blew into you the spirit – and it is a great, wondrous secret from His secrets – so you moved in your mother's belly, and He has not ceased to be kind and merciful to you. So this indicates the original love emanating from Him, may He be exalted, by which He willed to create creation. The hadith witnesses to it: 'I was a hidden treasure, and I loved to be known, so I created the creation.' So creation arises from love first and last. For this great secret, he, peace and blessings be upon him, said: 'Three things from your world have been made beloved to me: women and perfume, and the coolness of my eyes was placed in prayer.' His saying: (In a firm resting place): i.e., the womb. His saying: (Then He created that drop of fluid into a clot): i.e., after forty days. His saying: (Then He created the clot into a chewed lump): i.e., likewise. His saying: (And made it): i.e., the tongue. His saying: (The throne of the head) – adding the likened to the likeness – i.e., the head that resembles the throne in loftiness, elevation, and the beauties of the body. His saying: (And the bowels) – a clarifying conjunction to (intestines). His saying: (And created in them the palms and fingers): i.e., for fulfilling needs, reflection, and remembering the name of God; for the fingers are a majesty: the little finger is the alif, the ring finger and middle finger are the two lams, and the index finger with the thumb is the ha'. Some gnostics said: 'You have spread in the sea of your body an expanse to which the fingers point with faithfulness.' His saying: (Then He blew into you the spirit): i.e., after four months. His saying: (And it is a great secret) i.e., by it the body subsists, permeating it like the permeation of water in a green branch.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is an extended reflection on God's creative power and mercy as manifested in the creation of man. The author first presents the original text (likely from al-Kharidah or its commentary) describing the stages of embryonic development from a drop to a clot to a lump, then the formation of the head, senses, mouth, tongue, neck, internal organs, limbs, and finally the blowing of the spirit. This is followed by a gloss (qāla) from the commentator (al-Ṣāwī) explaining key phrases. The passage begins by noting that the embryo rests 'in a firm resting place' (qarār makīn), which the commentator identifies as the womb. Each stage is specified: the nutfah becomes a clot after forty days, then the clot becomes a mudghah after another forty days. The creation of the tongue is emphasized as a 'soldier from God's soldiers' that translates the sciences and cognitions of the heart. The head is likened to a throne ('arsh) in its loftiness. The fingers are given symbolic meaning related to the letters of the divine name (Allāh). The spirit (rūḥ) is blown after four months and is described as a 'great secret' that gives life to the body, like water in a fresh branch. Crucially, the author connects this entire process to divine love by citing the famous hadith qudsī: 'I was a hidden treasure, and I loved to be known, so I created the creation.' Thus, creation is not arbitrary but flows from God's primordial love. This love is also reflected in the Prophet's statement that women and perfume were made beloved to him, with prayer being the true delight of his eye. The commentary follows the order of the original text, explaining each phrase in sequence.

نطفة
علقة
مضغة
روح
سر عظيم
محبة أصلية
عرش الرأس

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The stages of creation are purely mechanical biological processes.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that each stage is a manifestation of divine love and mercy, not just a random biological sequence.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The creation of man, from the drop to the soul, is a profound sign of God's power, wisdom, and merciful love, as confirmed by the hadith of the hidden treasure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your entire existence, from a drop to a breathing soul, is a direct result of God's love. If you truly believed this, what would be different in the way you live today? What is one specific action you will take before sunset to respond to that love?
?
The passage describes how God created your tongue to translate the heart's knowledge. When was the last time you used your speech to express gratitude for the very ability to speak? What will you say today to thank the One who gave you that tongue?
?
God has been 'kind and merciful' to you from before you were born. Yet, how often do you feel unloved or forgotten? Will you let the truth of this passage—that you are created from love—silence the whispers of doubt? Commit now to repeat the hadith 'I was a hidden treasure...' whenever you feel unloved.

4 min
Divine Protection in the Womb and Birth
Al-Sawi describes fetal development and birth as divine blessings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God's kindness manifests in the fetal stage, birth, and the instinct to suckle.

Core Idea
God's care begins before we are born, in the narrowest places.

Al-Sawi invites us to contemplate the earliest moments of our existence. From the womb's protection to the mother's compassion, every detail displays divine wisdom. This lesson traces God's blessings from conception to the first suckle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on fetal protection
Read the Arabic and translation describing how God keeps you safe in the womb, delivering nourishment via the navel.
Notice the phrase 'في أضيق مكان' for the womb.
2
Reflect on the birth process
Consider the transition from womb to world, and the inspiration to suckle immediately.
3
Appreciate the mother's compassion
Note how God places compassion in the mother's heart so she cares for you even in mess.
This is a subtle blessing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حافظاً لك في أضيق مكان، يوصل لك غذاءك وأنت لا تعلم شيئاً، حتى إذا تمّ خلقك أنزلك من الرَّحِم من أضيق محلّ فلَطَف بك وبأمك، حتّى إذا برزت ألهمك بمجرَّد النُّزول إلى ثدي أمك وأجرى فيه اللَّبَنَ، وأنزل في قلبها الرَّأفة والرَّحمة، حتى إنَّها ترى بَوْلَكَ وغائطك من أحسن ما يكون، والمنة له تعالى في ذلك. ولمّا أن أوان الأكل خلق لك الأسنان والأضراس ورتَّبَها ترتيباً عجيباً مع ما فيها من كمال الزينة والجمال والكمال. لمّا قَرُب بلوغك وكانت هذه الأسنان ضعيفة أسقطها وأبدلها بأقوى منها، ثمَّ إذا أكلت فجر الله في فمك عيناً جارية – وهي الرّيق - لا ينقطع جريانها ما دمت تأكل لتبتل اللُّقمة بها ويسهل بلعها، لا تملكها النَّفْس ولا تجري على الدوام ولا تنقطع، فانظر إلى هذه الحكمة البديعة التي أنت في غاية الافتقار إليها، وليس في قدرتك إجراؤها ولا منعها بالضرورة.
قوله : (حافظاً لك أي ومن جملة ذلك أن جعل وجهك لظهر أمك وظهرك لبطنها ؛ لئلا تتأذى بالطعام والشراب، وجعل نفسك لمخرج أمك لتتنفس في فارغ.
قوله : (يوصل لك غذاءك) : أي : من سرَّتك ؛ لعدم قوَّتك على البلع والمضغ .
قوله : (ألهمك بمجرد النزول إلى ثدي أمك) : أي : وعلمك كيفية المص والارتضاع.
فإذا نزل الطعام والشراب في المعدة صرفه إلى ما يشاء، فبعضُه يتربى به اللحم، وبعضُه يتربَّى به العظم، وبعضه يتربى به الشحم، وبعضُه يتربّى به الدَّم مع كمال اللَّذَّة حال الأكل وبعده، ثمَّ ما فضل عن ذلك وكان فيه الإيذاء للبدن على تقدير إبقائه في البطن أخرجه من مخرجيك، وانظر إلى هذين المخرجين وبديع حكمتهما وإلى إقدارك على إمساكهما عند تهيؤ الفضلة للخروج .
وبالجملة فلم يزل سبحانه بك رؤوفاً رحيماً ودوداً كريماً في كلِّ لحظة وأنت غافل عن نفسك.
وانظر إلى خروج النَّفَس ودخوله الذي به قوام الرُّوح حالة اليقظة والنَّوم والصحة والمرض . ومن أكبر عبرة: العقلُ الذي به التَّمييز والتَّدبير وإدراك العلوم والمعارف وما يضرُّ وما ينفع وَإِن تَعُدُّوا نِعْمَتَ اللَّهِ لَا تُحْصُوهَا ) (١) ، فَتَبَارَكَ اللهُ أَحْسَنُ الْخَلِقِينَ)
Keeping you in the narrowest place, delivering your nourishment to you while you know nothing, until when your creation is completed, He brought you down from the womb from a narrow place, and He was kind to you and your mother, until when you emerged, He inspired you, merely upon descending to your mother's breast, and made milk flow in it, and placed in her heart compassion and mercy, so that she sees your urine and stool as the best of things, and the favor is due to Him, exalted, in that. And when the time for eating came, He created for you teeth and molars and arranged them in a wonderful arrangement, along with what they contain of complete adornment, beauty, and perfection. When your puberty approached, and these teeth were weak, He removed them and replaced them with stronger ones. Then, when you eat, God causes to flow in your mouth a flowing spring – which is saliva – whose flow does not cease as long as you eat, so that the morsel becomes moistened by it and its swallowing becomes easy; you do not control it (the soul) nor does it flow continuously nor does it cease. So consider this wonderful wisdom which you are in extreme need of, and you have no power to make it flow or prevent it by necessity. His saying: 'Keeping you' – that is, among that is that He made your face toward your mother's back and your back toward her belly, so that you would not be harmed by food and drink, and He made your breath toward your mother's exit so that you breathe in empty space. His saying: 'delivers your nourishment' – that is, from your navel, due to your lack of strength for swallowing and chewing. His saying: 'He inspired you merely upon descending to your mother's breast' – that is, He taught you the manner of sucking and nursing. So when food and drink descend into the stomach, He directs it as He wills: some of it nourishes the flesh, some of it nourishes the bone, some of it nourishes the fat, and some of it nourishes the blood, along with complete pleasure during and after eating. Then, what is surplus to that and which would cause harm to the body if it were retained in the belly, He expels it from your two exits. And consider these two exits and the wonderful wisdom of them, and your ability to hold them back when the waste is ready for exit. In short, He, exalted, has not ceased being kind, compassionate, affectionate, and generous to you at every moment, while you are heedless of yourself. And consider the exhalation and inhalation of breath, by which the spirit subsists, in wakefulness and sleep, health and illness. And among the greatest lessons is the intellect, by which there is discrimination, management, and acquisition of sciences and knowledge, and what harms and benefits. 'And if you count the blessings of Allah, you cannot enumerate them' (Surah Ibrahim, 34). 'So blessed is Allah, the best of creators' (Surah Al-Mu'minun, 14).
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is an extended reflection on God's kindness and wisdom in human creation and sustenance. The author begins by describing the protection and nourishment of the fetus in the womb, then the birth and natural inspiration of the infant to suckle, and the mother's compassion. He then details the creation and replacement of teeth, the provision of saliva for swallowing, and the digestion process that allocates nutrients to different parts of the body. The author highlights the wisdom of the two excretory openings and the ability to control them. He concludes by noting God's continuous mercy and the greatest sign: the intellect. The passage is deeply devotional, aiming to instill gratitude and awareness of divine beneficence.

Key Terms
اللطف
الرأفة
الرحمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is the fetus positioned in the womb to avoid harm?
A) Face to mother's belly
B) Face to mother's back
C) Upside down
D) On its side

Key Takeaway
God's care encompasses every detail of our earliest existence, from womb to first breath.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's care began before you were born, how will you remind yourself of His presence when you feel isolated or forgotten today?
?
The passage says God placed compassion in your mother's heart. Have you thanked Him for that gift, and are you extending that same compassion to others?
?
If even your waste was made dear to your mother, what does that tell you about God's ability to accept your most flawed moments? Will you trust His mercy with them?

Teeth, Saliva, and Digestion as Divine Favors
Al-Sawi details the blessings of teeth, saliva, and digestion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how even the details of eating, from teeth to digestion, are signs of God's wisdom and generosity.

Core Idea
From teeth replacement to flowing saliva, every aspect of eating is divinely orchestrated.

The passage turns to the marvels of nutrition: the creation and renewal of teeth, the continuous flow of saliva, and the intricate allocation of nutrients to different body parts. Each step reveals God's care and wisdom, inviting gratitude.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of teeth creation and replacement
Read how God creates milk teeth and later replaces them with stronger ones at puberty.
Note the phrase 'ترتيباً عجيباً'.
2
Consider the provision of saliva
Reflect on how God causes a 'flowing spring' in your mouth to aid swallowing, which you cannot control.
3
Examine the digestion process
Understand how God directs food to nourish different parts: flesh, bone, fat, and blood, and prevents waste from harming you.
Notice the wisdom in the two exits.

Continuous Mercy, Breath, and the Intellect
Al-Sawi summarizes God's continuous mercy, the wonder of breath, and the intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God's continuous mercy is manifested in the breath of life and the gift of intellect.

Core Idea
God's mercy never ceases, even in the most ordinary acts like breathing and thinking.

Al-Sawi concludes his reflection by reminding us of God's perpetual kindness at every moment. He points to the constant inhalation and exhalation that sustain life, and the intellect that enables knowledge and discernment. This lesson highlights these overarching blessings.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding summary of mercy
Read how God has never ceased being kind and compassionate to you at every moment.
Notice the phrase 'وأنت غافل عن نفسك'.
2
Contemplate the breath
Reflect on the regular inflow and outflow of breath that sustains the spirit in all states.
3
Understand the gift of intellect
Consider how the intellect allows discernment, management, and knowledge of what is beneficial and harmful.
This is called the greatest lesson.

In short, He, exalted, has not ceased being kind, compassionate, affectionate, and generous to you at every moment, while you are heedless of yourself. And consider the exhalation and inhalation of breath, by which the spirit subsists, in wakefulness and sleep, health and illness. And among the greatest lessons is the intellect, by which there is discrimination, management, and acquisition of sciences and knowledge, and what harms and benefits. 'And if you count the blessings of Allah, you cannot enumerate them' (Surah Ibrahim, 34). 'So blessed is Allah, the best of creators' (Surah Al-Mu'minun, 14).
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is an extended reflection on God's kindness and wisdom in human creation and sustenance. The author begins by describing the protection and nourishment of the fetus in the womb, then the birth and natural inspiration of the infant to suckle, and the mother's compassion. He then details the creation and replacement of teeth, the provision of saliva for swallowing, and the digestion process that allocates nutrients to different parts of the body. The author highlights the wisdom of the two excretory openings and the ability to control them. He concludes by noting God's continuous mercy and the greatest sign: the intellect. The passage is deeply devotional, aiming to instill gratitude and awareness of divine beneficence.

Key Takeaway
God's mercy is perpetual, manifesting even in the breath we breathe and the mind we think with.

The Fourfold Division of Divine Attributes
Al-Sawi enumerates the four categories of divine attributes and explains their logical division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four categories of divine attributes (nafsi, salbi, ma'ani, ma'nawiyya) and the logical division that underlies them.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are logically divided into four categories based on signification of existence or non-existence.

This passage from Al-Sawi presents a fundamental classification in kalam: the four types of divine attributes. Rather than being arbitrary, this classification is derived from a logical division that considers whether an attribute signifies existence or non-existence, and whether it adds a meaning to the essence. Understanding this classification is essential for correctly describing Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation to get an overview of the classification and the logical division.
2
Trace the logical division
Identify how Al-Sawi splits attributes: first by signification (non-existence vs. existence), then by existence (real meaning vs. not), then by directness (essence itself vs. derived). This yields the four categories.
Start from 'attribute' and follow the branches.
3
Examine the definition of nafsi attribute
Read the commentary from Taftazani: nafsi is a positive attribute described by a derived word (e.g., 'existent'), not the infinitive (e.g., 'existence'). Understand why this matters.
This guards against attributing existence as an independent entity to God.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this classification prevents errors in theology. The logical approach ensures systematic thinking about Allah's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وذي تسمّى صفة نفسيَّة: اعلم أنَّ الصِّفات من حيث هي منقسمة إلى أربعة أقسام لا زائد عليها نفسيَّة، وسلبية، ومعان، ومعنوية، ووجه ذلك : أنَّ الصِّفة إما أن يكون مدلولها عدماً أو لا
الأوَّل: السَّلبيَّة، والثَّاني : إمَّا موجودة أو لا، الأوّل: المعاني، والثَّاني: إما أن يدلَّ الوصف بها على نفس الذات دون معنى زائد عليها أو لا، الأوّل: النَّفسيَّة، والثَّاني : المعنوية.
قوله : (وهي صفة ثبوتيَّة . . . إلخ) : هذا التّعريف للشَّيخ سعد الدين التفتازاني(۱)، وقوله: (صفة) كالجنس، يدخل فيه سائر الصفات، وقوله: (ثبوتيَّة) نسبة للثُبوت؛ لكونها ثابتة في الذهن، فخرج بذلك الصفات
السَّليَّة.
قوله : (يدلُّ الوصف بها أي بالمشتقّ منها لا بها نفسها؛ لعدم صحة ذلك، فنقول : الله موجود، ولا يصح أن نقول: الله وجود.
(۱) شرح المقاصد (٤٩/٢).
His saying: (And this is called a nafsī attribute): Know that the attributes in themselves are divided into four categories, no more: nafsī, negative, meanings, and meaning-related. And the rationale for that: the attribute is either its signification is non-existence or not. The first: negative. The second: either existent or not; the first: meanings, and the second: either the description by it indicates the essence itself without an added meaning or not; the first: nafsī, and the second: meaning-related. His saying: (And it is a positive attribute ... etc.): This definition is by Shaykh Saʿd al-Dīn al-Taftāzānī. His saying: (attribute) is like a genus, including all other attributes. His saying: (positive) is relative to affirmation because it is established in the mind, thus excluding negative attributes. His saying: (the description by it) means by the derived word from it, not by itself, because that is invalid; so we say: God is existent, and it is not correct to say: God is existence. (1) Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid (2/49).
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is a classification of divine attributes into four categories along with a logical division and then an explanation of the definition of the nafsī attribute. The author first presents the four categories: nafsī (essential), salbī (negative), maʿān (meanings, i.e., attributes like knowledge and power that inhere in the essence), and maʿnawiyya (meaning-related, i.e., derived attributes like 'knowing'). Then he gives the logical rationale: attributes are either negative (signifying non-existence) or positive. If positive, they are either maʿān (attributes that are real entities) or not; if not, they are either nafsī (describing the essence directly without an added meaning) or maʿnawiyya (describing the essence with an added meaning). The commentary then explains that the definition of the nafsī attribute as 'positive attribute by which description is made' is from Taftāzānī, and clarifies that 'attribute' is a genus including all attributes, 'positive' excludes negative attributes, and 'description by it' means using the derived form (e.g., 'existent') not the infinitive (e.g., 'existence'), which guards against the error of attributing existence itself as an independent entity to God.

Key Terms
صفات
نفسية
سلبية
معان
معنوية
صفة ثبوتية
مشتق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The four categories are arbitrary divisions.
Correction: They are derived from a logical division based on the signification of existence or non-existence and whether the attribute adds a meaning to the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the logical division, what kind of attribute results when the description indicates the essence itself without an added meaning?
A) Salbī attribute
B) Maʿnawī attribute
C) Nafsī attribute
D) Maʿnā attribute

Key Takeaway
The four categories of attributes are derived from a logical division that distinguishes between negative and positive attributes, and within positive attributes, between those that are essential (nafsi) and those that are derived (ma'nawiyya).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about Allah's attributes, do you ever slip into thinking of them as separate parts added to His essence? How does knowing the distinction between saying 'God is existent' and 'God is existence' change how you relate to His oneness?
?
The passage shows that even negative attributes like 'not unjust' are real descriptions of Allah. How often do you reflect on what Allah is not (tanzih) compared to what He is (ithbat)? Which aspect needs more attention in your heart?
?
This logical division reveals the precision of Islamic theology. Are you content with a vague or emotional understanding of Allah, or does this precision call you to deepen your knowledge? What step will you take today to replace your guesses about Allah with certain knowledge?

Existence as an Attribute: The Conditional View
The commentator presents the conditional status of existence as an attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the status of existence as a self-subsistent attribute depends on one's position regarding the doctrine of states (ahwal).

Core Idea
Existence as an attribute depends on whether you affirm states; otherwise it is the essence itself.

The commentator presents a crucial point: whether existence is considered a self-subsistent attribute additional to the essence depends on the theological school one follows. Those who affirm states see existence as an attribute with a special status, while others see it as identical to the essence. This lesson explores this conditional nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the first part of the text, which establishes the two views.
2
Identify the two positions
Notice that the passage contrasts two groups: those who affirm states (ahwal) and those who do not. For the first group, existence is a self-subsistent attribute; for the second, it is just the essence.
3
Understand the reasoning
Think about why existence cannot be a real attribute for those who deny states. The commentator will explain further in the next part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجَعْلُ الوجود صفةً نفسيَّة إِنَّما يصح عند من يُثبت الأحوال، فيكون صفة زائدة على الذات، غير موجودة في نفسها، ولا معدومة، وأما عند من لم يُثبت الأحوال فليس بصفة أصلاً، وإنما هو عين ذات الموجود كما مرّ . قوله : (وإنَّما هو عين ذات الموجود) : أي : فليس أمراً ثابتاً في الخارج؛ كالقدرة والإرادة، فلا ينافي أنَّه أمر اعتباري يعتبره الشَّخص ذهناً فقط، وذلك كما إذا أخرجت ثوباً من صندوق مثلاً، فالثّوب يوصف بالظهور، وهو أمر اعتباري لا ثبوت له في الخارج بحيث يصح أن يرى ولا في نفسه، بل هو أمر يعتبره الشَّخص في نفسه فقط .
And making existence a self-subsistent attribute is only valid according to one who affirms states (ahwal); then it becomes an attribute additional to the essence, not existent in itself nor non-existent. But according to one who does not affirm states, it is not an attribute at all; rather, it is the very essence of the existent, as has passed. His saying: (rather, it is the very essence of the existent) means: it is not a thing established externally, like power and will; so it does not contradict that it is a mental consideration (i'tibari) considered by the person only mentally. This is like when you take out a garment from a chest, for example, then the garment is described as 'appearing'; this is a mental consideration with no external existence such that it can be seen, nor in itself; rather, it is a thing considered by the person in his mind only.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of existence as a self-subsistent attribute. The commentator states that considering existence as a self-subsistent attribute (sifa nafsiyya) is only valid for those who affirm the doctrine of states (ahwal). According to this view, existence is an attribute additional to the essence, but it is not existent in itself nor non-existent—it is a state. However, for those who do not affirm states (the majority of Ash'aris or others), existence is not an attribute at all; rather, it is identical to the essence of the existent. The commentator then explains the phrase 'rather, it is the very essence of the existent' by clarifying that existence is not a fixed external entity like power or will. Instead, it is a mental consideration (i'tibari) that a person considers only in the mind. He gives an analogy: when you take a garment out of a chest, the garment is described as 'appearing' – this appearing is a mental consideration with no external reality that can be seen. Similarly, existence is a concept the mind derives from the existent itself.

Key Terms
الأحوال (al-ahwal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think existence is always a separate attribute in all theological schools.
Correction: Existence as a separate attribute is only affirmed by those who accept the doctrine of states; others consider it identical to the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, for whom is existence a self-subsistent attribute?
A) Those who affirm states
B) Those who deny states
C) All theologians
D) Only the later Ash'aris

Key Takeaway
The status of existence as a self-subsistent attribute is conditional upon accepting the doctrine of states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah exists,' do you conceive of existence as something added to His essence or as His very reality? How does the conditional nature of this theological commitment challenge your current understanding?
?
The passage shows that a technical debate about states has real consequences for how we think about God. Have you ever considered that your belief about God's attributes might be shaped by an unexamined assumption? Would you be willing to explore where your own view falls?
?
If existence is not an attribute for those who reject states, then what exactly are you affirming when you say 'God exists'? Is it just a label, or does it carry a deeper reality that you must grapple with in your worship?

Existence as a Mental Consideration
The commentator explains existence as a mental consideration using an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence is considered a mental consideration (i'tibari) and how it differs from real attributes like power and will.

Core Idea
Existence is a mental consideration, not a real external attribute.

The commentator clarifies the meaning of the phrase 'rather, it is the very essence of the existent' by explaining that existence is not a fixed external entity like power or will. Instead, it is a mental consideration (i'tibari) that the mind derives from the existent itself. An analogy of a garment appearing from a chest illustrates this concept.
