Proof that Allah Does Not Need a Locus
Sanusi presents the rational proof that Allah is not in need of a locus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that Allah is not in need of a locus, based on the impossibility of His being an attribute.

Core Idea
Allah does not need a locus because that would make Him an attribute, which is impossible.

This lesson presents the rational argument that Allah cannot be in need of a locus (maḥall). The proof is based on the nature of attributes and essences: if Allah needed a locus, He would be an attribute, but an attribute cannot be the subject of other attributes. This leads to several absurdities, showing the necessity of Allah's self-subsistence.

Guided Path
1
Understand self-subsistence
Read the opening definition: qiyām bi al-nafs means negation of need for a locus or specifying principle.
This sets the conceptual ground for the proof.
2
Grasp the main proof
The key inference: if Allah needed a locus, He would be an attribute, not an essence. But it is impossible for Allah to be an attribute.
3
Examine the argument against being an attribute
The proof shows that if Allah were an attribute, then other attributes could not subsist in Him, since an attribute cannot accept another attribute. This leads to absurdities.
4
Consider the absurd consequences
The argument details impossible outcomes: attributes described by opposites, infinite regress, and preponderance without a preponderant.
These consequences are logically impossible.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends with an invitation to reflect, emphasizing the soundness of the proof.
Reflection helps internalize the rational necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القيام بالنفس
بمعنى : سلب الافتقار إلى المحل أو المخصص ؛ أي: الفاعل.
دليل عدم افتقاره تعالى إلى محل
أمَّا أنَّه تعالى لا يفتقر إلى محل يقوم به قيام الصفة بموصوفها، فلأنه لو افتقر إلى ذلك لكان صفة لا ذاتاً ، إذ الذَّات لا تقوم بالذات، لكنَّ كونه تعالى صفة محال، إذ لو كان صفة لاستحال قيام الصفات الثبوتيَّة ؛ كالعلم والقدرة والإرادة به تعالى، إذ الصفة لا تقبل صفة أخرى تقوم بها، وإلا لزم أن لا تخلو عنها ، أو عن مثلها، أو عن ضدّها، ويلزم مثل ذلك في الأخرى التي قامت بها، وهكذا، إذ القَبول أمر نفسى لابدَّ أن يتَّحد بين المتماثلين أو المتماثلات، وهو محال لما يلزم عليه :
- من اتّصاف الصفة بمثلها أو بضدّها أو بخلافها، فيكون العِلْمُ
عالماً وجاهلاً وقادراً، وكذا العكس، وهو باطل.
الصّفة
- ومن دخول ما لا نهاية له من الصفات الوجوديَّة، على لو اتصفت بأخرى للزم الترجيح بلا مرجّح، إذ جَعْلُ إحداهما موصوفةً والأخرى صفةً لها دون أن تكون صفة للذات التي قامت بها الموصوفة،
ودون أن تكون الموصوفة هي الصِّفة للأخرى تحكُّم ، فليتأمل .
Self-subsistence in the sense: negation of need for a locus or a specifying principle; i.e., the agent. The proof that He the Exalted is not in need of a locus. As for that He the Exalted does not need a locus in which He subsists as the subsisting of an attribute in its subject, because if He were in need of that, He would be an attribute not an essence, for an essence does not subsist in an essence. But that He the Exalted be an attribute is impossible, because if He were an attribute, then the subsistence of the positive attributes such as knowledge, power, and will in Him the Exalted would be impossible, since an attribute does not admit another attribute that subsists in it. Otherwise, it would necessitate that it is not devoid of it, or of its like, or of its opposite, and the same would follow for the other [attribute] that subsists in it, and so on. For receptivity is an essential matter, and there must be unity between the similar [attributes] or [between the similar]? And that is impossible due to what follows from it: - That the attribute is described with its like or its opposite or its contrary, so knowledge would be knowing and ignorant and powerful, and likewise the reverse, and that is false. - And from the entry of infinite existential attributes, and if it were described with another, then there would be preponderance without a preponderant, because making one of them described and the other an attribute for it without being an attribute for the essence in which the described subsists, and without the described being the attribute for the other, is arbitrary. So reflect.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage provides the rational proof that Allah is not in need of a locus (maḥall). It begins by stating the meaning of self-subsistence as the negation of need for a locus or a specifying principle. Then the proof: if Allah needed a locus, He would be an attribute, not an essence (dhāt). But it is impossible for Allah to be an attribute, because if He were, then positive attributes like knowledge could not subsist in Him—since an attribute cannot be the subject of another attribute. The argument elaborates on the consequences: if an attribute could accept another attribute, it would be described by opposites (e.g., knowledge would be both knowing and ignorant), leading to absurdity. Additionally, accepting another would require a preponderance without a preponderant, which is arbitrary. The author concludes by urging reflection on this reasoning.

Key Terms
محل
مخصص
صفة
ذات
قبول
ترجيح بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may mistakenly think that Allah could be an attribute (like a quality) of something else.
Correction: The proof shows that if Allah were an attribute, He could not be the subject of other attributes, leading to logical absurdities. Therefore, Allah cannot be an attribute.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for Allah to be an attribute according to the proof?
A) Because an attribute cannot be the subject of another attribute
B) Because attributes are created
C) Because an essence cannot be described
D) Because attributes are limited

Key Takeaway
Allah's self-subsistence is proven by the impossibility of Him being an attribute, which would lead to rational absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly internalized that Allah is self-subsistent and needs no locus, how would that change the way you seek help and support in your life? Are there areas where you depend on created things as if they were necessary for your existence? What would it look like to rely solely on the Self-Subsistent?
?
The proof shows that Allah cannot be an attribute because that would lead to contradictions. How does this affect your understanding of tawheed? Do you ever unconsciously attribute to Allah limitations that imply He is like created things? Examine your heart—where do you fall short in affirming His absolute uniqueness?
?
Sanusi urges 'so reflect'. What does this proof demand from you personally? Are you willing to engage your intellect to deepen your faith, or do you content yourself with blind acceptance? Will you commit to studying such proofs until they become part of your conviction?

The Fourfold Division of Entities
Sanusi enumerates four categories by need for locus and specifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourfold classification of entities based on their need for a locus (maḥall) and a specifying principle (mukhaṣṣiṣ), and how this classification distinguishes Allah's essence, His attributes, human attributes, and human essences.

Core Idea
The fourfold division clarifies the unique ontological status of Allah vs. creation.

This lesson presents a fundamental classification in Islamic theology that defines the relationship of entities to two key concepts: locus and specifying principle. By understanding which entities are independent and which are dependent, we gain clarity on the nature of Allah, His attributes, and created things.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the literal meaning.
2
Identify the four categories
The passage enumerates four categories. List them and note what each is independent of or in need of.
3
Understand 'locus' and 'specifying principle'
Understand that 'locus' (maḥall) is the substrate in which something inheres, and 'specifying principle' (mukhaṣṣiṣ) is the efficient cause that brings it into existence.
4
Reflect on theological significance
Reflect on how this classification shows that only Allah's essence is absolutely independent, while His attributes need His essence as a locus, and created things are dependent in various ways.
This connects to the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بمعنى سلب الافتقار إلى المحلّ أو المخصص) : واعلم : أنَّ القسمة رباعية : مستغن عن المحل والمخصص معاً، وهو ذات الله، ومستغن عن المخصّص فقط، وهو صفات الله تعالى، ومفتقر إليهما معاً، وهو صفاتنا، ومفتقر إلى المخصّص فقط، وهو ذواتنا .
His statement: 'in the sense of negation of need for a locus or a specifying principle': And know that the division is fourfold: that which is independent of both locus and specifying principle, and that is the Essence of Allah; and that which is independent of the specifying principle only, and that is the attributes of Allah the Exalted; and that which is in need of both, and that is our attributes; and that which is in need of the specifying principle only, and that is our essences.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents a fourfold classification of entities based on their need for a locus (maḥall) and a specifying principle (mukhaṣṣiṣ, which refers to the efficient cause or agent). The four categories are: (1) What is independent of both—this is Allah's essence alone. (2) What is independent of the specifying principle only—these are Allah's attributes, which subsist in His essence but are not caused by an external agent. (3) What is in need of both—these are human attributes, which require a locus (the human self) and a specifying principle (God's creation). (4) What is in need of the specifying principle only—these are human essences (souls/bodies), which are created by God but do not inhere in a further locus. This division helps clarify the unique ontological status of Allah and the difference between divine and created things.

Key Terms
القسمة الرباعية
مستغن
مفتقر
محل
مخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Allah's attributes are completely independent like His essence.
Correction: The classification shows that Allah's attributes are independent of a specifying principle but still need His essence as a locus.

Checkpoint
Which category includes the attributes of Allah?
A) Independent of both locus and specifying principle
B) Independent of the specifying principle only
C) Needy of both
D) Needy of the specifying principle only

Key Takeaway
The fourfold division shows that only Allah's essence is absolutely independent; everything else has some form of dependency, whether on a locus, a cause, or both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you act as if you are independent of Allah, forgetting that every aspect of your being—your essence and attributes—depends entirely on Him? Will you repent for that assumption today?
?
If only Allah's essence is absolutely independent, how should that reshape your reliance on your own strength and intelligence? Commit to one practical way you will turn to Allah alone this week.
?
Do you treat Allah's attributes as if they are separate entities like created things? This passage shows they need His essence but not an external cause—does your worship reflect that Allah's attributes are intrinsic to Him, not created? How can you deepen your tawheed in light of this?

Proof of God's Self-Subsistence Without a Locus
Al-Sawi presents a proof that God is a self-subsistent essence needing no locus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Sawi proves that God is a self-subsistent essence that does not require a locus, based on His essential attributes.

Core Idea
God's essential attributes prove He is not a dependent attribute but a self-sufficient being.

This lesson examines a concise yet powerful proof from Al-Sawi's commentary on Al-Kharidah. The argument begins with an established fact about God's attributes and draws a necessary conclusion about His nature as a self-subsistent essence. By following the logical steps, you will see how the existence of essential attributes in God entails His independence from any locus.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow. Then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms: صفات تبوتية, ذات, محل.
The passage is a single sentence, but it contains a complete proof.
2
Identify the premise
The proof starts with the premise that God has established essential attributes (الصفات التبوتية). This is taken as given from prior proofs. Accept this as your starting point.
3
Follow the first inference
Because God has these attributes, He cannot be an attribute belonging to another. An attribute is not self-subsistent but needs a locus. Since God has attributes, He must be a self (ذات) rather than an attribute.
This step rules out the possibility that God is merely an accident or property of something else.
4
Reach the conclusion
As a self (ذات), God does not depend on a locus (محل) for existence. Thus He is self-subsistent. This is the required point (المطلوب).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو تعالى قد ثبت أنه قامت الصفات التبوتيَّة فلا يكون صفة به لغيره، فوجب أن يكون ذاتاً فلا يفتقر إلى محلّ، وهو المطلوب.
And He, exalted, it is established that He has the established attributes [الصفات التبوتية], so He is not an attribute belonging to another. Thus He must be a self [ذات] and not need a locus, and that is the required.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a self-contained proof for the divine attribute of self-subsistence (القيام بالنفس). The argument begins from the premise that God has established essential attributes (الصفات التبوتية). Because these attributes are inherent to His essence, He cannot be an attribute subsisting in something else. If He were an attribute, He would need a locus in which to subsist. But since He is not an attribute of another, He must be a self-subsistent essence (ذات) that does not depend on a locus. The conclusion is that God is independent of any substrate—this is the required point (المطلوب).

Key Terms
صفات تبوتية
ذات
محل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the proof conclude about God based on His having essential attributes?
A) That He is an attribute of another
B) That He is a self who does not need a locus
C) That He is composed of parts
D) That He is originated

Key Takeaway
God's essential attributes demonstrate that He is a self-subsistent essence needing no locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is a self-subsistent essence needing no locus, what does that reveal about the locus of your trust? What created 'locus' (people, wealth, status) are you depending on as if it were essential to your existence? How will you shift your dependency to God alone starting today?
?
The proof shows that having essential attributes makes God not an attribute of another. What does this say about your own identity? Are you defined by your attributes (role, possessions) or by your essence as a servant of God? In what specific area do you act as if you are an attribute of the world rather than a self-subsistent soul accountable to God?
?
If God does not need a locus, but you are a contingent being that does, how does that affect your sense of humility? When do you pretend to be self-sufficient? Commit to one act this week that acknowledges your total dependence on God.

The Fourth Negative Attribute: Contrary to Originated Things
Al-Sawi defines the fourth negative attribute, مخالفة للحوادث, and explains its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning and implications of the divine attribute 'contrary to originated things' and why it must be explicitly stated in kalam.

Core Idea
God's absolute transcendence means He resembles nothing in creation.

In this lesson, we explore the fourth negative attribute: that Allah is contrary to all originated things. Al-Sawi defines this attribute by listing what God is not—no substance, no body, no accident—and explains why this negation cannot be left implicit. Prepare to see how careful theological language safeguards divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic source and its literal translation. Notice the list of negations.
2
Identify why the attribute is explicitly stated
Find the explanation of why this attribute is mentioned despite being entailed by positive attributes.
The text says implicational indication is not sufficient.
3
Understand key definitions
Learn the definitions of 'originated thing', 'substance', 'body', and 'accident' as used here.
4
Clarify the meaning of 'bigness'
Notice the distinction between sensory bigness and conceptual greatness, and which is negated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابع الصفات السَّلبيَّة (تَخَالُفٌ للغير ) أي : مخالفته تعالى لغيره من الحوادث . ومعناها : عدم الموافقة لشيء من الحوادث، فليس تعالى بجوهر ولا جسم ولا عرض ولا متحرّك ولا ساكن ولا يوصف تعالى بالكِبَر قوله : (تخالف للغير) عطفه على ما قبله من عطف اللازم على الملزوم، إذ يلزم من وجوب الوجود والقدم والبقاء والقيام بالنفس مخالفته لكل ما سواه تعالى، ولم يكتف بذكر اللازم لما سبق من خطر هذا الفنّ، فلا يكتفى فيه بدلالة الالتزام . :قوله من الحوادث): جمع حادث، وهو الموجود بعد عدم، وهو الجواهر والأعراض. قوله : ( ولا جسم : هو أخصُّ من الجوهر ، إذ الجسم خاص بالمركب والجوهر الصَّادق به وبالجوهر الفرد. قوله : ( بالكبر) : أي : الحسي، وأما الكبر المعنوي: بمعنى العظم؛
And the fourth of the negative attributes: (contrary to other) i.e., His, exalted, being contrary to other than Him from the originated things. And its meaning: non-conformity to any of the originated things, so the Exalted is not a substance, nor a body, nor an accident, nor moving, nor stationary, nor is the Exalted described with bigness. His statement: (contrary to other) — it is conjoined to what precedes it as the conjunction of the necessary to the necessitated, for from the necessity of existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, and self-subsistence necessarily follows His being contrary to everything other than Him, exalted, and He was not satisfied with mentioning the necessary because of what passed of the danger of this discipline, so one is not satisfied in it with implicational indication. His statement: 'from the originated things' — plural of ḥādith, which is that which exists after non-existence, and they are substances and accidents. His statement: 'and not a body' — it is more specific than substance, for body is specific to the composite, while substance applies to both body and indivisible atom. His statement: 'with bigness' — i.e., sensory bigness; as for conceptual bigness, meaning greatness,
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage provides the definition and explanation of the fourth negative divine attribute: مخالفة للحوادث (being contrary to originated things). The original line of the poem states 'تَخَالُفٌ للغير' as a nominal phrase. The hashiah explains that this attribute means God does not resemble any created thing in any way. It then lists specific negations: God is not a substance (جوهر), not a body (جسم), not an accident (عرض), not moving or stationary, and not described with physical bigness. The hashiah goes into detail: first, it notes that this attribute is entailed necessarily by the positive attributes of necessity of existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, and self-subsistence, but it is mentioned explicitly because in the science of kalām, implicational indication (دلالة الالتزام) is not sufficient—one must state the necessary consequence outright. Then it defines 'حادث' (originated thing) as what exists after non-existence, comprising substances and accidents. It clarifies the difference between body and substance: body is a composite, while substance includes both body and the indivisible atom (جوهر فرد). Finally, it distinguishes between sensory bigness (كبر حسي) and conceptual greatness (كبر معنوي), clarifying that the negation of 'bigness' refers only to physical magnitude, not to greatness in majesty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The negation of 'bigness' in the attribute means Allah is not great in majesty.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'bigness' here refers to sensory physical size, not conceptual greatness. Allah is indeed described with greatness (الكبر المعنوي) in a positive sense.

B) Because in kalam, implicational indication is not sufficient

D) Because it is a separate category

Key Takeaway
The fourth negative attribute affirms that Allah is absolutely unlike any created thing, and this is explicitly stated because kalam does not rely on implication alone.

The Negative Attribute of Oneness (Wahdaniyya)
Sanusi defines Oneness as negation of multiplicity in essence, attributes, and actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the attribute of Oneness (Wahdaniyya) is a negative attribute that negates all forms of multiplicity in God's essence, attributes, and actions.

Core Idea
Oneness is not a positive attribute—it negates any multiplicity in God.

Among the negative attributes of God, Oneness (Wahdaniyya) holds a central place. It is not a positive assertion of unity but a negation of multiplicity—in essence, attributes, and actions. This lesson unpacks its definition, its linguistic form, and why it is the most important attribute, naming the science of tawḥīd itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for its flow. Then read the literal translation. Notice how the definition is structured.
The passage begins by stating that Oneness is a negative attribute.
2
Identify the core definition
Find the phrase that defines Oneness as 'negation of multiplicity.' Notice it specifies three domains: essence, attributes, and actions.
3
Understand the linguistic breakdown
The author explains the word 'wahdāniyya' by analyzing its letters: yā for attribution, tā for unity, alif-nūn for intensity. This shows the word itself implies a strong negation.
Compare with 'ruqbānī' as an example of the same pattern.
4
Grasp the importance of this attribute
The author says this attribute is so important that the science of tawḥīd is named after it. Only some humans deny it (by committing shirk); the jinn do not associate partners with God.
Reflect on why negation of multiplicity is the foundation of tawḥīd.
5
Clarify the term 'ithnayniyya'
The passage explains that 'absence of twoness' means multiplicity in general, because twoness is the beginning of multiplicity. Also note that negation of composition refers to continuous quantity.
This prevents misunderstanding that Oneness only negates the number two.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الوحدانية وخامس الصفات السلبية (وَحْدانية) وهي : عبارة عن سلب الكثرة الذات والصفات والأفعال ؛ أي: عدم الإثنينية (في الذات) أي : ذاته تعالى اتّصالاً وانفصالاً. فوحدانية الذَّات تنفي عنه تعالى الكم المتصل والمنفصل، أي: تنفي العدد في الذَّات، متّصلاً كان أو منفصلاً؛ فتنفي التركيب في ذاته تعالى قوله : (واعلم أنَّ العالم . . . إلخ) : زيادة في الإيضاح . قوله : (وحدانيَّة) الياء للنسبة والتّاء للوحدة، والألف والنُّون للمبالغة؛ كرقباني، وهذه الصفة أهمُّ الصِّفات، ولذا سمى علم التوحيد بها، ولم يكفر بضدها إلا بعض الإنس، وأمَّا الجن برمتهم؛ فلا يعتقدون الشرك الله سبحانه، وإنَّما الكافر منهم بغير الشّرك . قوله : (أي : عدم الإثنينيَّة : مراد بها التَّعدُّد مطلقاً، واقتصر على الإثنينيَّة ؛ لأنَّها مبدأ التعدد. قوله : (فتنفي التركيب): راجع للمتصل.
On the Oneness. And the fifth of the negative attributes is (Oneness), and it is: an expression of the negation of multiplicity in the essence, attributes, and actions; that is, the absence of twoness (in the essence), that is, His exalted essence (is not) by connection or disconnection. So oneness of the essence negates from Him, exalted, continuous and discrete quantity, that is, it negates number in the essence, whether continuous or discrete; so it negates composition in His essence, exalted. His saying: '(And know that the world... etc.)' is an increase in clarification. His saying: '(Oneness)' the yā' is for attribution, the tā' for unity, and the alif and nūn for exaggeration; like 'ruqbānī'. And this attribute is the most important of attributes, so the science of tawḥīd is named after it, and no one denies its opposite except some humans, as for the jinn entirely, they do not believe in associating partners with God, and the disbeliever among them is due to other than shirk. His saying: '(That is, absence of twoness)' means multiplicity absolutely, and he limited it to twoness because it is the beginning of multiplicity. His saying: '(So it negates composition)' refers to continuous (quantity).
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the negative attribute of Oneness (wahdāniyya). It begins by stating that this is the fifth negative attribute, defined as 'negation of multiplicity in the essence, attributes, and actions'—specifically, the absence of duality (ithnayniyya) in the essence, meaning that God's essence is not subject to connection or disconnection. This negation of number applies to both continuous quantity (e.g., physical parts) and discrete quantity (e.g., numerical multiplicity). Consequently, it negates composition (tarkīb) in God's essence. The author then comments on the word 'wahdāniyya' itself: the yā' indicates attribution, the tā' denotes unity, and the alif-nūn suffix conveys intensity (like 'ruqbānī'). He emphasizes that this attribute is the most important, as the entire science of tawḥīd is named after it, and that denial of its opposite (i.e., shirk) only occurs among some humans, not jinn. Finally, he clarifies that 'ithnayniyya' is used as a starting point for multiplicity, and 'tarkīb' refers to continuous quantity.

Misconception: Some think Oneness is a positive statement that God is 'one' in a numerical sense.
Correction: Oneness is a negative attribute: it denies any multiplicity, not that God is counted as one. It negates number and composition altogether.

Key Takeaway
Wahdaniyya is the negative attribute that denies all multiplicity in God's essence, attributes, and actions, establishing the foundation of tawḥīd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Oneness negates composition in God. Do you sometimes imagine God in a limited, compartmentalized way? How can you purify your understanding of God to truly affirm that He is free from all parts and multiplicity?

The Meaning and Scope of Divine Oneness
Al-Sawi defines the attribute of Oneness and explains the negation of composition in the divine essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Wahdaniyya (Unity) is a negative attribute that negates multiplicity in essence, attributes, and acts, and specifically negates composition and any like essence.

Core Idea
God's oneness is not just numerical: it negates all multiplicity and composition.

This passage from al-Sawi defines the attribute of Oneness (Wahdaniyya) as the negation of multiplicity. While we often think of 'oneness' as simply 'one', Islamic theology gives it a deeper technical meaning: it denies any kind of multiplicity in God's essence, attributes, and actions. The passage focuses on the unity of essence, explaining that God is not composed of parts and that nothing else shares His essence. Let's explore how al-Sawi unpacks this crucial attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then follow the translation. Notice how al-Sawi defines Wahdaniyya as 'negation of multiplicity'.
2
Understand the three domains
Identify that Wahdaniyya negates multiplicity in essence, attributes, and acts. The passage then zooms in on unity of essence.
3
Grasp the two negations in essence
Unity of essence negates both 'composition' (tarkib) and 'having a like' (nazir). Al-Sawi explains why composition is impossible and why no essence can be similar to God's.
4
Appreciate the significance
Note that this attribute is so important that the whole science of tawhid is named after it. Also, disbelief in this unity (shirk) is rare even among jinn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الوحدانية وخامس الصفات السلبية (وَحْدانية) وهي : عبارة عن سلب الكثرة الذات والصفات والأفعال ؛ أي: عدم الإثنينية (في الذات) أي : ذاته تعالى اتّصالاً وانفصالاً. قوله : (وحدانيَّة) الياء للنسبة والتّاء للوحدة، والألف والنُّون للمبالغة؛ كرقباني، وهذه الصفة أهمُّ الصِّفات، ولذا سمى علم التوحيد بها، ولم يكفر بضدها إلا بعض الإنس، وأمَّا الجن برمتهم؛ فلا يعتقدون الشرك الله سبحانه، وإنَّما الكافر منهم بغير الشّرك. قوله : (أي : عدم الإثنينيَّة : مراد بها التَّعدُّد مطلقاً، واقتصر على الإثنينيَّة ؛ لأنَّها مبدأ التعدد. قوله : (فتنفي التركيب): راجع للمتصل. ووجود ذات أخرى تماثل الذات العليَّة؛ أي: أنَّه تعالى ليست ذاتُه مركَّبة من أجزاء متّصل بعضُها ببعض، وإلا لكان مماثلاً للحوادث من حيث التركيب، فيحتاج إلى من يُركبه، وهو محال. وليس له نظير في ذاته.
In the [matter of] Unity. And the fifth of the negative attributes is Unity, and it is: an expression for the negation of multiplicity in essence, attributes, and acts; i.e., absence of duality (in the essence), i.e., His essence, exalted be He, in terms of connection and separation. He said: ('Unity') the ya' is for relationality, the ta' for the oneness, and the alif and nun for exaggeration; like رقباني, and this attribute is the most important of the attributes, hence the science of tawhid is named by it, and no one disbelieves in its opposite except some humans, but the jinn in their entirety do not believe in shirk with Allah, exalted be He, indeed the disbeliever among them is due to other than shirk. He said: (i.e., absence of duality) is intended to refer to multiplicity absolutely, and he confined to duality because it is the beginning of multiplicity. He said: (it negates composition): it refers to the conjunct [quantum]. And the existence of another essence similar to the supreme essence; i.e., that His essence, exalted be He, is not composed of parts connected to each other, for then He would be similar to contingent things in terms of composition, and would need one to compose Him, and that is impossible. And He has no like in His essence.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage begins by defining the negative attribute of Unity (Wahdaniyya) as the negation of multiplicity in three domains: essence, attributes, and acts. The author then focuses on the first domain: unity of essence. He explains that it negates both conjunct and disjunct quantum (al-kamm al-muttasil wa al-munfasil) from the divine essence, which means that God's essence is not composed of parts (negation of composition) and that there is no other essence like His (negation of a similar). The commentary on the word 'Wahdaniyya' explains its morphological form and its significance: it is the most important attribute, so the science of tawhid is named after it. Disbelief in its opposite (shirk) is rare; even the jinn do not commit shirk. The passage further explains that the negation of composition (tarkib) is the rejection of conjunct quantum, and the negation of a similar (nazir) is the rejection of disjunct quantum. The impossibility of composition is argued: if God were composed, He would need a composer, which is impossible.

Correction: The passage shows that Wahdaniyya negates multiplicity in essence, attributes, and acts, specifically denying composition and any similar essence.

The passage says the jinn as a whole do not commit shirk. You are human—have you ever, even for a moment, attributed a partner to Allah? If not outwardly, inwardly, have you feared or loved anyone as you fear and love Allah? What would it take for your heart to be purely for Him alone?

Unity of Attributes
Al-Sawi negates multiplicity in God's attributes and affirms their uniqueness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the oneness of God extends to His attributes, negating any multiplicity or partners in His qualities.

Core Idea
God's attributes are single, indivisible, and unique to Him.

This lesson focuses on the first aspect of tawhid: the unity of God’s attributes. The author explains that just as God’s essence is one, each attribute—life, knowledge, power—is one and not shared by any other being. He introduces the concepts of conjunct and disjunct quantum to clarify the negation of multiplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on unity of attributes
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the central idea that God's attributes have no multiplicity.
Notice the terms 'conjunct quantum' and 'disjunct quantum'.
2
Understand the two types of quantum
Conjunct quantum refers to multiplicity within a single attribute; disjunct quantum to multiple instances of the same attribute. Both are negated for God.
For example, God has one knowledge, not many knowledges (conjunct), and no other being has divine knowledge (disjunct).
3
Identify the uniqueness of divine attributes
The passage states that no one besides God is described with the attributes of divinity. This means creatures may have attributes like life but not in the divine sense.
This reinforces the absolute transcendence of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو) أي : وعدم الإثنينية في (صفاته العليَّة) اتصالاً أو انفصالاً أيضاً، فوحدانية الصفات تنفي عنه تعالى الكمّ المتصل والمنفصل فيها ؛ أي : تنفي العدد في حقيقة كلِّ واحدة منها ، متّصلاً كان أو منفصلاً ؛ أي : أنَّه تعالى له حياة واحدة، وعِلْمٌ واحد، وهكذا لا أكثر. وليس ثَمَّ من يتّصف بصفات الألوهية سواه تعالى. Or, i.e., and the absence of duality in (His supreme attributes) in terms of connection or separation as well, so the unity of attributes negates from Him, exalted be He, the conjunct and disjunct quantum in them; i.e., it negates number in the reality of each one of them, whether connected or separated; i.e., that He, exalted be He, has one life, one knowledge, and so on, no more. And there is no one who is qualified with the attributes of divinity other than Him, exalted be He. (And) unity; i.e., absence of duality in (the act) means: that He, exalted be He, is qualified with unity of acts, so there is no one who has any act of the acts other than Him, exalted be He, since everything other than Him is impotent, having no effect in any of the things. The proof of His being qualified with unity: and the well-known in proving unity is one proof. And he said: (So there is no one who has an act ... etc.) : this is the disjunct quantum in acts; as for the conjunct quantum in them, it is established and not negated, because His acts are many according to His affairs in His creation, and this is according to the choice of al-Ash'ari that the attributes of acts are originated; but according to the statement of al-Maturidi that the attributes of acts are eternal and return to the attribute of origination, then both quanta are also negated. One; and it is ...
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage continues the explanation of Wahdaniyya by discussing the unity of attributes and the unity of acts. For attributes, it negates multiplicity in each attribute: God has one life, one knowledge, etc., and no one else shares these attributes. For acts, it negates that anyone else has effective agency; all actions are from God alone. The passage then mentions the well-known proof for unity (the dalil), but it is only briefly stated as 'one proof'. The commentary then discusses the quantum in acts: the disjunct quantum (multiple agents) is negated, but the conjunct quantum (multiple acts from one agent) is not negated, because God's acts are many. However, this is according to the Ash'ari view that the attributes of acts are originated (haditha). On the Maturidi view, those attributes are eternal and fall under the attribute of takwin (bringing into existence), so both quanta are negated. The text ends incomplete, but it likely continues the discussion.

Unity of Acts and the Quantum in Acts
Al-Sawi explains unity of acts, negates other agents, and discusses quantum in acts with Ash'ari-Maturidi divergence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the oneness of God's actions, the difference between conjunct and disjunct quantum in acts, and the Ash'ari-Maturidi debate on the attributes of acts.

Core Idea
No act is truly done by any but God; all agency belongs to Him alone.

After establishing the unity of attributes, the author turns to the unity of acts. He declares that no one other than God performs any act because all creatures are powerless. He then defines two types of quantum in acts and discusses how the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools differ on whether God's acts are many or one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on unity of acts
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the central claim: only God truly acts.
The phrase 'everything other than Him is impotent' is key.
2
Distinguish disjunct vs conjunct quantum in acts
Disjunct quantum (multiple agents) is negated; conjunct quantum (multiple acts from one agent) is discussed differently by the two schools.
Ash'ari affirms conjunct quantum; Maturidi denies it.
3
Understand the Ash'ari and Maturidi positions
The difference stems from whether attributes of acts are originated (Ash'ari) or eternal and reduced to takwin (Maturidi).
This shows how theological commitments shape understanding of divine unity.

Refutation of the Claim That Allah Punishing Sinners Is Unjust
Refutation of the Mu'tazili objection that Allah punishing sinners is injustice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni definition of injustice (zulm) and how it refutes the Mu'tazili objection that Allah's punishment of sinners would be unjust.

Core Idea
Injustice is only possible over another's property; Allah owns all, so His acts are never unjust.

The Mu'tazila argued that if Allah punishes sinners, He would be unjust. This lesson examines the Sunni response: a precise definition of injustice that shows why the objection fails. By defining zulm as 'disposing of another's property,' the argument shifts the ground entirely — since everything belongs to Allah, His punishment cannot be injustice.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the passage and note the Mu'tazili claim that Allah punishing sinners would be injustice. Recognize that this objection targets the Sunni position.
The objection is presented in the first part of the sentence.
2
Examine the definition of zulm
Focus on the definition given: 'injustice is disposing of the property of another.' This is the core of the refutation.
Note that the definition is precise and limited.
3
Apply the definition
Consider why this definition makes the objection invalid. Since Allah owns everything, He never disposes of another's property. Thus, His actions cannot be unjust.
The refutation works because the definition excludes Allah from the category of unjust actors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم : إنَّه يلزم على كلام أهل السُّنَّة : أنَّ تعذيب الله للعصاة ظلم فباطل؛ لأنَّ الظلم هو التّصرُّف في ملك الغير .
As for their statement that it follows from the words of the Ahl al-Sunna that Allah’s punishment of sinners is injustice, it is false, because injustice is disposing of the property of another.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Mu‘tazili objection. The objection claims that the Sunni position (that Allah punishes sinners despite creating their actions) entails that Allah is unjust. The refutation defines injustice as 'disposing of the property of another.' Since everything belongs to Allah, He is not disposing of another's property when He punishes; therefore, it is not injustice. The definition is key: injustice is only possible when one acts in another's dominion.

Correction: Injustice is not simply causing harm; it is disposing of another's property without right. Allah owns all, so His punishment is not injustice.

Key Takeaway

The Comprehensive Proof for Negative Attributes
Al-Sawi presents the comprehensive conditional proof and its commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditional argument that establishes the necessity of God's negative attributes and how it answers an implied question about why they are required.

Core Idea
If God lacked any negative attribute, temporality would follow — so He must possess them all.

Al-Sawi begins by laying out the overarching proof for the five negative attributes (eternity, everlastingness, being unlike temporals, self-subsistence, oneness). He presents a conditional statement: if God were not described with these, He would be temporal. The commentator then clarifies that this conditional is the answer to an unspoken question: 'Why must God be described with negative attributes?' This lesson unpacks that logical move and the structure of the comprehensive proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation together. Notice the conditional 'if...then' structure and the list of five attributes.
The passage begins with 'البرهان الإجمالي' and ends with 'وظاهر'.
2
Identify the implied question and answer
The commentator explains that the conditional is actually an answer to an assumed question. Try to reconstruct that question from the context.
The question is: 'Why must God be described with these negative attributes?'
3
Analyze the conditional's logical force
The conditional says: if any negative attribute is missing, temporality follows. This shows that negative attributes are necessary conditions for eternality.
The negation of any one attribute suffices to prove temporal origination.

The comprehensive proof for His being described with negative attributes. Then he (may Allah forgive him) pointed to the proof of the negative attributes in summary by his saying: 'If He were not' meaning: It is necessary that He be described with negative attributes because if He were not described with them, such that He was not eternal or everlasting, or was similar to temporal things, or not self-subsisting, or not one in what passed, [then] His temporality would follow — exalted is He above that. As for pre-eternity: al-Sanusi [mentioned it] in his books. He mentioned it. His saying: '(in summary)' meaning: As for in detail, the proof of each has preceded in the books of al-Sanusi. His saying: '(meaning: It is necessary that He be described... etc.)': He indicated by that that his saying: 'If He were not... etc.' is in reality a cause for something omitted occurring in the answer to an assumed question; he estimated it by his saying: 'It is necessary that He be described... etc.' His saying: 'described with them' i.e. with these five, such that the description is negated from Him, even with some of them. His saying: '(that He was not eternal)' i.e. only, and a fortiori if He was not described with all of them, for the negation of any one of them necessitates temporality — exalted is Allah above that. His saying: '(in what passed)': i.e. in the essence, attributes, and actions. It is apparent. As for everlastingness: because if He were not described with it, He would not be eternal; for whoever's pre-eternity is established, non-existence is impossible for him; otherwise he would be possible of non-existence, thus he would need a preponderating factor, and everything needing a preponderating factor is temporal. As for self-subsistence: because if He subsisted in another, He would be an accident, and the demonstration of the temporality of accidents has preceded; or He would be an eternal attribute subsisting in its subject, thus it would follow that He is not described with attributes of meanings, as has passed, and that is false. His saying: '(It is apparent)': i.e. because there is no intermediary between pre-eternity and temporality; if pre-eternity is negated from Him, then temporality is affirmed for Him. His saying: '(If He were not described with it)': i.e. with everlastingness, meaning the necessity of everlastingness. His saying: '(He would not be eternal)': i.e. due to the entailment between them, for whoever non-existence is possible for him, pre-eternity is impossible for him. His saying: '(Otherwise) he would be possible of non-existence': i.e. if non-existence is not impossible for him, he would be... etc., and a fortiori the necessity of non-existence, so he mentioned the possible confining to the conceivable possibility. His saying: '(because if He subsisted in another)': i.e. as a temporal attribute. His saying: '(and it) is false' i.e. His being an attribute, whether temporal or eternal, and this is for the specifier because of its clarity and its being known from the proof of pre-eternity and everlastingness. One of the two sides of self-subsistence, and he left the other which is not needing [a place?].
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage presents the comprehensive proof (al-burhan al-ijmali) for God's negative attributes (al-sifat al-salbiyya). The commentator Al-Sawi explains the reasoning step by step. The author begins with a conditional statement: if God were not described with these five negative attributes (eternity, everlastingness, being unlike temporal things, self-subsistence, and oneness), then temporality (huduth) would necessarily follow. This is the core argument. The commentator clarifies that this conditional is the answer to an implied question: 'Why must God be described with negative attributes?' The proof for each attribute is said to be detailed earlier in Sanusi's works. The passage then elaborates on two attributes: everlastingness (baqa') and self-subsistence (qiyam bi nafsih). For everlastingness, the logic is that if God were not everlasting, He would not be eternal, because whatever is eternal cannot cease to exist. If He could cease, He would be contingent and need a preponderating factor, which implies temporality. For self-subsistence, if God subsisted in another, He would be an accident (which is temporal) or an eternal attribute in a subject, which would prevent Him from having attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani) — a consequence deemed false. The commentary further explains that the negation of any one of these attributes suffices to prove temporality, and that there is no middle ground between pre-eternity and temporal origination.

البرهان الإجمالي
الصفات السلبية
القدم
الحدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) His temporality would follow.
C) He would be imperfect in essence.
D) He would need a cause.

Key Takeaway
The comprehensive proof shows that God must possess all five negative attributes because lacking any one would imply temporality.

Proof for Everlastingness (Baqa')
Al-Sawi presents the proof for God's everlastingness and explains its logical steps.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that God must be everlasting, and why everlastingness is a necessary consequence of pre-eternity.

Core Idea
Eternity implies everlastingness; if God could cease, He would be contingent and temporal.

After establishing the comprehensive proof, Al-Sawi elaborates on two specific negative attributes. This lesson focuses on the proof for everlastingness (baqa'). The argument is straightforward: if God were not everlasting, He would not be eternal, because pre-eternity entails impossibility of non-existence. The passage breaks down the reasoning, showing that any being that can cease to exist is contingent and therefore temporal.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof for everlastingness
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. The proof is a short conditional chain: if not everlasting → not eternal → because eternal cannot cease.
The passage starts with 'وأما البقاء' and includes the commentary on each part.
2
Identify the entailment relationship
The proof rests on the entailment between pre-eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqa'). Understand why one implies the other.
If something is eternal, its existence is necessary, so non-existence is impossible.
3
Analyze the contingency argument
The passage argues that if non-existence were possible, the being would need a preponderating factor, making it temporal. This is a classic kalam proof.
The 'preponderating factor' (murajjih) is a cause that tips the balance from non-existence to existence.

البرهان الإجمالي لاتصافه تعالى بالصفات السلبية ثم أشار غفر الله له إلى برهان الصفات السلبية إجمالاً بقوله : لو لم يكن أي : إنما وجب اتصافه بالصفات السلبية لأنه لو لم يكن متصفاً بها بأن كان غير قديم أو باق، أو كان مماثلاً للحوادث، أو غير قائم بنفسه، أو غير واحد فيما مر ، (الزم) : حدوثه) تعالى عن ذلك. أما القدم : السنوسي في كتبه . ذكره . قوله : (إجمالاً) أي: وأما تفصيلاً فقد تقدم دليل كل منها عند السنوسي في كتبه . قوله : (أي : إنما وجب اتصافه . . . إلخ ) : أشار بذلك إلى أن قوله : (لو لم يكن .... إلخ علة في الحقيقة لمحذوف واقع في جواب سؤال مقدر ؛ قدره بقوله : إنما وجب ... إلخ . قوله : متصفاً بها أي بهذه الخمسة بأن انتفى عنه الاتصاف ولو ببعضها . قوله : ( بأن كان غير قديم أي فقط ومن باب أولى إذا كان غير متصف بجميعها، فنفي أي واحد منها يلزم منه الحدوث، تعالى الله عنه . قوله : فيما مر) : أي : في الذات والصفات والأفعال. وظاهر، وأما البقاء : فلأنه لو لم يكن متصفاً به لم يكن قديماً؛ لأن من ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه وإلا لكان جائز العدم، فيحتاج إلى مرجح ، وكل محتاج إلى مرجح حادث. وأما القيام بالنفس : فلأنه لو قام بغيره لكان عرضاً، وقد تقدم بيان حدوث الأعراض، أو كان صفة قديمة قائمة بموصوفها، فيلزم ألا يتصف بصفات المعاني، لما مر، وهو باطل . قوله : ( فظاهر) : أي : لأنه لا واسطة بين القدم والحدوث، فإن انتفى عنه القدم؛ فقد ثبت له الحدوث. قوله : (لو لم يكن متصفاً :به : أي : بالبقاء، بمعنى : وجوب البقاء. قوله : (لم يكن قديماً :أي لوجود التلازم بينهما، إذ من جاز عليه العدم؛ يستحيل عليه القدم. قوله : (وإلا) لكان جائز العدم :أي: وإن لم يستحل العدم لكان . . . إلخ، ومن باب أولى وجوب العدم، فذكر الجائز اقتصار على الشق المتوهم . قوله : فلأنه لو قام بغيره) : أي : بأن كان صفة حادثة . قوله (وهو) باطل أي كونه صفة سواء كانت حادثة أو قديمة، وهذا هو للمخصص ؛ لوضوحه وعلمه من دليل القدم والبقاء. أحد شقي القيام بالنفس، وترك الآخر وهو عدم احتياجه
The comprehensive proof for His being described with negative attributes. Then he (may Allah forgive him) pointed to the proof of the negative attributes in summary by his saying: 'If He were not' meaning: It is necessary that He be described with negative attributes because if He were not described with them, such that He was not eternal or everlasting, or was similar to temporal things, or not self-subsisting, or not one in what passed, [then] His temporality would follow — exalted is He above that. As for pre-eternity: al-Sanusi [mentioned it] in his books. He mentioned it. His saying: '(in summary)' meaning: As for in detail, the proof of each has preceded in the books of al-Sanusi. His saying: '(meaning: It is necessary that He be described... etc.)': He indicated by that that his saying: 'If He were not... etc.' is in reality a cause for something omitted occurring in the answer to an assumed question; he estimated it by his saying: 'It is necessary that He be described... etc.' His saying: 'described with them' i.e. with these five, such that the description is negated from Him, even with some of them. His saying: '(that He was not eternal)' i.e. only, and a fortiori if He was not described with all of them, for the negation of any one of them necessitates temporality — exalted is Allah above that. His saying: '(in what passed)': i.e. in the essence, attributes, and actions. It is apparent. As for everlastingness: because if He were not described with it, He would not be eternal; for whoever's pre-eternity is established, non-existence is impossible for him; otherwise he would be possible of non-existence, thus he would need a preponderating factor, and everything needing a preponderating factor is temporal. As for self-subsistence: because if He subsisted in another, He would be an accident, and the demonstration of the temporality of accidents has preceded; or He would be an eternal attribute subsisting in its subject, thus it would follow that He is not described with attributes of meanings, as has passed, and that is false. His saying: '(It is apparent)': i.e. because there is no intermediary between pre-eternity and temporality; if pre-eternity is negated from Him, then temporality is affirmed for Him. His saying: '(If He were not described with it)': i.e. with everlastingness, meaning the necessity of everlastingness. His saying: '(He would not be eternal)': i.e. due to the entailment between them, for whoever non-existence is possible for him, pre-eternity is impossible for him. His saying: '(Otherwise) he would be possible of non-existence': i.e. if non-existence is not impossible for him, he would be... etc., and a fortiori the necessity of non-existence, so he mentioned the possible confining to the conceivable possibility. His saying: '(because if He subsisted in another)': i.e. as a temporal attribute. His saying: '(and it) is false' i.e. His being an attribute, whether temporal or eternal, and this is for the specifier because of its clarity and its being known from the proof of pre-eternity and everlastingness. One of the two sides of self-subsistence, and he left the other which is not needing [a place?].
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage presents the comprehensive proof (al-burhan al-ijmali) for God's negative attributes (al-sifat al-salbiyya). The commentator Al-Sawi explains the reasoning step by step. The author begins with a conditional statement: if God were not described with these five negative attributes (eternity, everlastingness, being unlike temporal things, self-subsistence, and oneness), then temporality (huduth) would necessarily follow. This is the core argument. The commentator clarifies that this conditional is the answer to an implied question: 'Why must God be described with negative attributes?' The proof for each attribute is said to be detailed earlier in Sanusi's works. The passage then elaborates on two attributes: everlastingness (baqa') and self-subsistence (qiyam bi nafsih). For everlastingness, the logic is that if God were not everlasting, He would not be eternal, because whatever is eternal cannot cease to exist. If He could cease, He would be contingent and need a preponderating factor, which implies temporality. For self-subsistence, if God subsisted in another, He would be an accident (which is temporal) or an eternal attribute in a subject, which would prevent Him from having attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani) — a consequence deemed false. The commentary further explains that the negation of any one of these attributes suffices to prove temporality, and that there is no middle ground between pre-eternity and temporal origination.

Key Terms
البقاء
القدم
جائز العدم
المرجح
الحدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what follows if God were not everlasting?
A) He would be unlike temporal things.
B) He would not be eternal.
C) He would be self-subsisting.
D) He would be one.

Key Takeaway
God's pre-eternity entails His everlastingness, because anything that can cease to exist is contingent and thus temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's everlastingness is as certain as His pre-eternity, why do you live as if His mercy might run out before your next sin?
?
You affirm that God cannot cease to exist, but do you truly believe that He will never abandon you? If not, what does that say about your understanding of His attributes?
?
The proof shows that God's existence is necessary from eternity to eternity. Does your worship reflect this unchanging nature, or do you treat Him as if He is affected by your changing circumstances?

Proof for Self-Subsistence (Qiyam bi nafsih)
Al-Sawi presents the proof for God's self-subsistence and explains the reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-part proof that God is self-subsistent, and why alternative possibilities lead to false consequences.

Core Idea
If God subsisted in another, He would be either an accident (temporal) or a dependent attribute — both impossible.

The last elaborated attribute in the passage is self-subsistence (qiyam bi nafsih). Al-Sawi presents a disjunctive proof: if God subsisted in another, two possibilities arise — either He would be an accident (which is temporal) or an eternal attribute subsisting in a subject (which would prevent Him from having attributes of meanings). Both are false, so God must be self-subsistent. This lesson unpacks the logic and the commentary on its terminology.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof for self-subsistence
Read the Arabic and translation. The proof lists two possibilities (being an accident or an attribute of a subject) and shows each leads to an impossibility.
The passage starts with 'وأما القيام بالنفس' and includes the commentary.
2
Identify the two cases
The proof presents two cases: (1) if He subsisted in another, He would be an accident (temporal); (2) if He were an eternal attribute in a subject, He could not be described with attributes of meanings.
Both conclusions are false, so the premise that He subsists in another is rejected.
3
Understand the conclusion
The absence of a middle ground between pre-eternity and temporality reinforces the proof: if God were not self-subsistent, He would be temporal.
The commentary on 'فظاهر' clarifies that there is no intermediary between qidam and huduth.

The Rational Proof of God's Subsistence
Proof of God's subsistence with explanatory commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for God's subsistence (al-baqa') and why it is a necessary attribute contingent on His eternity (al-qidam).

Core Idea
If God could cease to exist, He would not be eternal.

This lesson presents a classical kalam proof that God must possess the attribute of subsistence (al-baqa'). The argument is straightforward: because God is eternal, He cannot be subject to non-existence. The commentary clarifies the logical entailment between eternity and necessary permanence, showing that any possibility of ceasing to exist would contradict His eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how the proof is structured as a conditional argument.
The passage includes both the main text and the commentator's glosses in parentheses.
2
Identify the main conditional argument
The proof states: if God were not described by subsistence, He would not be eternal. Trace the logical steps that lead from this condition to the conclusion.
3
Understand key terms
Focus on the terms qadim (eternal), baqa' (subsistence), ja'iz al-'adam (possible of non-existence), murajjih (preponderating factor), and hadith (originated). These are essential for the logic.
Qadim here means 'eternal without beginning,' not merely 'ancient.'
4
Follow the commentator's glosses
The commentary explains each part of the proof. Pay attention to the explanation of 'fazahir' (no intermediate), the entailment between qidam and baqa', and the handling of the hypothetical alternative.
5
Reflect on the logical necessity
Ask yourself: Why is subsistence not a separate contingent attribute? How does the proof show that God's existence must be necessary in every aspect, including permanence?
This connects to the broader doctrine of divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فظاهر، وأما البقاء : فلأنَّه لو لم يكن متّصفاً به لم يكن قديماً؛ لأنَّ من ثبت قِدَمُه استحال عدمه وإلا لكان جائز العدم، فيحتاج إلى مرجح، وكلُّ محتاج إلى مرجّح حادث.
قوله : ( فظاهر) : أي : لأنَّه لا واسطة بين القدم والحدوث، فإن انتفى عنه القدم؛ فقد ثبت له الحدوث.
قوله : (لو لم يكن متّصفاً به ) : أي : بالبقاء، بمعنى : وجوب البقاء. قوله : (لم يكن قديماً ) :أي لوجود التّلازم بينهما، إذ من جاز عليه العدم؛ يستحيل عليه القدم.
قوله : (وإلَّا) لكان جائز العدم :أي: وإن لم يستحيل العدم لكان . . . إلخ، ومن باب أولى وجوب العدم، فذكر الجائز اقتصار على الشَّقِّ المتوهم .
It is apparent. And as for subsistence: because if He were not described by it, He would not be eternal; because one whose eternity is established, non-existence is impossible for him; otherwise he would be possible of non-existence, thus needing a preponderating factor, and everything needing a preponderating factor is originated. His statement: '(It is apparent)' means: because there is no intermediate between eternity and origination; if eternity is negated from Him, then origination is established for Him. His statement: '(If He were not described by it)' means: by subsistence, meaning the necessity of subsistence. His statement: '(He would not be eternal)' means: because of the entailment between them, for whoever non-existence is possible for him, eternity is impossible for him. His statement: '(And if not) he would be possible of non-existence' means: and if non-existence were not impossible for him, he would be ... etc., and by primary inference the necessity of non-existence, so the mention of the possible is limited to the presupposed alternative.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage presents the proof of God's subsistence (al-baqa') as one of His necessary attributes. The argument is structured as a conditional: if God were not qualified by subsistence, He would not be eternal (qadim). The reason is that one whose eternity is established cannot possibly cease to exist; otherwise, He would be contingent (ja'iz al-'adam), requiring a preponderating factor (murajjih), and anything requiring a preponderating factor is originated (hadith). The commentator then explains each part of the proof. The first gloss clarifies why the statement 'it is apparent' is made: because there is no intermediate between eternity and origination—denying one entails the other. The second gloss specifies that 'being described by it' refers to subsistence, defined here as the necessity of remaining in existence (wujub al-baqa'). The third gloss emphasizes the entailment: if non-existence is possible for something, it cannot be eternal. The final gloss addresses the conditional's alternative: if non-existence is not impossible, then it is at least possible; by primary inference, it would even be necessary, but the argument only mentions the possible case as the weaker presupposition. The overall logic is a consistent application of the principle that necessary existence entails necessary permanence.

Key Terms
البقاء
القدم
الحدوث
جائز العدم
المرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'subsistence' is a separate contingent attribute that could be added or removed from God, or that 'qadim' means simply old in time.
Correction: In kalam, qadim means 'eternal without beginning,' and subsistence (baqa') is a necessary concomitant of eternity: if a being is eternal, it must also be subsistent (permanent). Subsistence here is defined as the necessity of remaining (wujub al-baqa'), not a mere possibility.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, why would God not be eternal if He lacked subsistence?
A) Because subsistence is the same as eternity.
B) Because He would then be possible of non-existence, which requires a preponderating factor, and anything needing a preponderating factor is originated.
C) Because subsistence is a contingent attribute that cannot belong to the Eternal.
D) Because the concept of subsistence is contradictory to the concept of eternity.

Key Takeaway
God's subsistence is rationally necessary because His eternity entails the impossibility of non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's subsistence is necessary and He cannot cease to exist, how does that confront your fear of losing things you cling to in this world that are transient? Will you realign your heart to cling only to the Permanent today?
?
The proof shows that God's eternity entails impossibility of non-existence. Do you live as if God is the only truly permanent reality, or do you act as if His promises might fade? What one change would prove you truly believe He never ceases?
?
The argument uses logical necessity to assert God's subsistence. Does your submission to this truth demand that you reorder your priorities, trusting in God's permanence over the fleeting? What attachment are you unwilling to release despite knowing this truth?

Proof of God's Self-Subsistence (Al-Qiyam bi al-Nafs)
Proof of God's self-subsistence via elimination of alternatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument proving that Allah is self-subsistent (qā'im bi nafsihi) and why He cannot be an accident or an inherent attribute.

Core Idea
Allah's self-subsistence means He needs no substratum—neither as accident nor as eternal attribute.

Sanusi presents a concise disjunctive argument to establish that Allah is self-subsistent. He eliminates two possibilities: that Allah could be an accident (which is originated) or an eternal attribute subsisting in another (which would prevent Him from possessing attributes of meanings). The argument forces the conclusion that Allah is independent of any substrate. The accompanying commentary clarifies key terms and notes that the argument focuses on one aspect of self-subsistence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the original Arabic and translation. Notice the two disjuncts presented by Sanusi.
The passage is dense; read slowly to follow the logical structure.
2
Follow the disjunctive argument
Identify the two false possibilities and why each is impossible. The first possibility leads to being an accident (originated), the second to being an eternal attribute that cannot have attributes of meanings.
Both conclusions are already established as false in earlier lessons.
3
Understand the commentator's gloss
Note that the commentator clarifies that 'subsisting in another' means being an originated attribute. Also note that the proof only addresses one part of self-subsistence (not needing a substratum), leaving the other part (being a substance) unaddressed.
Avoid the misconception that self-subsistence means material substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القيام بالنفس : فلأنَّه لو قام بغيره لكان عرضاً، وقد تقدم بيان حدوث الأعراض، أو كان صفة قديمة قائمة بموصوفها، فيلزم ألا يتّصف بصفات المعاني، لما مرّ، وهو باطل.
قوله : فلأنَّه لو قام بغيره) : أي : بأن كان صفة حادثة .
قوله (وهو) باطل أي كونه صفة سواء كانت حادثة أو قديمة، وهذا هو للمخصّص ؛ لوضوحه وعلمه من دليل القدم والبقاء.
أحد شقّي القيام بالنَّفس، وترك الآخر وهو عدم احتياجه.
And as for self-subsistence: because if He subsisted in another, He would be an accident, and the explanation of the origination of accidents has preceded; or He would be an eternal attribute subsisting in its subject, which would entail that He is not described by attributes of meanings, as has passed, and that is false. His statement: 'because if He subsisted in another' means: that He is an originated attribute. His statement: 'and that' is false, meaning His being an attribute whether originated or eternal, and this is for the specific [argument] due to its clarity and its being known from the proof of eternity and subsistence. One of the two parts of self-subsistence, and the other is left out, which is His not needing [a substratum].
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage proves God's self-subsistence (al-qiyam bi al-nafs). The argument considers two possibilities if God were not self-subsistent: either He would subsist in another, making Him an accident ('arad), which is impossible because accidents are originated; or He would be an eternal attribute subsisting in its subject, which would prevent Him from being described by attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani), a conclusion already shown false earlier. Thus, self-subsistence is established. The commentator clarifies that the first possibility means 'originated attribute,' and the rejection of 'He is an attribute' applies whether the attribute is originated or eternal—this is the specific argument, clear from the proofs of eternity and subsistence. The commentator notes that the argument mentions only one part of self-subsistence (not needing a substratum) and leaves out the other part (being a substance). The overall reasoning eliminates alternatives to affirm God's independence from any substrate.

Key Terms
القيام بالنفس
العرض
صفة المعاني
القدم
البقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-subsistence implies God is a material substance or occupies space.
Correction: Self-subsistence (al-qiyam bi al-nafs) means God does not depend on any substrate for His existence; it is a necessary attribute of God, distinct from accidents (which require a substratum) and from inherent attributes (which subsist in a subject). It does not imply materiality.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion if God were an eternal attribute subsisting in its subject?
A) He would be an accident.
B) He would be originated.
C) He would not be described by attributes of meanings, which is false.
D) He would be dependent on a subject, which is permissible.

Key Takeaway
The self-subsistence of Allah is proven by eliminating the possibilities that He could be an accident or an eternal attribute, both of which lead to absurd consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah were not self-subsistent, He would depend on something else. In your daily life, what created things or people have you come to rely on as if they were self-sufficient? How can you shift that dependence back to Allah alone?
?
The proof shows that Allah cannot be an accident or an eternal attribute—He is utterly independent. Is there any area of your life where you live as though Allah's help is just one of many options, rather than the only necessary source? What would it look like to change that today?
?
Imagine you are in a moment of deep need. Do your actions and heart reflect belief that Allah is self-subsistent (needing nothing) while you are completely dependent on Him? Write down one specific situation where you will consciously affirm this truth and act accordingly.

The Divine Name al-Jalīl: Meaning and Context
Al-Sawi defines the divine name al-Jalīl and explains the rhetorical fā'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the divine name al-Jalīl and how it is derived from negative attributes, as well as the rhetorical function of the fā' in the text.

Core Idea
Divine names like al-Jalīl are systematically derived from negative attributes.

Al-Sawi now moves from discussing negative attributes to specific divine names. The first name he explains is al-Jalīl (The Majestic). This lesson examines its meaning and the rhetorical structure that introduces it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from the previous discussion on negative attributes to this new section.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand the definition of al-Jalīl
Focus on the definition: 'the magnificent in status, to whose majesty every great thing submits, and everything proud is considered insignificant relative to His greatness.' Reflect on how this relates to the concept of divine transcendence.
3
Analyze the fā' al-faṣīḥa
The gloss explains that the 'fā' in 'fa-huwa' is for rhetorical eloquence, answering an implied question. Consider how this grammatical feature makes the sentence more emphatic.
This shows the careful literary style of the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقال : متفرقات في بيان بعض الأسماء والتنزيهات. ثم فرع على ما ذكره من صفات السلوب بعض أسماء وتنزيهات (فهو سبحانه وتعالى (الجليل) أي : العظيم الشأن، الذي يخضع لجلاله كل عظيم، ويستحقر بالنسبة لعظمته كل فخيم والأظهر أن الجلال.... قوله : (فهو الجليل): الفاء للفصيحة واقعة في جواب سؤال مقدر.
He said: 'Miscellaneous matters in explanation of some names and transcendences.' Then he branched out from what he mentioned of the negative attributes to some names and transcendences: (He, glorified and exalted, is al-Jalīl) meaning: the magnificent in status, the one to whose majesty every great thing submits, and everything proud is considered insignificant relative to His greatness. And the most apparent is that al-Jalāl... He said: ('He is al-Jalīl'): the fā' is for rhetorical eloquence (al-faṣīḥa), occurring in answer to an implied question.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section titled 'Miscellaneous matters in explanation of some names and transcendences' (mutafarriqāt fī bayān baʿḍ al-asmāʾ wa al-tanzīhāt). It is a direct outgrowth of the preceding discussion on negative attributes: those attributes logically lead to specific divine names and transcendent conceptions. The first name discussed is al-Jalīl (The Majestic). The author defines it as 'the magnificent in status, the one to whose majesty every great thing submits, and everything proud is considered insignificant relative to His greatness.' The gloss explains that the initial 'fā' (فـ) in 'fa-huwa' (فهو) is the fā' of rhetorical eloquence (fāʾ al-faṣīḥa), which introduces a sentence that is the answer to an implied question (e.g., 'What is He?' or 'How is He described?'). The text cuts off with 'and the most apparent is that al-Jalāl...', indicating that the definition continues in the original work.

Key Terms
الجليل (al-Jalīl)
تنزيهات (tanzīhāt)
الفاء للفصيحة (fāʾ al-faṣīḥa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Someone might think divine names are arbitrarily chosen titles without systematic connection to other attributes.
Correction: This passage shows that the name al-Jalīl is derived from negative attributes (the attributes of transcendence), indicating a deliberate theological system where names follow from conceptual foundations.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Al-Jalīl (The Majestic) is a divine name derived from negative attributes, emphasizing that all greatness submits to Allah's majesty.
