al-Jalīl and al-Jamīl: Comprehensive Divine Names
Al-Sawi explains the comprehensive nature of al-Jalīl and al-Jamīl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the divine names al-Jalīl and al-Jamīl encompass both negative and positive attributes, and why they are considered comprehensive.

Core Idea
The names al-Jalīl and al-Jamīl simultaneously declare transcendence and affirm perfection.

Al-Sawi explains that some divine names, like al-Jalīl and al-Jamīl, serve dual purposes: they negate imperfections and affirm perfections. This lesson unpacks how these comprehensive names function and corrects a common misunderstanding that they refer to only one type of attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the flow: definition of al-Jamīl, the idea of completion through transcendence, and then a discussion of different views.
The passage is dense; take your time with each phrase.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage opens by stating that these names are 'for the negative and positive attributes, together, not for one of them only.' This is the central thesis.
3
Note the different opinions
The text mentions a view that each name refers to only one type (negative only or positive only). Contrast this with the correct view that they are comprehensive (jāmiʿa).
The wrong view is indicated by 'كما قيل بكل' – 'as it was said with each.'
4
Understand the manifestations
Learn how jalal (majesty) manifests as vengeance and wrath, while jamal (beauty) manifests as mercy, bounty, and pleasure. This distinguishes their outward expressions while both remain comprehensive.
5
Examine additional names
The passage ends with brief explanations of al-Aʿazz (the Mighty, without equal) and al-Aḥmā (the Inviolable, transcendent). See how they fit into the same framework of transcendence and perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان في بعض الأسماء والتنزيهات
للصِّفات السَّلبيَّة والكماليَّة ،معاً لا لأحدهما فقط، كما قيل يرجع بكلِّ.
(والجميل) أي : المتّصف بصفات الجمال والكمال من علم وحياة وقدرة وإرادة وغيرها، وإنما تتمُّ بالتنزيه عن كل عيب ونقص ممَّا لا يليق بالجناب الأعزّ الأحمى، ويندرج في ذلك اللطف والحلم والكرم والعفو وغير ذلك مما لا يحصى،
تقديره : إذا علمت ما ذكر من تلك الصفات؛ فهو تعالى الجليل. إلخ . قوله : ( يرجع للصفات السَّلبيَّة والكماليَّة معاً) : أي : فهو من الصفات الجامعة، فالجلال في حقه تعالى هو التَّنزه عن النَّقائص والاتصاف بالكمالات.
قوله: (كما قيل بكل) : أي : بأنَّه يرجع للصفات السلبية فقط،
والكمالية فقط .
قوله : ( وإنَّما تتمُّ :أي: صفات الجمال والكمال، فتحصل أنَّ الجمال والجلال من الصِّفات الجامعة للتّنزيه عن النقائص والاتصاف بالكمالات، لكنَّ مظهر الجلال؛ الانتقام والغضب، ومظهر الجمال:
الرَّحمة والفضل والرّضا .
قوله : (الأعزّ) أي : عديم المثيل.
وقوله : (الأحمى) : أي : المحمي المنزه عن كل ما لا يليق به .
قوله : ( وغير ذلك) : أي : من باقي أسمائه الحسنى وصفاته الحسنى؛
An explanation of some of the names and declarations of transcendence. For the negative and positive attributes, together, not for one of them only, as it was said it refers to each. (And al-Jamīl), i.e., the One described with the attributes of beauty and perfection: knowledge, life, power, will, and others. And they are only completed by transcendence above every defect and imperfection that does not befit the Most Sacred and Inviolable Majesty. And included in that are gentleness, forbearance, generosity, forgiveness, and countless other things. Its estimation: when you know what has been mentioned of those attributes, then He, exalted, is al-Jalīl, etc. His statement: (it refers to negative and positive attributes together), i.e., it is among the comprehensive attributes. For jalal (majesty) with respect to Him, exalted, is transcendence above imperfections and description with perfections. His statement: (as it was said with each), i.e., that it refers only to negative attributes, and only to positive attributes. His statement: (and they are only completed), i.e., the attributes of beauty and perfection. So it is established that al-Jamāl (beauty) and al-Jalāl (majesty) are among the comprehensive attributes of transcendence above imperfections and description with perfections. However, the manifestation of jalal is vengeance and wrath, and the manifestation of jamal is mercy, bounty, and pleasure. His statement: (al-Aʿazz), i.e., the one without equal. And His statement: (al-Aḥmā), i.e., the protected, transcendent above everything that does not befit Him. His statement: (and other than that), i.e., from the rest of His beautiful names and high attributes.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is an explanation (bayān) of some of the divine names and declarations of transcendence. It begins with a statement that these names pertain to both negative (sulūbiyya) and positive (kamāliyya) attributes together, not to one alone, as some have claimed that each name refers to only one type. The text then introduces the name al-Jamīl (the Beautiful) and defines it as the One described with the attributes of beauty and perfection: knowledge, life, power, will, and others. These attributes are only complete when combined with transcendence above every defect and imperfection that does not befit the Most Sacred and Inviolable Majesty. Included in this are gentleness, forbearance, generosity, forgiveness, and countless others. The commentary then explains that al-Jalīl (the Majestic) is from the same category of comprehensive attributes: His jalal is His transcendence above imperfections and His description with perfections. There is a view that it refers only to negative or only to positive attributes, but the correct view is that it encompasses both. The text then clarifies that the manifestation (mazhar) of jalal is vengeance and wrath, while the manifestation of jamal is mercy, bounty, and pleasure. Finally, it explains al-Aʿazz (the Mighty) as the One without equal, and al-Aḥmā (the Inviolable) as the One protected and transcendent above everything unfitting. The passage concludes by referencing the rest of His beautiful names and high attributes.

الجميل
صفات سلبية
صفات كمالية
صفات جامعة

الجمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that al-Jalīl and al-Jamīl refer only to negative attributes (transcendence) or only to positive attributes (perfection).
Correction: The text clarifies that they encompass both: they are comprehensive attributes of transcendence above imperfections and description with perfections.

Checkpoint

A) They refer only to negative attributes.
B) They refer only to positive attributes.
C) They encompass both negative and positive attributes.
D) They refer to neither.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jalīl and al-Jamīl are comprehensive divine names that combine both the negation of imperfections and the affirmation of perfections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This lesson shows that God's attributes are only complete when combined with tanzīh—transcendence. In what area of your life are you attributing a false perfection to yourself or others, forgetting that only God is truly transcendent? What step will you take this week to strip away that idol and redirect all glory to Him alone?
?

Al-Jalil and Al-Jamil: Comprehensive Divine Attributes
Al-Sawi explains the names Al-Jalil and Al-Jamil as encompassing negative and positive attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the divine names Al-Jalil and Al-Jamil each encompass both negative attributes (transcendence) and positive attributes (perfection), and that they are comprehensive in meaning.

Core Idea
Majesty and beauty are two sides of the same divine perfection: transcendence from deficiency and possession of all perfections.

In this passage, Al-Sawi clarifies two important divine names: Al-Jalil (the Majestic) and Al-Jamil (the Beautiful). He explains that each name is comprehensive, combining both the negation of any imperfection and the affirmation of all perfections. This corrects the misunderstanding that these names refer only to one category of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without analyzing. Get a sense of the flow and the key names discussed.
Notice that the passage begins by stating that some names combine both negative and positive attributes.
2
Identify the two names and their definitions
Find where Al-Sawi defines Al-Jamil and then discusses Al-Jalil. Note how each definition includes both transcendence and perfection.
For Al-Jamil, he explicitly mentions attributes of beauty and perfection completed by transcendence.
3
Understand the correct view on Al-Jalil
Al-Sawi states that Al-Jalil returns to both negative and perfectional attributes together, and he rejects the view that it returns to only one category.
Look for the phrase 'يرجع للصفات السَّلبيَّة والكماليَّة معاً'.
4
Note the manifestations of jalal and jamal
Al-Sawi distinguishes between the manifestations: jalal is expressed in vengeance and wrath, while jamal is expressed in mercy, favor, and satisfaction.
This differentiation shows how the same comprehensive attribute operates in different modes.
5
Reflect on the broader principle
The passage concludes that all other beautiful names and attributes ultimately return to the attributes of will (irada) or power (qudra).
Consider how this connects to the idea of comprehensive attributes.

بيان في بعض الأسماء والتنزيهات للصِّفات السَّلبيّة والكماليَّة ،معاً لا لأحدهما فقط، كما قيل يرجع بكلِّ. (والجميل) أي : المتّصف بصفات الجمال والكمال من علم وحياة وقدرة وإرادة وغيرها، وإنما تتمُّ بالتنزيه عن كل عيب ونقص ممَّا لا يليق بالجناب الأعزّ الأحمى، ويندرج في ذلك اللطف والحلم والكرم والعفو وغير ذلك مما لا يحصى، تقديره : إذا علمت ما ذكر من تلك الصفات؛ فهو تعالى الجليل. إلخ . قوله : ( يرجع للصفات السَّلبيَّة والكماليَّة معاً) : أي : فهو من الصفات الجامعة، فالجلال في حقه تعالى هو التَّنزه عن النَّقائص والاتصاف بالكمالات. قوله: (كما قيل بكل) : أي : بأنَّه يرجع للصفات السلبية فقط، والكمالية فقط . قوله : ( وإنَّما تتمُّ :أي: صفات الجمال والكمال، فتحصل أنَّ الجمال والجلال من الصِّفات الجامعة للتّنزيه عن النقائض والاتصاف بالكمالات، لكنَّ مظهر الجلال؛ الانتقام والغضب، ومظهر الجمال: الرَّحمة والفضل والرّضا . قوله : (الأعزّ) أي : عديم المثيل. وقوله : (الأحمى) : أي : المحمي المنزه عن كل ما لا يليق به . قوله : ( وغير ذلك) : أي : من باقي أسمائه الحسنى وصفاته الحسنى؛ إذ هي ترجع للإرادة أو مع القدرة.
An explanation concerning some names and transcendences for the negative and perfectional attributes, together, not for only one of them, as it was said it returns to each. (And the Beautiful) meaning: the one described with the attributes of beauty and perfection: knowledge, life, power, will, and others, and they are only completed by transcendence from every defect and deficiency that is unbefitting to the most mighty and most immune station, and that includes kindness, forbearance, generosity, pardon, and other countless things. Its estimation: if you know what was mentioned of those attributes, then He, exalted, is the Majestic, etc. His statement: '(It returns to the negative and perfectional attributes together)' meaning: it is among the comprehensive attributes, for majesty (jalal) in His right, exalted, is the transcendence from deficiencies and the description with perfections. His statement: '(as it was said to each)' meaning: that it returns only to the negative attributes, and only to the perfectional attributes. His statement: '(and they are only completed)' meaning: the attributes of beauty and perfection, so it results that beauty (jamal) and majesty (jalal) are from the comprehensive attributes for transcendence from defects and description with perfections, but the manifestation of majesty is vengeance and wrath, and the manifestation of beauty is mercy, favor, and satisfaction. His statement: '(the most mighty)' meaning: peerless. And His statement: '(the most immune)' meaning: the protected, transcendent from everything unbefitting to Him. His statement: '(and other than that)' meaning: from the rest of His beautiful names and good attributes; for they return to the will or with the power.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the names Al-Jalil and Al-Jamil. It begins by stating that these names combine both negative and positive attributes. The name Al-Jamil refers to the one described with attributes of beauty and perfection, which are only complete through transcendence from defects. The commentary then explains that Al-Jalil is a comprehensive attribute: jalal (majesty) is defined as both being free from imperfections and possessing perfections. Some say it returns only to one or the other, but Al-Sawi affirms the correct view that it includes both. He then clarifies that the manifestation of jalal is vengeance and wrath, while the manifestation of jamal is mercy, favor, and satisfaction. He also glosses 'al-a'azz' as peerless and 'al-ahma' as protected from all that is unbefitting. Finally, he notes that all other beautiful names and attributes return to the attribute of will (irada) or power (qudra).

Correction: Al-Sawi states explicitly that these names are comprehensive: they combine both transcendence from defects and possession of perfections.

Effects of Divine Majesty and Beauty on the Knowers
Al-Sawi describes the effects of jalal and jamal on the knowers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the divine attributes of majesty and beauty affect the souls of those who truly know Allah, producing complementary states of humility and passionate love.

Core Idea
Divine majesty humbles; divine beauty enraptures.

Al-Sawi describes the spiritual states of the knowers (al-ʿārifūn) when they witness the divine attributes. He explains that Allah's majesty (jalāl) inspires awe and humility, while His beauty (jamāl) ignites passionate love and joy. These are not contradictory but complementary responses to different divine manifestations.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic from 'ولجلاله' to 'وعنا بهم' once, then read the English translation. Let the words sink in before analysis.
Note that the passage uses vivid imagery: 'earth becomes constrained' and 'increase in joy.'
2
Identify the two attributes and effects
Find where jalal and jamal are mentioned and note the corresponding effects: khushuʿ (humility) from jalal, and tawalluh (passionate love) from jamal.
Khushuʿ is also described as khudhuʿ and tadhallul.
3
Reflect on the contrast
Notice that the effects are opposite: one involves contraction and humility, the other expansion and joy. Yet both stem from the same knowers witnessing different attributes.
This shows that the knowers' states depend on which divine attribute they witness.
4
Connect to spiritual states
Think about how these states can occur in real life: a person can feel both awe and love in their relationship with Allah, depending on the context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولجلاله ترى العارفین به تعالى من هيبته ،خاشعين، ولجماله تراهم من حبه مولهين . (والولي) أي : مالك الخلائق، ومتولّي أمورهم، لأنَّ سائر أسمائه وصفاته الواردة نتائج تلك الصفات. الذات. ثم قوله : (إذ هي ترجع للإرادة) : أي : صفة وهي صفة الفعل، فيقال في وقوله : (أو مع القدرة) : أي : تعلقها، اللطف : : هو إرادة الإحسان، أو هو نفس الإحسان، والحلم هو إرادة ترك الانتقام، أو هو ترك الانتقام، وهكذا. تعالى. قوله : (من هيبته خاشعين أي خاضعين متذلّلين من شهود هيبته قوله : (تراهم من حبه مولهين أي هائمين، فتحصل : أنَّ العارفين : : بربهم إذا تجلى عليهم بالجلال؛ خشعوا وخضعوا وضاقت عليهم الأرض بما رحبت ولو كانوا في أعزّ النَّعيم، وإذا تجلَّى عليهم بالجمال؛ تولهوا وتهيموا وازدادوا فرحاً وسروراً و لو كانوا في ضيق الحال وعنا بهم . قوله : ( ومتولّي أمورهم) : أي : متصرف فيها فلا يكلهم لغيره ، قال تعالى : اللهُ وَلِيُّ الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا) (۱) ، أَمِ اتَّخَذُوا مِن دُونِهِ أَوْلِيَةً فَاللَّهُ هُوَ الْوَلِيُّ ) (٢) . (۱) سورة البقرة : (٢٥٧) . (۲) سورة الشورى: (۹) .
And due to His majesty, you see the knowers of Him, exalted, from His awe, humble; and due to His beauty, you see them from His love, passionate. (And the Protecting Guardian) meaning: the Owner of the creatures, and the One who undertakes their affairs, because all His other names and attributes that come are results of those attributes of the Essence. Then His statement: '(for they return to the will)' meaning: an attribute and it is an attribute of action, it is said in... and His statement: '(or with the power)' meaning: its attachment. Kindness (al-lutf) is the will to do good, or it is the good itself; forbearance (al-hilm) is the will to refrain from vengeance, or it is refraining from vengeance, and so on. Exalted. His statement: '(from His awe, humble)' meaning: submissive, humbled from witnessing His awe. His statement: '(you see them from His love, passionate)' meaning: bewildered. So it results that the knowers of their Lord, when He manifests upon them with majesty, they become humble and submissive and the earth becomes constrained for them despite its breadth, even if they were in the most honored bliss; and when He manifests upon them with beauty, they become passionate and bewildered and increase in joy and happiness, even if they were in distress and hardship. His statement: '(and the One who undertakes their affairs)' meaning: the disposer of them, so He does not entrust them to others. He, exalted, said: 'Allah is the Protecting Guardian of those who believe' (2:257) and 'Or have they taken protectors besides Him? But Allah - He is the Protector' (42:9). (1) Surah Al-Baqarah: 257. (2) Surah Ash-Shura: 9.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary by discussing the effects of divine majesty (jalal) and beauty (jamal) on those who know Allah. It states that due to His jalal, the knowers are humble and submissive, and due to His jamal, they are passionately enamored. Then Al-Sawi defines the name Al-Wali as the Owner and Manager of all creatures, explaining that all other names and attributes are results of those inherent to the Essence. There follows a comment on the line 'for they return to the will or with the power,' clarifying that attributes like kindness (lutf) and forbearance (hilm) are either the will to do good or the act itself. Next, Al-Sawi elaborates on the effects: when Allah manifests jalal, the knowers become utterly humble and feel constrained; when He manifests jamal, they become joyful and delighted even in hardship. Finally, he comments on Al-Wali as the One who disposes all affairs, citing Quranic verses.

الهيبة

الجلال
الجمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine majesty and beauty produce contradictory or mutually exclusive states in the believer.
Correction: Al-Sawi shows they are complementary: humility from majesty and passionate love from beauty are both appropriate responses, each valid in its own context.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Sawi, what happens to the knowers when Allah manifests His jamal (beauty)?
A) They become fearful and humble
B) They become passionate and joyful
C) They become angry
D) They become indifferent

Key Takeaway
The knowers' humility from majesty and passionate love from beauty are both authentic responses to divine self-disclosure, appropriate to which attribute they witness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When in your daily life do you feel most aware of your own smallness before Allah? Could that be a moment of jalal calling you to humility? What would change if you paused to acknowledge that feeling as a divine invitation?
?
Recall a time when your heart felt unexpectedly light or expansive despite difficult circumstances. Did you recognize that as a taste of His jamal? How would your worship transform if you actively sought that awareness every day?
?
Which of these two responses—awe or love—do you find more natural? Which do you avoid? Ask yourself honestly: why might you be resistant to the one you neglect?

The Name Al-Wali: Owner and Manager of All Affairs
Al-Sawi defines the name Al-Wali and supports it with Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of Allah's name Al-Wali as the absolute owner and disposer of all affairs, supported by Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
Al-Wali: Allah is the sole owner and manager of all creation.

After describing the effects of jalal and jamal, Al-Sawi turns to the divine name Al-Wali. He explains that it means the Owner (Mālik) of all creatures and the One who undertakes (mutawallī) their affairs. This establishes total reliance on Allah, as He does not delegate control to any other.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of Al-Wali
Read the passage where Al-Sawi defines Al-Wali as 'the Owner of the creatures and the One who undertakes their affairs.'
Note that the name implies both ownership and active management.
2
Understand the relation to other attributes
Al-Sawi mentions that other names and attributes are 'results of those attributes of the Essence.' This means Al-Wali is a primary name from which others derive.
This part may be dense; focus on the core definition.
3
Examine the Quranic verses
Al-Sawi cites two Quranic verses: 2:257 and 42:9. Read them in context and see how they affirm that Allah is the ultimate Wali.
The verses emphasize that Allah is the protector of believers and that no one else is a true protector besides Him.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider what it means that Allah does not entrust us to others. It inspires trust and reliance on Him alone.

The Reduction of Action Attributes to Will and Power
Commentator reduces action attributes to will/power with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine attributes of action like lutf and hilm are not independent but are reducible to the essential attributes of will and power.

Core Idea
God's kindness and forbearance are expressions of His will and power, not separate attributes.

The commentator clarifies that attributes such as lutf (kindness) and hilm (forbearance) are not separate eternal attributes. Instead, they are functional manifestations that 'return' to either the attribute of will alone or will combined with power. This understanding preserves the oneness of God's essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the key terms: الإرادة (will), القدرة (power), اللطف (kindness), الحلم (forbearance).
The passage is concise; pay attention to the flow of the argument.
2
Understand the reduction principle
The commentator states that these attributes 'return' (ترجع) to either will alone or will with power. Examine the examples: lutf is either the will to do good or the good itself; hilm is either the will to refrain from revenge or the act of refraining.
This shows that these attributes are not self-standing but depend on will/power.
3
Appreciate the theological significance
By reducing action attributes to essential attributes, we avoid multiplicity in God's essence. This aligns with the principle of tawḥīd.
The passage ends with 'تعالى' (Exalted is He), affirming God's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذ هي ترجع للإرادة أو مع القدرة. قوله : (إذ هي ترجع للإرادة) : أي : صفة وهي صفة الفعل، فيقال في [؟] وقوله : (أو مع القدرة) : أي : تعلقها، اللطف : هو إرادة الإحسان، أو هو نفس الإحسان، والحلم هو إرادة ترك الانتقام، أو هو ترك الانتقام، وهكذا. تعالى.
For they return to will or with power. His statement: 'For they return to will' means: an attribute, and it is an attribute of action, so it is said regarding [؟] and his statement: 'or with power' means: its relation. Al-lutf is: will to do good, or it is the good itself, and al-hilm is will to refrain from revenge, or it is refraining from revenge, and so on. Exalted is He.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage examines the nature of certain divine attributes of action, specifically al-lutf (kindness) and al-hilm (forbearance). The commentator begins by stating that these attributes 'return' (tarjiʿ) either to the attribute of will (al-irādah) alone or to will accompanied by power (al-qudrah). He then explains the gloss: when the text says 'they return to will', it means that these are attributes of action (ṣifat al-fiʿl). The phrase 'or with power' indicates that the relation of the attribute involves both will and power. He gives examples: al-lutf is either the will to do good (irādat al-iḥsān) or the actual good deed itself (nafs al-iḥsān). Similarly, al-ḥilm is either the will to refrain from revenge or the actual refraining. This shows that these attributes are not separate eternal attributes but are reducible to the essential attributes of will and power, underscoring the simplicity of the divine essence.

Key Terms
الإرادة
القدرة
اللطف
الحلم
صفة الفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might mistakenly consider lutf and hilm as independent eternal attributes.
Correction: This passage shows they are functional manifestations of will and power, not separate.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how are al-lutf and al-hilm related to God's attributes?
A) They are separate eternal attributes
B) They return to either irādah alone or irādah with qudrah
C) They are identical to God's essence
D) They are created attributes

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes of action are not separate but are reducible to Allah's will and power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recall moments when Allah showed you kindness (lutf) or forbearance (hilm), do you perceive these as separate 'forces' or as direct expressions of His will and power? How should this understanding reshape your gratitude and reliance on Him alone?
?
If Allah's forbearance is simply His will not to punish you immediately, what does this imply about your procrastination in repentance? How will you respond today to this direct call to turn back to Him without delay?
?
This lesson reduces all divine action to Allah's will and power—nothing else. In your daily life, do you ever subconsciously attribute events to 'luck' or 'nature' instead of His will and power? What concrete step will you take to affirm tawhid in your mind when something happens today?

The States of the Knowers: Awe Under Majesty, Ecstasy Under Beauty
The commentator describes the humble and ecstatic states of the knowers of God under jalal and jamal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the knowers of God (al-ʿārifīn) experience contrasting spiritual states when witnessing divine majesty and beauty.

Core Idea
Divine majesty inspires awe and humility; divine beauty inspires love and ecstasy.

This passage from the commentary on al-Kharida describes the profound spiritual experiences of the true knowers of Allah. The author contrasts two states: one of humble submission upon witnessing divine majesty, and another of joyful bewilderment upon witnessing divine beauty. These states are not opposites but complementary manifestations of the same divine reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the author's words describe the two states.
Notice the parallel structure: 'wa li-jalālihī... wa li-jamālihī...'
2
Identify the two states
Find the specific terms for humility (khushūʿ, khāḍiʿīn) under majesty and bewilderment (tawalah, hāʾimīn) under beauty.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Read the commentator's summary (fa-taḥaṣṣala) and consider how the ʿārifīn react even when external circumstances are contrary.
The earth becomes narrow despite vastness; they increase in joy despite hardship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولجلاله ترى العارفين به تعالى من هيبته خاشعين، ولجماله تراهم من حبه مولهين. قوله : (من هيبته خاشعين) أي خاضعين متذلّلين من شهود هيبته. قوله : (تراهم من حبه مولهين) أي هائمين، فتحصل : أن العارفين بربهم إذا تجلى عليهم بالجلال؛ خشعوا وخضعوا وضاقت عليهم الأرض بما رحبت ولو كانوا في أعز النعيم، وإذا تجلى عليهم بالجمال؛ تولهوا وتهيموا وازدادوا فرحاً وسروراً ولو كانوا في ضيق الحال وعنا بهم.
And by His majesty you see the knowers of Him, exalted is He, from His awe, humble, and by His beauty you see them from His love, bewildered. His statement: 'from His awe humble' means submissive, humiliated from witnessing His awe. His statement: 'you see them from His love bewildered' meaning infatuated. So it is concluded: that the knowers of their Lord, when He manifests to them with majesty, they become submissive and humble, and the earth becomes narrow for them despite its vastness, even if they were in the most precious bliss; and when He manifests to them with beauty, they become infatuated and passionate, and increase in joy and delight, even if they were in straitened circumstances and burdened.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage describes the spiritual states of the true knowers of God (al-ʿārifīn) when they witness the divine manifestations of majesty (al-jalāl) and beauty (al-jamāl). The commentator first paraphrases the text: by God's majesty, the knowers are seen as humble and submissive from awe (haybah). By God's beauty, they are seen as bewildered (mūlahīn) from love. He then explains two glosses: the first gloss clarifies that 'humble' means submissive and lowly from witnessing divine awe. The second gloss explains 'bewildered' as infatuated (hāʾimīn). Then he summarizes: when God manifests His majesty, the knowers are filled with fear and contraction, such that even the vast earth becomes narrow for them, even if they were in the most comfortable circumstances. Conversely, when He manifests His beauty, they become passionately enamored and increase in joy and delight, even if they were in hardship and burden. Thus, the knowers experience opposite spiritual states—fear and love—depending on which divine attribute predominates in the self-disclosure.

Key Terms
الجلال
الجمال
الهيبة
الخشوع
التوله
العارفون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that divine majesty and beauty produce contradictory or incompatible states in the knowers.
Correction: The passage shows that both states are complementary and proceed from the same divine source; they are two sides of the same spiritual experience.

Checkpoint
What happens to the ʿārifīn when God manifests with beauty (jamāl)?
A) They become fearful and subdued
B) They become bewildered and increase in joy
C) They feel the earth narrow upon them
D) They cease to exist

Key Takeaway
The knowers of Allah experience humble awe under divine majesty and joyful bewilderment under divine beauty, both deepening their connection to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial, do you feel constriction and awe, or do you feel bewilderment and joy? Which attribute of God (majesty or beauty) are you seeing in that moment? What would need to change in your perception to see both?
?
The knowers in this lesson experience both constriction and expansion, even in opposite circumstances. Have you ever felt joy in hardship or narrowness in ease? If not, what might be blocking you from experiencing the full range of Divine self-disclosure?
?
If you truly believed that God's beauty can bring joy even in 'straitened circumstances and burden,' would you still fear hardship as you do? What is one difficulty you are facing right now where you can intentionally seek the beauty of God to transform your response?

The Divine Name al-Walī: Owner and Disposer of All Affairs
The commentator defines the name al-Walī and relates all divine names to attributes of the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the divine name al-Walī and its relation to the essential attributes of Allah.

Core Idea
Allah is the sole Owner and Manager of all creation; His names derive from His essence.

This lesson explores the divine name al-Walī (the Guardian, the Protector). The commentator defines it as emphasizing Allah's exclusive control and ownership over all creatures, explaining that all other divine names and attributes are results of the essential attributes. We will examine the linguistic and theological significance through the passage and Quranic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms: al-Walī, mālik, mutawallī.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the phrase 'نتائج تلك الصفات الذات'.
2
Understand the two aspects of al-Walī
The commentator gives two meanings: Owner (mālik) and Undertaker (mutawallī). Understand how they relate.
The Quranic verses reinforce the meaning.
3
Reflect on the connection to essential attributes
The passage states that all other names and attributes are results (natā'ij) of the essential attributes. Consider why this matters for tawḥīd.
This aligns with the Ashʿarī view that attributes are not separate from the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(والولي) أي : مالك الخلائق، ومتولّي أمورهم، لأنَّ سائر أسمائه وصفاته الواردة نتائج تلك الصفات. الذات. قوله : ( ومتولّي أمورهم) : أي : متصرف فيها فلا يكلهم لغيره ، قال تعالى : اللهُ وَلِيُّ الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا) (۱) ، أَمِ اتَّخَذُوا مِن دُونِهِ أَوْلِيَةً فَاللَّهُ هُوَ الْوَلِيُّ ) (٢) .
(And al-Walī) meaning: the Owner of creatures, and the One who undertakes their affairs, because the rest of His names and attributes that are transmitted are results of those attributes. The essence. His statement: 'and the One who undertakes their affairs' meaning: the disposer of them, so He does not entrust them to other than Him. He said, exalted: 'Allah is the Walī of those who believe' (1), 'Or have they taken protectors besides Him? But Allah—He is al-Walī' (2).
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage explains the divine name al-Walī. The commentator first gives the meaning: al-Walī is the Owner (mālik) of all creatures and the One who undertakes their affairs (mutawallī umūrihim). He then adds a reason: all other divine names and attributes that are reported in revelation are results (natāʾij) of those essential attributes (tilka al-ṣifāt) of the Essence (al-dhāt). In other words, the name al-Walī encompasses the idea that God alone has full control over creation and does not delegate to others. The commentator then elaborates on the gloss: 'the One who undertakes their affairs' means He is the disposer (mutaṣarrif) of them, not entrusting them to anyone else. He supports this with two Quranic verses: 'Allah is the Walī of those who believe' (2:257) and 'Or have they taken protectors besides Him? But Allah—He is al-Walī' (42:9). Thus, the name emphasizes God's exclusive guardianship and the fact that all other attributes are ultimately rooted in the essence.

Key Terms
الولي
المالك
المتولي
الصفات
الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Walī is merely a name meaning 'protector' without implying exclusive control over creation and without indicating that all attributes derive from the essence.
Correction: The passage shows that al-Walī encompasses both ownership and active management, and that all divine names are consequences of the essential attributes.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator state about the relationship of other divine names to the essence?
A) They are independent of the essence
B) They are results (natā'ij) of the essential attributes
C) They are created in time
D) They are metaphorical only

Key Takeaway
Al-Walī means Allah is the sole Owner and Disposer of all affairs, and all divine names are rooted in His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly acknowledge Allah as the sole disposer of every matter in your life, or do you still attribute outcomes to your own planning and efforts? What one area of your life do you need to hand over to His management today?
?
When you call upon Allah by a name like al-Walī, do you recognize that this name is not separate from His essence but flows from His very nature? How does this understanding change the way you trust Him?
?
The commentator says that all divine names are results of essential attributes. Are there any names of Allah that you treat as mere labels rather than as true windows into His reality? What would change if you took each name as a direct revelation of who He is?

Understanding Four Divine Names: al-Ṭāhir, al-Quddūs, al-Rabb, al-ʿAlī
Hashi defines four divine names with brief commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of four divine names mentioned in the hashiyah and how they describe Allah's transcendence and lordship.

Core Idea
Divine names are not labels but descriptions of Allah's perfect attributes.

In this passage, the author defines four names of Allah: al-Ṭāhir (the Pure), al-Quddūs (the Holy), al-Rabb (the Lord), and al-ʿAlī (the Exalted). These names capture key aspects of Allah's majesty—His transcendence above all imperfection and His role as the Nurturer of all creation. Understanding these names deepens our knowledge of Allah's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once and then the literal translation. Note the concise style of definition.
Pay attention to the words used for each definition.
2
Examine each name's meaning
The text gives a brief definition for each name. Focus on the words 'طاهر', 'قدوس', 'رب', 'علي' and their explanations.
3
Understand the commentary
The hashiyah clarifies phrases like 'عظيم التنزيه' and 'المبرأ عن كل عيب'. See how they amplify the core meanings.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these particular names are singled out and what they teach about Allah's nature.

Source Text

(والطَّاهر ) أي : المنزّه عن كلِّ ما لا يليق به، (القدوس) من القدس، الظهر؛ أي: العظيم التَّنزيه عن كل نقص ، (والرَّبُّ) أي : المالك ومربي الخلائق (العلي) أي: المرتفع القدر، المبرأُ عن كل عيب.
قوله : (أي: العظيم التَّنزيه) : من إضافة الصّفة للموصوف ؛ أي: التَّنزيه العظيم. أراده .
قوله : (ومربي الخلائق) أي منمِّيهم شيئاً فشيئاً إلى الحد الذي أراده .

(And al-Ṭāhir) meaning: the one purified from everything that does not befit Him, (al-Quddūs) from al-quds, al-ẓahr; meaning: the great sanctification from every deficiency, (and al-Rabb) meaning: the owner and nurturer of creatures, (al-ʿAlī) meaning: the exalted in rank, the one free from every flaw. His saying: (meaning: the great sanctification): a genitive construction of an adjective to the described; meaning: the great sanctification. He intended it. His saying: (and nurturer of creatures) meaning: bringing them gradually to the limit He intended. His saying: (the one free from every flaw): an explanation of what precedes.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a definition of four divine names: al-Ṭāhir, al-Quddūs, al-Rabb, and al-ʿAlī. It explains that al-Ṭāhir means God is purified from all that does not befit His majesty. Al-Quddūs derives from 'quds' meaning sanctity, and 'al-ẓahr' here likely refers to clarity or manifestness; the phrase 'the great sanctification' means He is greatly sanctified above any imperfection. Al-Rabb is the owner and the one who nurtures all creatures, bringing them from state to state until they reach their intended goal. Al-ʿAlī is the exalted in rank, free from any defect. The hashīyah then comments that 'the great sanctification' is a construct phrase where the adjective describes the attribute, and that 'free from every flaw' simply explains the attribute already mentioned.

Correction: Each name describes a real attribute of Allah's perfection and transcendence.

The Meaning of 'Istawa' and Divine Transcendence
Al-Sawi explains 'istawa' as 'istila' and narrates a story about Zamakhshari asking Ghazali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'istawa' in the Quran means domination (istila) and that Allah is transcendent beyond physical attributes.

Core Idea
God's 'istawa' is absolute control, not physical location.

This lesson delves into the Islamic understanding of the divine attribute of istiwa' (establishing on the throne). Drawing from linguistic evidence and scholarly commentary, al-Sawi clarifies that 'istawa' signifies domination and authority, not physical settlement. The lesson also explores a profound aphorism by Ibn Ata'illah and a key question from Zamakhshari to Ghazali, all reinforcing the principle that Allah is beyond 'how' and 'where'.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic evidence
The passage first states that 'istawa' means 'istila', one of its two meanings. Read the line of poetry that uses 'istawa' in this sense of domination without physical contact.
The poet uses 'istawa' to mean gaining control over Iraq without fighting.
2
Reflect on Ibn Ata'illah's aphorism
The aphorism shows that the throne, though great, is hidden in God's mercy. This illustrates that 'istawa' does not imply spatial relation but complete control and encompassment.
The throne becomes 'hidden' in mercy, meaning it is enveloped by it.
3
Learn from the Ghazali-Zamakhshari exchange
Ghazali's response uses an analogy from self-knowledge to argue that just as you cannot describe your soul with 'how' or 'where', it is unfitting for the Creator. This reinforces that God is beyond physical attributes.
This exchange underscores the principle of tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاستواء على الاستيلاء أي: لأنَّه أحد معنييه، ومنه قول الشاعر: قد استوى بشر على العراق من غير سيف ودم مهراق. وفي آخر حكم ابن عطاء الله السكندري: (يا من استوى برحمانيته على عرشه، فصار العرش غيباً في رحمانيَّته، كما صارت العوالم غيباً في عرشه)، فهو يشير إلى أنَّ معنى الآية: أنَّ العرش وإن كان أكبر المخلوقات وكلُّها مغيبة فيه هو صغير بالنسبة لرحمة الله، ومغيب فيها كما غيبت العوالم فيه. ويؤيده قوله تعالى: (وَرَحْمَتِي وَسِعَتْ كُلَّ شَيْءٍ). وسأل الزمخشري أبا حامد الغزالي عن هذه الآية، فأجابه بقوله: إذا استحال أن تعرف نفسك بكيفيّة أو أينيَّة فكيف يليق بعبوديتك أن تصف الربوبية بأين أو كيف، وهو مقدَّس عن الأين والكيف؟! ثم جعل يقول: من الرمل (الأبيات).
And 'al-istawa' means 'al-istila' (domination), because it is one of its two meanings, and from that is the poet's saying: 'Bishr has established (istawa) over Iraq without sword and shed blood.' And in the concluding aphorism of Ibn Ata'illah al-Sakandari: 'O He who established (istawa) by His mercy upon His throne, so the throne became hidden in His mercy, just as the worlds became hidden in His throne.' And it indicates that the meaning of the verse is that the throne, even though it is the greatest of created things and all are hidden in it, is small relative to the mercy of Allah, and hidden in it just as the worlds are hidden in the throne. And it is supported by His saying: 'And My mercy encompasses all things.' And al-Zamakhshari asked Abu Hamid al-Ghazali about this verse, and he answered him by saying: 'If it is impossible for you to know yourself with a how-ness or a where-ness, then how is it fitting for your servitude that you describe the Lordship with a where or a how, and He is sanctified from where and how?!' Then he began to say: from the meter rajaz (the verses).
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the attribute of istiwa' (establishing on the throne). Al-Sawi states that the meaning of 'istawa' here is 'istila' (domination, taking control), which is one of its two lexical meanings. He supports this by citing a line of poetry from al-Ba'ith al-Mujash'i: 'Bishr has established over Iraq without sword and shed blood.' This use of 'istawa' in the sense of 'domination' demonstrates that the word can have that meaning in Arabic. Then he quotes the final aphorism (ḥikma) of Ibn Ata'illah al-Sakandari, which says: 'O He who established (istawa) by His mercy upon His throne, so the throne became hidden in His mercy, just as the worlds became hidden in His throne.' Ibn Ata'illah suggests that the meaning of the verse is that the throne, though the largest creation, is small in relation to Allah's mercy, and is enveloped by it. Al-Sawi then cites the Qur'anic verse 'My mercy encompasses all things' (7:156) in support. Finally, he narrates a story: al-Zamakhshari (a Mu'tazili scholar) asked Abu Hamid al-Ghazali about this verse (presumably the istiwa' verse). Ghazali responded by making an analogy: just as you cannot describe your own soul with a 'how' or a 'where', it is even more inappropriate to describe the Lord with such terms, for He is sanctified from 'where' and 'how'. Then Ghazali began to recite poetry (the poem is not given in the text). This story reinforces the idea that istiwa' should not be interpreted as a physical settling, but rather in a manner befitting Allah's transcendence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'istawa' in the Quran means physical sitting or settling in a place.
Correction: The passage shows that 'istawa' can mean domination (istila), as used in Arabic poetry, and that Allah is beyond how and where.

Checkpoint

A) As physical settlement
B) As domination (istila)
C) As unknowable

Key Takeaway
The word 'istawa' in reference to Allah means domination (istila), not physical settlement, and Allah is transcendent beyond how and where.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you ever find yourself unconsciously imagining Allah in a specific direction or location? How does Ghazali's question to Zamakhshari challenge that tendency in your own worship?
?
Think of a time you felt distant from Allah because you couldn't 'feel' His presence. Does this lesson's emphasis on Allah being beyond 'where' and 'how' change the way you understand that distance? What would it mean for your relationship with Him if you truly stopped trying to locate Him?
?
If you cannot fully know your own soul—its origin, its essence, its location—how can you dare to confine the Creator of that soul to any description? What would you need to let go of in your mental image of Allah to worship Him as He truly is?

Defending al-Ghazali's Statement: Nothing More Excellent Than What Is
Al-Sawi defends al-Ghazali's statement with three refutations of the incapability objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Sawi defends al-Ghazali's statement against the objection that it implies divine incapability, using three distinct arguments that reinterpret the word 'possibility'.

Core Idea
The statement does not limit God's power; it affirms the perfection of creation based on God's eternal knowledge.

Al-Sawi presents a classic theological defense of a famous saying attributed to al-Ghazali. The objection is that it suggests God could not create a better world. Al-Sawi offers three answers, each reinterpreting the word 'possibility' in a different way. As you read, notice how each answer protects God's omnipotence while maintaining the perfect order of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Get a sense of the flow: context, statement, objection, three answers.
2
Identify the objection and the three answers
Find where al-Sawi states the objection (that the statement suggests incapability). Then locate the three answers, each beginning with 'منها'.
The answers are numbered in the commentary.
3
Examine the first answer: possibility of creatures
The first answer reinterprets 'possibility' as the capacity of creatures. Understand that this negates any limitation on God.
This answer focuses on the power of creation, not the Creator.
4
Understand the second and third answers
The second answer relates possibility to God's eternal knowledge, and the third to the logical impossibility of changing essential categories (temporal vs eternal). Compare these approaches.
The Quranic verse is explained as a rational possibility, not actual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) منزّه أيضاً عن (السَّفَة) وهو : وضع الشَّيء في غير محله، إذ هو المدبّر الحكيم الخبير العليم، ولذا قال بعض أهل العرفان لما شاهد من عجيب الإتقان : ليس في الإمكان أبدع ممَّا كان. قوله : (بعض أهل العرفان هو حجة الإسلام الغزالي) واستشكل قوله قديماً بأنَّه يوهم العجز، وهو عليه محال، تعالى الله عنه. وأجيب عنه بأجوبة؛ منها : أنَّ المراد بالإمكان إمكان الخلائق فالمعنى : ليس في إمكان الخلائق تغيير ما أراده الله وأبدعه، فالمنفي تعلق قدرة الخلق. ومنها : أنَّ المراد إمكان الله باعتبار تعلق علمه أزلاً بإيجاد هذا العالم على هذا النظام، وتعلق القدرة التنجيزي لا يكون إلا على طبق ما سبق به العلم، وإلَّا لانقلب العلم جهلاً، فليس من الممكن إيجاد عالم غير هذا الموجود، وأما قوله تعالى: (إِنَّا لَقَدِرُونَ عَلَى أَن تُبَدِّلَ خَيْرًا مِنْهُمْ) فباعتبار الجواز العقلي بقطع النظر عن تعلّق العلم . ومنها : أنَّ المراد ليس في الإمكان جعل الحادث قديماً؛ لعدم القدرة بذلك ؛ لأنَّ الشَّيء إمَّا قديم أو حادث، فالحادث يستحيل خروجه عن الحادثية والقدم يستحيل خروجه عن القدم.
And He is also transcendent above (foolishness), which is placing a thing in other than its proper place, since He is the Wise, the All-Knowing, the Aware. Hence some of the people of spiritual insight said, when observing the wonder of perfect order: ‘There is nothing more excellent in possibility than what has come to be.’ His saying: ‘Some of the people of spiritual insight’ is the Proof of Islam Al-Ghazali. And this saying was objected to in the past as suggesting incapability, which is impossible for Him – exalted is God far above that. It was answered with several answers: Among them: that what is meant by ‘possibility’ is the possibility of creatures, so the meaning is: ‘It is not within the capacity of creatures to change what God has willed and created,’ so what is negated is the power of creatures. Among them: that what is meant is God’s possibility with respect to the pre-eternal attachment of His knowledge to bringing this world into existence on this system, and the actual exercise of power only occurs in accordance with what knowledge had preceded, otherwise knowledge would turn into ignorance, so it is not possible to bring into existence a world other than this existing one. As for His statement: ‘Indeed We are able to replace them with better than them’, it is with respect to rational possibility, disregarding the attachment of knowledge. Among them: that what is meant is that it is not possible to make the temporal eternal, because there is no power for that; for a thing is either eternal or temporal, so it is impossible for the temporal to leave its temporariness and for the eternal to leave its eternity.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a theological defense of the statement attributed to Al-Ghazali: 'There is nothing more excellent in possibility than what has come to be.' The commentator first establishes the context by noting that God is free from foolishness (safah), i.e., placing things improperly, because He is the Wise and All-Knowing. This leads to the observation of perfect order in creation, prompting the statement. Then an objection is raised: the statement seems to imply that God could not have created a better world, which suggests incapability – impossible for God. Three answers are given: (1) The term 'possibility' refers to the capacity of creatures, so the meaning is that creatures cannot alter God's creation; it does not limit God. (2) 'Possibility' refers to what is possible for God given His eternal knowledge. Since God's knowledge necessarily attaches to the best order, His actual power only executes that order; otherwise knowledge would be ignorance. Therefore, no other world is possible given this knowledge. The Quranic verse about replacing them with better is interpreted as a rational possibility, not considering the actual attachment of knowledge. (3) The statement means it is impossible to make a temporal thing eternal, as that would be a contradiction. Thus the statement is defended without compromising divine power.

Key Terms
إمكان
قدرة
علم
حادث
قديم
سفَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The statement 'Nothing more excellent in possibility than what has come to be' limits God's power by suggesting He could not create a better world.
Correction: The statement is not about God's power but about the perfection of His creation; the three answers clarify that it refers to creaturely possibility, God's knowledge, or logical impossibilities, none of which compromise divine omnipotence.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection to al-Ghazali's statement 'ليس في الإمكان أبدع مما كان' (Nothing more excellent could be)?
A) It contradicts the Quranic verses about God's power.
B) It implies that God is incapable of creating a better world.
C) It denies the existence of multiple possible worlds.
D) It suggests that the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's statement is not a limitation on God's power but a reflection of the perfect fit between God's eternal knowledge and His creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every circumstance in your life, even the painful ones, is exactly as God's knowledge determined it? How does that affect your reaction to hardship and disappointment?
?
Are there things in your past that you wish were different? How does al-Ghazali's insight that it is not possible to have something other than what God knows challenge your discontent and push you toward acceptance?
?
When you face a situation that seems unjust or poorly arranged, do you trust that God's wisdom placed it exactly as it should be? Or do you secretly think you could have arranged it better? What would it take for you to surrender that illusion and rest in His perfect knowledge?

Definition and Classification of Ṣifāt al-Maʿānī
Al-Ṣāwī defines ṣifāt al-maʿānī and distinguishes them from negative and essential attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ṣāwī defines ṣifāt al-maʿānī as existential attributes and how they are distinguished from negative and essential attributes.

Core Idea
Ṣifāt al-maʿānī are the existential attributes that subsist in the essence, whether eternal or temporal.

In this passage, al-Ṣāwī builds on the earlier discussion of essential and negative attributes to introduce the third category: attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī). He defines them as existential attributes and provides a precise technical definition that excludes other types. The passage also clarifies how these attributes apply to both the eternal and temporal realms, highlighting a key distinction in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Context
Recognize the sequence: after essential and negative attributes, the student must now learn about ṣifāt al-maʿānī. This sets the stage for the definition.
The term 'maʿānī' here is a technical term in kalām, not the linguistic meaning.
2
Grasp the Twofold Classification
Notice the key dichotomy: attributes are either existential (wujūdiyyah) or non-existential. If existential, they are ṣifāt al-maʿānī; if non-existential and signify negation, they are negative (salbīyah).
Existential attributes have a real existence in themselves, not merely mental.
3
Analyze the Technical Definition
Al-Ṣāwī gives a formal definition: every attribute subsisting in a subject, additional to the essence, necessitating a judgment. Work through each part to see what it excludes.
This definition is important for distinguishing ṣifāt al-maʿānī from essential, negative, and attributive (maʿnawiyyah) attributes.

ثمَّ المعاني أي : ثمَّ بعد أن عرفت ما تقدَّم من النَّفسيَّة والسلبية، يجب عليك معرفة الصفات المسماة بالمعاني؛ لأنَّ كلَّ واحدة منها معنى قائم بذاته تعالى . ومرادهم بصفات المعاني الصِّفاتُ الوجودية؛ أي: التي لها وجود في نفسها، قديمة كانت أو حادثة؛ كعلمه وقدرته تعالى، وكعلمنا وقدرتنا، والبياض والسَّواد. والحاصل : أنَّ الصِّفات إن كانت وجوديَّة سُمِّيت صفات معانٍ، وإن لم تكن وجوديَّة، فإن كان مدلولها عدم أمر لا يليق سُمِّيت سلبية. قوله : (المسماة بالمعاني) : أي : في اصطلاح المتكلمين، وتسمى أيضاً بالصفات الذاتيَّة ؛ لأنَّها لا تنفكُ عن الذات والوجوديَّة؛ لأنَّها متحققة باعتبار نفسها وهي في اللغة: ما قابل الذات، فيشمل النَّفسيَّة والسَّلبيّة والمعنوية، وفي الاصطلاح كلُّ صفة قائمة بموصوف زائدة على الذات، موجبة له حكماً، فخرج بقولنا : (قائمة بموصوف السَّلبيَّة، وبقولنا : (زائدة على الذَّات) النَّفسيَّة ؛ لأنَّها عين الذات، وبقولنا : (موجبة له حكماً) المعنوية ؛ لأنَّها نفسها حكم، وعلى القول بأنَّها أمور اعتبارية، فقد خرجت بقولنا : (قائمة بموصوف)، وهذا التَّعريف للمعاني من حيث هي كانت لقديم أو حادث، وحينئذ فالفرق بين صفات القديم والحادث أنَّ صفات القديم قديمة، ولا تسمى أعراضاً، وصفات الحادث حادثة وتسمّى أعراضاً .
Then the attributes of meanings: that is, then after you have known what preceded of the essential and negative attributes, it is incumbent upon you to know the attributes called 'of meanings' (al-maʿānī); because each one of them is a meaning (maʿnā) subsisting in His essence, exalted. And what they mean by 'attributes of meanings' is the existential attributes (al-ṣifāt al-wujūdiyyah); that is, those that have existence in themselves, whether eternal or temporal; like His knowledge and power, exalted, and like our knowledge and power, and whiteness and blackness. The conclusion: that if attributes are existential, they are called attributes of meanings; and if they are not existential, then if their signification is the non-existence of something unbefitting, they are called negative (salbīyah). His statement: (called 'of meanings'): that is, in the terminology of the theologians (al-mutakallimīn), and they are also called the essential attributes (al-ṣifāt al-dhātiyyah); because they are inseparable from the essence, and existential because they are realized in themselves. And they are in language: what is opposed to the essence, so it includes the essential, negative, and attributive (maʿnawiyyah). And in terminology: every attribute subsisting in a described entity, additional to the essence, necessitating a judgment for it. So by our saying 'subsisting in a described entity' the negative attributes are excluded; by our saying 'additional to the essence' the essential attribute is excluded, because it is identical to the essence; by our saying 'necessitating a judgment for it' the attributive (maʿnawiyyah) is excluded, because it itself is a judgment. And according to the view that they are mental considerations (umūr iʿtibāriyyah), then they are excluded by our saying 'subsisting in a described entity'. And this definition of meanings applies whether they are for the eternal or temporal. Accordingly, the difference between the attributes of the eternal and the temporal is that the attributes of the eternal are eternal and are not called accidents (aʿrāḍ), and the attributes of the temporal are temporal and are called accidents.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the attributes called 'ṣifāt al-maʿānī' (attributes of meanings). The author begins by explaining that after knowing the essential (nafsiyyah) and negative (salbīyah) attributes, one must know these attributes because each is a meaning (maʿnā) subsisting in God's essence. He then states that 'attributes of meanings' are existential attributes—those that have existence in themselves, whether eternal (like God's knowledge and power) or temporal (like human knowledge and whiteness). The conclusion: attributes are either existential (called maʿānī) or non-existential; the latter, if signifying a negation of what is unbefitting, are called negative. Next, the author gives a technical definition: in theological terminology, they are called 'dhātiyyah' (essential) because inseparable from the essence, and existential. Linguistically, 'maʿānī' means what is opposed to the essence, thus including nafsiyyah, salbiyyah, and maʿnawiyyah. Technically, it is every attribute subsisting in a subject, additional to the essence, necessitating a judgment. This definition excludes: negative attributes (by 'subsisting'), essential attribute (nafsiyyah, by 'additional to the essence' since it is identical), and attributive (maʿnawiyyah, by 'necessitating a judgment' since it is itself a judgment). On the view that these are mental considerations, they are excluded by the requirement of subsisting. Finally, the definition applies to both eternal and temporal attributes, but eternal attributes are not called accidents, while temporal ones are.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'attributes of meanings' refer only to the seven divine attributes commonly listed (life, knowledge, will, power, hearing, seeing, speech).
Correction: Al-Ṣāwī clarifies that ṣifāt al-maʿānī are any existential attributes, including temporal ones like human knowledge and whiteness. The term is not restricted to the divine.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ṣāwī, what is the defining characteristic of ṣifāt al-maʿānī?
A) They are purely negative attributes.
B) They are existential attributes that subsist in the essence of the described entity.
C) They are identical to the essence itself.
D) They are only applicable to temporal beings.

Key Takeaway
Ṣifāt al-maʿānī are the existential attributes that subsist in a subject, additional to the essence, and necessitate a judgment; they include both eternal and temporal attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your knowledge and power are temporal attributes that will perish; only God's knowledge and power are eternal. How does this reality affect the way you value your own intellect and abilities? Do you rely more on your own temporal knowledge or on the eternal knowledge of Allah? Today, commit to turning to Allah's revelation before relying on your own understanding.
?
The passage defines attributes of meanings as existential and additional to the essence. Reflect: Your own existence and attributes are gifts from Allah, not inherent to you. Are you living with a sense of humility that your very 'self' is dependent on Allah, or do you act as if your attributes (intelligence, health) are your own possessions? Make a conscious intention to attribute every good thing you have to Allah alone.
?
The technical definition excludes negative attributes from subsistence; they are simply the absence of imperfections. How often do you define yourself by what you are not (e.g., 'I am not a liar') rather than by positive actions? What is one positive attribute you can actively cultivate today, rather than merely avoiding its opposite?
